Mutual Oaths of Initiation: Simultaneous Greeting and Its Legal Effect
Ruling on mutual oaths and greeting groups

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mutual oaths conditioned on initiating speech and apply the distinction between religious and judicial belief.

Core Idea
Simultaneous greeting does not constitute initiation; mutual oaths render the condition impossible.

Oaths often hinge on precise definitions of actions. When two people swear that initiating speech will trigger a consequence, what happens if they greet each other at the same moment? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi view on this subtle issue, along with the related case of greeting a group containing the person one swore not to speak to.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath Condition
Each person swears: 'If I initiate speech with you, my slave is free.' The condition is 'initiating speech' (ibtida'), not just speaking.
Initiation implies one party starting the conversation before the other.
2
Determine if Condition Is Fulfilled with Simultaneous Action
When both greet at exactly the same time, neither precedes the other. Therefore, neither has initiated speech, and the condition is not triggered.
The oath is dissolved because the condition can no longer occur after the simultaneous greeting.
3
Apply the Ruling to Mutual Oaths Between Spouses
If a husband and wife both swear similarly, and the husband speaks to her, he does not break his oath because he did not initiate—her oath makes her initiation inconceivable.
The same logic applies: both have the same condition, so neither can be the initiator.
4
Analyze the Case of Greeting a Group
If someone swore not to speak to a particular person and then greeted a group containing that person, he breaks his oath unless he specifically intended only the others.
The apparent act of greeting includes everyone; intention alone is not enough in court.
5
Distinguish Between Religious and Judicial Belief
If he claims he intended to exclude that person, he is believed religiously (dinan) but not judicially (qada'an) because the external action contradicts his claim. However, if he explicitly says 'except one,' he is believed judicially because the exclusion is apparent.
This distinction protects inner sincerity while maintaining legal predictability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا إذا قال لآخر إذا ابتدأتك بكلام فعبدي حر فالتقيا فسلم كل على الآخر معاً لا يحنث وانحلت يمينه لعدم أن يكلمه بعد ذلك ابتداء، ولو قال لها أن ابتدأتك بكلام وقالت له كذلك لا يحنث إذا كلمها لأنه لم يبتدئها ولا يحنث بعد ذلك لعدم تصور ابتدائها، ولو حلف لا يكلمه فسلم على قوم هو فيهم حنث إلا أن يقصده فيصدق ديانة لا قضاء، أما لو قال السلام عليكم إلا على واحد صدق قضاء عندنا
And on this, if he said to another 'If I initiate speech with you then my slave is free,' then they met and both greeted each other simultaneously, he does not break his oath and his oath is dissolved because after that there is no initiation. And if he said to her 'If I initiate speech with you' and she said likewise, he does not break his oath if he speaks to her because he did not initiate with her, and he does not break his oath thereafter because her initiation is inconceivable. And if he swore not to speak to him, and he greeted a group in which he was, he breaks his oath unless he intended him specifically, in which case he is believed religiously but not judicially. But if he said 'Peace be upon you except one,' he is believed judicially in our school.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage discusses oaths that condition on the initiation of speech. The first scenario involves two people each swearing: 'If I initiate speech with you then my slave is free.' If they meet and both greet each other at the same time, neither has initiated because initiation requires one to precede the other. Thus the oath condition does not occur, and the oath is dissolved (i.e., no longer in effect). The same applies if a husband and wife both swear that the one who initiates speech causes a divorce; if they speak to each other, the husband is not considered the initiator because the wife also swore, making concurrent initiation impossible. The second part deals with greeting a group containing the person sworn against: if he greets the group without intending that person specifically, he breaks the oath. But if he intended him, he is believed in his intention before Allah (dinan) but not in court (qada'an) because the apparent act is speech. However, if he explicitly excludes one person from the greeting, he is believed judicially because the exclusion is manifest.

Key Terms
ابتداء (ibtida'): Initiating speech; the act of starting a conversation.
ديناً (dinan): Religiously, before Allah; the person is believed in his inner intention but not in a legal judgment.
قضاء (qada'an): Judicially; a legal ruling based on apparent evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two people both swear: 'If I initiate speech with you, my slave is free.' They meet and greet each other at the exact same time. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Both break their oaths because they spoke.
B) Neither breaks the oath because initiation requires one to precede the other.
C) The first one to speak breaks his oath.
D) The oath is invalid because it is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Simultaneous greeting does not break an oath conditioned on initiating speech because initiation requires precedence.

Writing and Signalling in Oaths: Custom vs Literal Speech
Ruling on whether writing or signalling counts as speech for breaking an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between oral speech and writing in oaths, and apply the principle of custom (urf) to determine when an oath is broken.

Core Idea
Oaths are interpreted by custom (urf), not by literal definitions; writing is not speech unless custom says so.

This lesson explores a key difference between the Hanafi school and other schools regarding oaths. While Malik and Ahmad consider writing or sending a messenger as a form of speech that breaks an oath, the Hanafi school insists that custom (urf) determines what counts as speech. We will examine the Quranic proof of the opposing view, the Hanafi response, and numerous practical examples from the classical texts, including a famous case from Harun al-Rashid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position and its basis
State the general Hanafi rule: writing or sending a messenger does not break an oath of speech because custom does not consider them speech. Understand that this is rooted in the principle that oaths are built on 'urf (custom).
The Hanafi school relies heavily on 'urf in oaths, unlike other schools that lean on literal definitions.
2
Contrast with the opposing schools
Recognize that Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad consider writing as a form of speech. They cite Quran 42:51, where Allah sends a messenger as a mode of speaking.
Their methodology is literalist, while the Hanafis prioritize the common understanding of people.
3
Analyse the Quranic verse and the Hanafi response
Read the verse (Shura 42:51) and understand the opponents' argument. Then see the Hanafi response: the verse is about divine speech, not human oaths. Oaths are based on 'urf, not analogies with divine revelation.
The Hanafi school uses the same verse but limits its application.
4
Examine the detailed applications
Work through the various oath formulas mentioned in the text: 'la yuhaddithuhu', 'la ukallimuhu', 'la ubashshiruhu', conditional oaths like 'in akhbartani', and the distinction between 'an' and 'bi' constructions. Note how custom dictates the outcome in each case.
For example, 'bishara' (glad tidings) is customarily done by writing, so writing breaks that oath.
5
Apply the custom principle to the ruler's case and intra-Hanafi difference
Consider the case of Harun al-Rashid: a ruler's custom is to command by gesture, so gesturing breaks an oath not to write. Also note the ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on reading a letter: Muhammad considers comprehension sufficient (custom of readers), while Abu Yusuf requires pronunciation.
This shows that even within the Hanafi school, custom may be interpreted differently depending on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كتب إليه كتاباً أو أرسل إليه رسولاً لا يحنث لأنه لا كلاماً عرفاً يسمى خلافاً لمالك وأحمد واستدلالهم بقوله تعالى وما كان لبشر أن يكلمه الله إلا وحياً إلى قوله أو يرسل رسولا) [الشورى: ٥١] أجيب عنه بأن مبنى الأيمان على العرف واعلم أن الكلام لا يكون إلا باللسان فلا يكون بالإشارة ولا بالكتابة والإخبار والإقرار والبشارة تكون بالكتابة لا بالإشارة والإيماء والإظهار والإفشاء والإعلام يكون بالإشارة أيضاً فإن نوى في ذلك كله أي في الإظهار والإفشاء والإعلام والإخبار كونه بالكلام والكتابة دون الإشارة دين فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى. ولو حلف لا يحدثه لا يحنث إلا أن يشافهه، وكذا لا يكلمه يقتصر على المشافهة. ولو قال لا أبشره فكتب إليه حنث وفي قوله إن أخبرتني أن فلاناً قدم ونحوه يحنث بالصدق والكذب، ولو قال بقدومه ونحوه فعلى الصدق خاصة، وكذا إن أعلمتني وكذا البشارة ومثله إن كتبت إلى أن فلاناً قدم فكتب قبل قدومه فوصل إليه الكتاب حنث سواء وصل إليه قبل قدومه أو بعده، بخلاف إن كتبت إلي بقدومه لا يحنث حتى يكتب بقدومه الواقع، وذكر هشام عن محمد سألني هرون الرشيد عمن حلف لا يكتب إلى فلان فأمر من يكتب إليه بإيماء أو إشارة هل يحنث ؟ فقلت : نعم يا أمير المؤمنين إذا كان مثلك. قال السرخسي : وهذا صحيح لأن السلطان لا يكتب بنفسه وإنما يأمر به ومن عادتهم الأمر بالإيماء والإشارة. ولو حلف لا يقرأ كتاب فلان فنظر فيه حتى فهمه لا يحنث عند أبي يوسف، ويحنث عند محمد لأن المقصود الوقوف على ما فيه لا عين التلفظ به . ولو حلف لا يكلم فلاناً وفلاناً لم يحنث بكلام أحدهما إلا أن ينوي كلاً منهما فيحنث بكلام أحدهما وعليه الثانية
And if he wrote a letter to him or sent a messenger to him, he does not break his oath because it is not called speech customarily, contrary to Malik and Ahmad, and their evidence is His statement (And it is not for a human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation... or He sends a messenger) [Ash-Shura 51]. It is answered that oaths are based on custom. Know that speech is only by the tongue, so it is not by signal nor by writing. Reporting, acknowledging, and giving glad tidings can be by writing, not by signal. Indicating, disclosing, informing, and spreading can be by signal as well. If he intended in all of that—i.e., indicating, disclosing, informing, reporting—that it be by speech and writing without signal, then it is between him and Allah. And if he swore not to speak with him (yuhaddithuhu), he does not break his oath unless he addresses him directly (yushafihuhu). Likewise, 'la ukallimuhu' (I will not speak to him) is limited to direct address. If he said 'la ubashshiruhu' (I will not give him glad tidings) and he wrote to him, he breaks his oath. And if he said 'in akhbartani' (if you inform me) that so-and-so arrived and the like, he breaks the oath whether the information is true or false. If he said 'bi qudumihi' (by his arrival) and the like, it is only on truth. Similarly, 'in a'lamtini' (if you notify me) and 'al-bishara' (glad tidings). And likewise, if he said 'in katabta ilayya anna fulanan qadima' (if you write to me that so-and-so arrived) and he wrote before his arrival and the letter reached him, he breaks his oath whether the letter reached before or after the arrival, unlike if he said 'in katabta ilayya bi qudumihi' (if you write to me of his arrival), he does not break his oath until he writes of the actual arrival. And Hisham narrated from Muhammad: Harun al-Rashid asked me about someone who swore not to write to so-and-so, but then ordered someone to write to him by gesture or signal—does he break his oath? I said: Yes, O Commander of the Faithful, if he is like you. Al-Sarakhsi said: This is correct because the ruler does not write himself but commands, and their custom is to command by gesture and signal. And if he swore not to read the letter of so-and-so, and he looked into it until he understood it, he does not break his oath according to Abu Yusuf, but he does according to Muhammad, because the purpose is to know its contents, not the actual pronunciation. And if he swore not to speak to so-and-so and so-and-so (two persons), he does not break his oath by speaking to one of them unless he intended each, in which case he breaks it by speaking to one, and the second [remains.]
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This is a comprehensive passage dealing with whether writing and signalling break an oath of speech. It begins by stating the Hanafi position: writing or sending a messenger does not break an oath not to speak, because custom does not consider those as speech. The text contrasts this with Malik and Ahmad, who argue using the Quranic verse (42:51) where Allah 'speaks' through revelation and sending a messenger; they analogize that writing or sending a messenger is like speech. The Hanafi response is that oaths are based on custom ('urf), not literal definitions. Then the author explains the general principle: speech is only by the tongue, so signals and writing are not speech. However, certain actions like 'giving glad tidings' (bishara) can be done by writing, so if one swears not to give glad tidings and writes, the oath is broken. Similarly, 'informing' (ikhbar) can be by writing. But if the swearer specifically intended to include writing in the oath, that is between him and Allah. The passage then details many specific oaths: 'la yuhaddithuhu' (not to speak with him) requires direct face-to-face speech; 'la ukallimuhu' is also limited. 'La ubashshiruhu' (not to give glad tidings) is broken by writing. Conditional oaths like 'if you inform me' are broken by both true and false information, but 'if you tell me of his arrival' is only broken by true information. The distinction between 'inkatabta ilayya anna fulanan qadima' (write to me that he arrived) and 'inkatabta ilayya bi qudumihi' (write to me of his arrival) is crucial: the former is broken even if the letter was written before the actual arrival (because it asserts the arrival), the latter is not broken until the actual arrival. Then a story from Imam Muhammad answering Harun al-Rashid: if a ruler (like the caliph) swears not to write to someone, but orders someone else to write by gesture, he breaks the oath because his custom is to command that way. Al-Sarakhsi affirms this. Finally, a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: if one swears not to read a letter, looking at it and understanding it breaks the oath according to Muhammad (because the purpose is comprehension), but not according to Abu Yusuf (because reading requires pronunciation). And an oath not to speak to two people separately: if he intends each individually, speaking to one breaks the oath fully (and the second remains), but if he intended them together, speaking to one does not break the oath until he speaks to both.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Custom; the common understanding in a particular time and place, used to interpret oaths.
مشافهة (mushafaha): Direct, face-to-face oral speech.
بشارة (bishara): Giving glad tidings; can be done by writing.
حنث (hanath): Breaking an oath; requires expiation (kaffarah).
كلام (kalam): Speech; only oral utterance according to Hanafi, not including writing or signs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following breaks an oath not to 'speak' (yukallim)?
A) Writing a letter
B) Sending a messenger
C) Speaking face-to-face
D) Making a gesture

Key Takeaway
Oaths are interpreted by custom (urf), so writing and signalling do not break an oath of speech unless custom or explicit intention says otherwise.

What Breaks an Oath About Speech?
Multiple rulings on oaths about speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rulings to determine whether an action breaks an oath related to speech or communication.

Core Idea
An oath about speech is broken only by the act customarily understood as that form of communication.

Oaths concerning speech and communication can be tricky. Does writing count as 'conveying'? Does a head shake break an oath not to lie? This lesson unpacks several case rulings from the Hanafi school, each illustrating how custom (urf) and literal meaning interact in oath enforcement. By the end, you'll be able to predict when a given action triggers hanth (violation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath's wording
Determine what exactly was sworn: 'I will not convey/remind/reveal/speak/lie' etc. The precise term governs the ruling.
Oaths are interpreted literally but within customary usage.
2
Classify the action performed
Was it writing, face-to-face speech, gesture, or a message? Note whether the action falls under the literal scope or customary scope of the oath.
Custom (urf) can expand or restrict the literal meaning.
3
Apply the Hanafi criterion
For each ruling, the Hanafi position hinges on whether the action is customarily understood as the prohibited act. For example, 'reminding' is customarily face-to-face, not by writing.
If the action is not customarily considered the act sworn against, there is no hanth.
4
Note exceptions based on subject
When the oath involves a category (e.g., 'woman'), children or non‑typical members may be excluded by custom (speaking) but included in other contexts (marriage).
Same word, different customary scope depending on the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفتوى وإن ذكر خلافه في بعض المواضع ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. ولو قال لا أبلغك شيئاً فكتب إليه حنث ولو قال لا أذكرك شيئاً فهو على المواجهة ولا يحنث بالكتابة، ولو قال لا أظهر سرك ولا أفشي أبداً فإن صرح إلى رجل واحد وذكره فقد أفشى سره، وكذلك يحنث بالكتابة والرسالة إلى إنسان؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي الواقعات حلف أن لا يكذب فسأله إنسان عن أمر فحرك رأسه بالكذب لا يحنث ما لم يتكلم لأن الكذب تكلم بكلام هو كذب . ابن بين زيد وعمر وحلف رجل لا يكلم ابن زيد وحلف الآخر لا يكلم ابن عمر وفكلما هذا الابن حنثاً لأن كل واحد كلم ابن من سمى : إن كلمت امرأة فعبدي حر فكلم صبية لم يحنث. ولو قال إن تزوجت امرأة فتتزوج صبية حنث لأن الصبا مانع من هجران الكلام فلا تراد الصبية في اليمين المعقودة على الكلام عادة ولا كذلك التزوج ا هـ .
And the fatwa, though disagreement is mentioned in some places; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And if he said 'I will not convey anything to you' and he wrote to him, he breaks his oath. And if he said 'I will not remind you of anything', it is regarding face-to-face and he does not break his oath by writing. And if he said 'I will not reveal your secret and I will never disclose it', then if he disclosed to one man and mentioned it, he has indeed disclosed his secret. Similarly he breaks his oath by writing and sending a message to a person; thus in al-Muhit. And in al-Waqi'at: He swore not to lie. A person asked him about something and he shook his head with a lie—he does not break his oath as long as he does not speak, because lying is speaking words that are a lie. A son between Zayd and `Amr? And a man swore not to speak to the son of Zayd, and another swore not to speak to the son of `Umar, and whenever this son? he breaks his oath? Because each one spoke to the son of the one he named. [If he said:] 'If I speak to a woman, my slave is free,' and he spoke to a young girl, he does not break his oath. And if he said 'If I marry a woman,' and he marries a young girl, he breaks his oath because being young prevents [the usual interpretation] of leaving speech? So a young girl is not intended in an oath based on speech customarily, but not so in marriage. End quote.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings from the Hanafi school regarding oaths related to speech. The first ruling addresses the difference between conveying something verbally and in writing: if one swears not to convey something and then writes it, he breaks the oath. But if he swears not to remind someone, it is limited to face-to-face reminder, not by writing. The second ruling: if one swears not to reveal a secret, telling it to even one person is a breach, and writing or sending a message also counts. The third: shaking one's head in response to a question does not constitute a lie in an oath against lying unless actual words are spoken, because lying is defined as speaking falsehood. Then there is an example of two different men each swearing not to speak to a specific person's son: if they speak to the son they named, they break their oaths. Finally, the distinction between speaking to a woman and marrying a young girl: in oaths about speaking, a young girl is not customarily considered a woman, so speaking to her does not break an oath against speaking to women. But in oaths about marriage, a young girl is considered a woman because being young does not prevent marriage, so marrying her does break the oath.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): A solemn oath sworn by Allah, used as a legal instrument to create obligations or prohibitions.
حنث (hanth): Violation of an oath; incurring the obligation of kaffarah (expiation).
الكذب (al-kidhb): Intentionally stating a falsehood; in oaths, it requires spoken words to be considered a violation.
المواجهة (al-muwajaha): Direct, face-to-face verbal communication.
الصبية (al-sabiyya): A young girl who has not reached puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person swears not to speak to a woman and then speaks to a young girl (sabiyya), what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath.
B) He does not break his oath.
C) He breaks his oath only if she is of marriageable age.
D) He must pay expiation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths about speech are interpreted by customary usage: writing is conveying but not reminding, a headshake is not lying, and 'woman' may or may not include children depending on the context.

Rulings on Oaths Not to Speak: Implicit Speech, Self-Questioning, and Counting Violations
Rulings on oaths not to speak to wife and a story about counting violations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a statement or question is considered speech under an oath not to speak, and apply the Hanafi method for counting oath violations.

Core Idea
A question directed at no one in particular is speech to the sole addressee; 'I wish I knew' is not; each separate utterance can count as a separate breach.

This lesson from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents key Hanafi principles on oaths not to speak. Through scenarios from al-Zahiriya and a famous test of Imam Abu Hanifa by his student Muhammad al-Shaybani, we learn how intent and context determine breach of oath, and how multiple oath violations are counted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and prohibited act
The oath is 'I will not speak to you'. The prohibited act is speaking (uttering words meant for communication).
Speech includes questions, statements, and commands directed at the sworn person.
2
Analyze whether the utterance is directed at the sworn person
If the utterance is a question with no specific addressee, it is considered directed at the only person present. If others are present, it may not be directed at the sworn person.
The presence of others changes the default assumption.
3
Distinguish self-questioning from direct speech
Phrases like 'I wish I knew' are self-questioning, not addressed to another, so they do not break the oath.
Hanafis classify these as internal speech (استفهم نفسه).
4
Consider intention in reading/dictating
If the swearing person reads a book to the other person and intends to dictate (i.e., communicate), it is feared he breaks his oath.
The key is the intent to communicate.
5
Apply counting of violations for multiple utterances
In the story, Abu Hanifa ruled that the man who said 'I will not speak to you three times' broke his oath twice after uttering 'What?' and possibly another statement, each separate speech act counting as a breach.
Each distinct utterance is a separate violation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : حلف لا يكلم امرأته فدخل داره وليس فيها غيرها فقال من وضع هذا حنث ولو كان معها غيرها لا يحنث . ولو قال ليت شعري من وضع هذا لا يحنث لأنه استفهم نفسه. ولو قرأ الحالف كتاباً على المحلوف عليه والمحلوف عليه يكتب إن قصد الحالف املاء المحلوف عليه قالوا يخاف عليه الحنث ا.هـ. وفي السراجية عن محمد بن الحسن أنه سأل حال صغره أبا حنيفة فيمن قال لآخر والله لا أكلمك ثلاث مرات فقال أبو حنيفة : ماذا ؟ فتبسم محمد رحمه الله وقال : انظر حسناً يا شيخ فنكس أبو حنيفة ثم رفع رأسه فقال : حنث مرتين . فقال له محمد : أحسنت. فقال أبو حنيفة : لا أدري أي الكلمتين أوجع لي قوله انظر حسناً أو أحسنت ا هـ.
And in al-Zahiriya: He swore not to speak to his wife, then he entered his house and there was no one else in it but her, and he said 'Who put this?' — he breaks his oath. And if there was someone else with her, he does not break his oath. And if he said 'I wish I knew who put this', he does not break his oath because he asked himself. And if the person who swore read a book to the one against whom the oath was made, and that person was writing, if the swearing person intended to dictate, they said: it is feared that he breaks his oath. End quote. And in al-Sirajiyya from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan that when he was young he asked Abu Hanifa about one who said to another 'By Allah, I will not speak to you three times'. Abu Hanifa said 'What?' Then Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) smiled and said 'Look well, O shaykh.' So Abu Hanifa lowered his head, then raised it and said 'He broke his oath twice.' Muhammad said 'Excellent.' Abu Hanifa said 'I do not know which of the two words hurt me more: his saying 'Look well' or 'Excellent'.' End quote.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage from al-Zahiriya deals with oaths not to speak to one's wife. If he enters a house where only she is present and asks a general question like 'Who put this?', his oath is broken because the question is implicitly directed at her. But if others are present, the question is not necessarily for her, so no breach. Similarly, if he says 'I wish I knew who put this', it is a self-question, not speech to her, so no breach. If the oath-bearer reads a book to the other person and intends to dictate while the other writes, it is feared he breaks his oath because it is like speaking. Then a famous story: young Muhammad al-Shaybani tests Imam Abu Hanifa by asking about a man who says 'I will not speak to you three times.' Abu Hanifa first asks 'What?', then after reflection says he broke his oath twice. The story illustrates the precision of the imam in counting the number of oath violations.

Key Terms
الظهيرية (al-Zahiriya): A Hanafi fiqh work by al-Zahiri, cited as a source for these rulings.
السراجية (al-Sirajiyya): A Hanafi work, likely by al-Sarakhsi or similar, containing the story of Muhammad al-Shaybani.
استفهم نفسه (istafhama nafsahu): He asked himself; a rhetorical self-question not directed at another.
حنث مرتين (hanth marratayn): Breaking the oath twice; in the story, the man's utterances constitute two separate violations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the story of Muhammad and Abu Hanifa, a man says 'By Allah, I will not speak to you three times.' Abu Hanifa initially responds 'What?' (ماذا). After reflection, how many times does he rule the man broke the oath?
A) One time
B) Two times
C) Three times
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the context and intent of an utterance determine whether it constitutes speech for oath purposes, and each separate speech act counts as a distinct violation.

Oath Conditional on Unknown Permission: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath and unknown permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two intra-Hanafi positions on whether an oath is broken when permission is given without the swearer's knowledge, explain the reasoning behind each, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Permission is an internal act; knowledge of it is not required for its legal effect in oaths.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the role of knowledge in permission (idhn). When a person swears not to speak except with another's permission, and that permission is granted but unknown to the swearer, does speaking break the oath? Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree, and the school ultimately adopts Abu Yusuf's view as the operative fatwa. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the Hanafi school defines permission and the importance of internal states (a'mal al-qalb) versus external awareness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the legal issue
State the scenario: a person swears not to speak to Zaid except with Zaid's permission. Zaid gives permission, but the swearer does not know. Does speaking break the oath?
The issue is whether the effectiveness of permission depends on the recipient's knowledge.
2
State each imam's position
Abu Yusuf: the oath is not broken (la yahnath). Muhammad: the oath is broken (yahnath).
Both are within the Hanafi school; this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the reasoning of each
Abu Yusuf: permission is an internal act of the heart (amal al-qalb), like consent (rida). It does not require external manifestation or knowledge. Muhammad: permission only takes effect when known, analogous to a master's permission to a slave being contingent on the slave's awareness.
The slave analogy is a key point of contention.
4
Examine the counter-arguments and reconciliations
The text references al-Tatimma and al-Fatawa al-Sughra, which say that a master's permission to a slave is not valid until the slave knows. But the text states this does not contradict Abu Yusuf's view. Why? Because the slave case is different: a slave's legal capacity is limited, whereas the oath case concerns a free person's internal state.
The distinction is that the slave case involves legal effect (ijazah) that requires knowledge, while permission in the oath context is merely an internal release of restriction.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical consequence
The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's saying: the oath is not broken. Therefore, if the swearer speaks without knowing of the permission, no expiation is due.
This is the relied-upon view for issuing fatwas, as affirmed by later authorities like al-Bazzaziyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المسألة الثانية وهي ما إذا حلف لا يكلمه إلا بإذنه فأذن له ولم يعلم بالإذن حتى الإعلام أو من الوقوع في الإذن وكل ذلك لا يتحقق فلان. الإذن مشتق من هو ثم يتم كلمه بالإذن كالرضا. قلنا : الأذان الذي هو إلا بالسماع وقال أبو يوسف : لا يحنث لأن الإذن الاطلاق وأنه أعمال القلب ولا كذلك الأذن على ما مر ولا يخالفه ما في التتمة والفتاوى الصغرى الرضا من إذا أذن المولى لعبده والعبد لا يعلم لا يصح الإذن حتى إذا علم يصير مأذوناً لأن الإذن يثبت موقوفاً على العلم فليس له قبل العلم حكم الإذن ولذا قال الشامل : إذا أذن لعبده فلم يعلم به أحد من الناس فتصرف العبد ثم علم بإذنه لم يجز تصرفه . من أنه يكون بالكتابة لا بالإشارة فإنه لو أخبر بالإشارة لم يحنث فما معنى كونه يصدق ديانة؟ والعبارة المذكورة مأخوذة من الفتح ومثلها في البزازية تأمل قوله : (وكذا إن أعلمتني وكذا البشارة) هذا مخالف لما سيذكره المؤلف في الباب الآتي من أن البشارة لا بد أن تكون على الصدق بلا فرق بين أن يأتي بالباء أو لا. وكذا الإعلام لا بد فيه من الصدق لأنه إثبات العلم والكذب لا يفيده بلا فرق بين أن يأتي فيه بالباء أو لا قوله : (لا يحنث عند أبي يوسف ويحنث عند محمد سيأتي في شرح قوله «لا يتكلم» أن الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف قوله: (ولا يخالفه ما في التتمة والفتاوى الصغرى الخ) أي لا
As for the second issue, which is: if he swore not to speak to him except with his permission, and he gave him permission, but he did not know about the permission until the announcement, or from the occurrence of the permission, and all that is not realized...? Permission is derived from... then he completes speaking with permission like consent. We said: hearing which is only through listening. And Abu Yusuf said: He does not break his oath because permission is absolute freedom and it is an act of the heart, and not so hearing, as mentioned earlier. And what is in al-Tatimma and al-Fatawa al-Sughra does not contradict this. Consent: if a master gives permission to his slave and the slave does not know, the permission is not valid until he knows, so he becomes authorized, because permission is established contingently on knowledge. So before knowledge there is no ruling of permission. For this reason, al-Shamil said: If a master gives permission to his slave and no one among the people knows about it, then the slave acts, then learns of the permission, his action is not valid. And that it is by writing, not by gesture; for if he informed by gesture he does not break his oath. So what is the meaning of his being truthful religiously? The mentioned phrase is taken from al-Fath and similarly in al-Bazzaziyya. Consider his saying: 'Similarly if you inform me, and similarly good news.' This contradicts what the author will mention in the following chapter that good news must be truthful, regardless of whether the preposition 'bi' is used or not. Similarly, informing must be truthful because it establishes knowledge, and lying does not produce knowledge, regardless of whether 'bi' is used or not. His saying: 'He does not break his oath according to Abu Yusuf, and breaks it according to Muhammad; it will come in the explanation of his saying 'he does not speak' that the fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf.' His saying: 'And what is in al-Tatimma and al-Fatawa al-Sughra does not contradict it' meaning no.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute about an oath conditional on permission. The scenario: a man swears not to speak to another except with his permission. The other person gives permission, but the swearer does not know about it. Does speaking break the oath? Imam Abu Yusuf holds that permission is essentially a state of mind ('amal al-qalb) and does not require the recipient's knowledge; therefore, once permission is granted, speaking is not a violation. Imam Muhammad disagrees, holding that permission only takes effect when known by the oath-bearer. The fatwa (relied-upon view) is on Abu Yusuf's position. The text also discusses a parallel: a master permitting his slave without the slave's knowledge—the permission is not effective until the slave knows. That example is used to challenge Abu Yusuf's view, but the text clarifies that the ruling from al-Tatimma and al-Fatawa al-Sughra does not contradict Abu Yusuf. The passage further examines how permission is communicated: by writing (which breaks the oath if used to inform) but not by gesture (which does not break the oath). The text also touches on the requirement of truthfulness in 'informing' and 'good news'—these must be true to be effective.

Key Terms
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission; in this context, it is considered an act of the heart (internal state) by Abu Yusuf, and not dependent on the recipient's knowledge.
أعمال القلب (a'mal al-qalb): Acts of the heart, internal mental or spiritual actions, as opposed to external speech or writing.
الفتوى (al-fatwa): The relied-upon legal opinion for practice; here it is the position of Abu Yusuf.
الإعلام (al-i'lam): Informing; must be truthful to be effective in establishing knowledge for the oath-bearer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon fatwa), if a person swears not to speak to Zaid except with Zaid's permission, and Zaid gives permission but the swearer does not know, and then the swearer speaks, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath and must pay expiation.
B) He does not break his oath because permission was already given.
C) He breaks his oath only if Zaid explicitly told him.
D) The oath is invalid from the start.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that permission in an oath is an internal act (amal al-qalb) effective without the oath-bearer's knowledge, so speaking with permission already given does not break the oath.

Oaths with Time Limits: Computing Month, Day, and Night
Rulings on computing oath periods for month, day, night

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state how to compute the period for an oath specifying a month, day, or night, distinguishing between definite and indefinite forms, and apply the Hanafi rule that indefinite periods start from the moment of swearing and include the remainder plus full units to complete the term.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, an indefinite time period in an oath starts at the oath moment and includes the remainder plus necessary completion.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the intricate rules for calculating time periods in oaths. The key distinction is whether the time word is definite (like 'today') or indefinite (like 'a day'). The student will learn how the indication of the situation (dalalat al-hal) determines the start and end of the oath period, and how this differs from vows and other contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Notice that an oath without a time limit is eternal. Mentioning a time period serves to exclude what is beyond it, so the period includes the time immediately after the oath. This is based on the indication of the situation (dalalat al-hal).
2
Distinguish indefinite from definite forms
For indefinite forms like 'a month' (شهراً), the period is 30 full days from the oath. For definite 'the month' (الشهر), it means the remainder of the current lunar month. Similarly, 'a day' (يوماً) indefinite includes the night following the oath until the same hour next day; definite 'today' ends at sunset.
This distinction arises because an indefinite form needs specification by the situation, while a definite form refers to a known unit.
3
Apply to specific cases: month, day, night
Using the principle, compute each case: for 'a month' indefinite, 30 days from the oath; for 'a day' sworn during the day, it covers the remainder of today, the coming night, and the next day until the same hour; for 'a night' sworn during the night, it covers the remainder of the night, the following day, and the next night until the same hour.
The night enters a day only as dependent (تابع), and vice versa, because the indefinite form requires fulfillment of a complete unit.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement on guarantee periods
The passage mentions a dispute regarding the phrase 'I guarantee you myself up to a month' (كفلت لك بنفسك إلى شهر). Abu Yusuf held that it marks the end of the claim (no obligation after the month), while others held it marks the beginning (no obligation before the month). The latter is preferred (أحسن) because the term in such contexts is for leniency.
This disagreement is resolved by analogizing to debt terms (آجال الديون), where the term indicates the beginning.
5
Understand the analogical extension
The same principle applies to other contracts like rental (إذا آجره شهراً) and debt terms. The reasoning (qiyas) is that just as in oaths, mentioning a time period in a rental or debt sets the start from the contract moment unless otherwise specified.
This shows the Hanafi method of extending rulings from one area to another through common illa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لا يكلمه شهراً فهو من حين حلف) لأنه لو لم يذكر الشهر تتأبد اليمين فذكر الشهر لإخراج ما وراءه فبقي ما يلي يمينه داخلاً عملاً بدلالة الحالة بخلاف ما إذا قال والله لأصومن شهراً أو لأعتكفن شهراً لأنه لو لم يذكر الشهر لا تتأبد اليمين فكان ذكره لتقدير الصوم به وأنه منكر فالتعيين إليه بخلاف ما إذا قال إن تركت الصوم شهراً فإنه يتناول شهراً من حين حلف لأن تركه مطلقاً يتناول الأبد فذكر الوقت لإخراج ما وراءه فهو كقوله إن تركت كلامه شهراً وإن لم أساكنه شهراً، ونظيره إذا آجره شهراً وكذا آجال الديون. وأما الأجل في قوله كفلت لك بنفسك إلى شهر اختلف في أنها البيان ابتداء المدة أو لانتهائها، فعن أبي يوسف لانتهاء المطالبة فلا يلزم بإحضاره بعد الشهر وإلحاقاها بآجال الديون فجعلاها لبيان ابتدائها فلا يلزم بإحضارها قبل الشهر وهو أحسن، لأن الأجل في مثله للترفية؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي البدائع : ولو حلف لا يكلمه شهراً يقع على ثلاثين يوماً، ولو قال الشهر يقع على بقية الشهر، ولو حلف لا يكلمه السنة يقع على بقية السنة . وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو حلف بالليل لا يكلمه يوماً فإنه يحنث بكلامه من حين حلف إلى أن تغيب الشمس من الغد يدخل في يمينه بقية الليل حتى لو كلمه فيما بقي من الليل أو الغد يحنث لأن ذكر اليوم للإخراج، وكذا لو حلف بالنهار لا يكلمه ليلة حنث بكلامه من حين حلف إلى طلوع الفجر، ولو قال في بعض النهار لا أكلمه يوماً فاليمين على بقية اليوم والليلة المستقبلة إلى مثل تلك الساعة التي حلف فيها من الغد لأنه حلف على يوم منكر فلا بد من استيفائه ولا يمكن استيفاؤه إلا باتمامه من اليوم الثاني فيدخل الليل بطريق التبع. وكذا إذا حلف لا يكلمه ليلة فاليمين من تلك الساعة إلى أن يجيء مثلها من الليلة المستقبلة فيدخل النهار الذي بينهما في ذلك لأنه حلف على ليلة منكرة فلا بد من الاستيفاء. فإن قال في بعض اليوم والله لا أكلمك اليوم فاليمين على ما بقي من اليوم، فإذا غربت الشمس سقطت اليمين، وكذلك إذا قال بالليل والله لا أكلمك الليلة فإذا طلع الفجر سقطت، ولو قال والله لا أكلمك اليوم ولا غداً فاليمين على بقية اليوم وعلى غد، ولا تدخل الليلة التي بينهما في اليمين كذا في البدائع وفي الواقعات : حلف لا يكلمه اليوم ولا غداً ولا بعد غد فله أن يكلمه بالليل لأنها أيمان ثلاثة ولو لم يكرر حرف النفي فهي يمين واحدة فيدخل الليل بمنزلة قوله ثلاثة أيام وفي الظهيرية: ولو قال والله لا
His saying: 'He will not speak to him for a month, so it is from the time he swore' because if he had not mentioned the month, the oath would be eternal. So mentioning the month is to exclude what is beyond it, so what immediately follows his oath remains included according to the indication of the situation. Unlike if he said 'By Allah, I will surely fast for a month' or 'I will surely perform i'tikaf for a month', because if he had not mentioned the month, the oath would not be eternal. So his mentioning it is to determine the fast's duration, and it is indefinite, so the specification is up to him. Unlike if he said 'If I leave the fast for a month', then it refers to a month from the time he swore, because leaving [the fast] absolutely would be eternal, so mentioning the time is to exclude what is beyond it. So it is like his saying 'If I leave speaking to him for a month' or 'If I do not live with him for a month.' And its analogue is if he rents something for a month, and similarly the terms of debts. As for the term in his saying 'I guarantee you myself up to a month', there is disagreement whether it indicates the beginning of the period or its end. From Abu Yusuf: it is the end of the claim, so he is not obliged to bring him after the month. But attaching it to the terms of debts makes it indicate the beginning, so he is not obliged to bring him before the month, and that is better because the term in such cases is for leniency. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Bada'i: If he swore not to speak to him for a month, it applies to thirty days. And if he said 'the month', it applies to the remainder of the month. And if he swore not to speak to him for a year, it applies to the remainder of the year. And the author indicated that if he swore in the night not to speak to him for a day, then he breaks his oath by speaking from the time of his oath until sunset of the next day. The remainder of the night enters into his oath, such that if he spoke during the rest of the night or the day, he breaks his oath because mentioning 'day' is for exclusion. Similarly, if he swore during the day not to speak to him for a night, he breaks his oath by speaking from his oath until dawn. And if he said during some of the day 'I will not speak to him for a day', the oath is on the remainder of the day and the coming night until the same hour of the next day, because he swore an indefinite day, so it must be fulfilled, and it cannot be fulfilled except by completing it from the second day, so the night enters by way of dependence. Similarly, if he swore not to speak to him for a night, the oath is from that hour until the same hour of the coming night, so the day between enters because he swore an indefinite night, so it must be fulfilled. If he said during some of the day 'By Allah, I will not speak to you today', the oath is on the remainder of the day, so when the sun sets, the oath lapses. Similarly, if he said during the night 'By Allah, I will not speak to you tonight', then when dawn breaks, it lapses. And if he said 'By Allah, I will not speak to you today nor tomorrow', the oath is on the remainder of today and on tomorrow, and the night between them is not included in the oath, thus in al-Bada'i. And in al-Waqi'at: He swore not to speak to him today, nor tomorrow, nor the day after, so he may speak to him at night because they are three separate oaths. And if he did not repeat the negative particle, it is one oath, so the night enters like saying 'three days'. And in al-Zahiriya: And if he said 'By Allah, no...'
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Raiq explains in detail how to compute the time period for oaths that specify a duration. The key principle is whether the time word is definite (e.g., 'today') or indefinite (e.g., 'a day'). For indefinite periods like 'a month' or 'a day', the period starts from the moment of the oath and includes the remainder of the current unit (e.g., remainder of the day) followed by full units to complete the total (e.g., 'a month' is 30 days; if said on the 15th, it covers the remaining 15 days plus 15 of the next month? Actually the text says 'إذا قال الشهر يقع على بقية الشهر' for 'the month' meaning the current month. For 'a month' indefinite, it is 30 days from the oath. The passage distinguishes this from vows (nadhr) of fasting or i'tikaf, where mentioning the month is for estimation and the person has discretion. It also compares the oath of not speaking for a month to similar contexts like rental periods and debt terms. There is a dispute between Abu Yusuf and others about whether a guarantee period indicates the beginning or end of the period. The passage then gives specific rulings for oaths using 'day' and 'night': if sworn during the day not to speak for 'a day', it includes the remainder of that day and the following night until the same time next day (since a day includes its night). If sworn at night not to speak for 'a day', it includes the remainder of the night and the next day until sunset. If the time word is definite ('today'), it lapses at sunset. For multiple days like 'today, tomorrow, and the day after', if the negative is repeated, they are separate oaths; if not repeated, it is one oath and includes nights. The passage ends with examples from al-Waqi'at and al-Zahiriya.

Key Terms
الشهر (al-shahr): A month; in indefinite oaths, it means 30 consecutive days from the oath. In definite form ('the month'), it means the remainder of the current lunar month.
اليوم (al-yawm): A day; in indefinite oaths, it includes the night following the oath until the same hour next day. Definite 'today' means until sunset.
الليلة (al-layla): A night; indefinite 'night' includes the following day until the same hour. Definite 'tonight' ends at dawn.
بقية (baqiya): The remainder; e.g., 'بقية الشهر' means the remaining days of the current month from the oath time.
الأجل (al-ajal): A term or period, especially in debts and guarantees; here it refers to the time limit for bringing a person in a kafalah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears at noon (during the day) 'By Allah, I will not speak to you for a day' (indefinite), when does the oath period end?
A) At sunset of the same day
B) At noon of the next day
C) At dawn of the next day
D) After 24 hours from the oath

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an indefinite time period in an oath starts from the moment of swearing and includes the remainder of the current unit plus full units to complete the term, while a definite period ends with the conclusion of the current unit.

When Does an Oath to Remain Silent for a Month Begin?
Ruling with proof on interpreting oath terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an oath not to speak for a month begins immediately from the time of swearing and distinguish it from oaths to perform acts for a month.

Core Idea
An oath not to speak for a month starts immediately, because the term limits a potentially perpetual oath.

When someone swears not to speak to another for a month, does the clock start ticking from the moment of the oath or from the next day? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the Hanafi position, using contextual indication (dalaalat al-haal) and drawing parallels with rental contracts and debt terms. Understanding this ruling helps avoid common mistakes in oath interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an oath not to speak for a month. The Hanafi ruling: the period begins from the time of swearing (min hina halafa).
The text explicitly states 'فهو من حين حلف'.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is contextual indication (dalaalat al-haal): mentioning the month serves to limit the otherwise perpetual oath, so the period immediately after swearing is included.
If no month were mentioned, the oath would be permanent (ta'abbud al-yamin).
3
Contrast with positive oaths
Contrast with oaths to fast or perform i'tikaf for a month. In those cases, the mention of a month is to specify the duration, but since the act itself is not perpetual, the person has discretion on when to start.
The key difference: abstaining from speech is inherently capable of being perpetual.
4
Apply the analogy
The principle extends to similar cases: rental for a month, debt deadlines, and oaths of abandonment like 'if I do not speak to him for a month' or 'if I do not cohabit with him for a month'. In all these, the period starts from the time of swearing.
The text cites parallels with إجارة وأجال الديون.
5
Differentiate indefinite vs definite
If the oath uses 'a month' (shahr, indefinite), it means thirty consecutive days. If it uses 'the month' (al-shahr, definite), it means the remainder of the current lunar month. Similarly for 'a year'.
This is from al-Bada'i' as quoted in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لا يكلمه شهراً فهو من حين حلف لأنه لو لم يذكر الشهر تتأبد اليمين فذكر الشهر لإخراج ما وراءه فبقي ما يلي يمينه داخلاً عملاً بدلالة الحالة بخلاف ما إذا قال والله لأصومن شهراً أو لأعتكفن شهراً لأنه لو لم يذكر الشهر لا تتأبد اليمين فكان ذكره لتقدير الصوم به وأنه منكر فالتعيين إليه بخلاف ما إذا قال إن تركت الصوم شهراً فإنه يتناول شهراً من حين حلف لأن تركه مطلقاً يتناول الأبد فذكر الوقت لإخراج ما وراءه فهو كقوله إن تركت كلامه شهراً وإن لم أساكنه شهراً، ونظيره إذا آجره شهراً وكذا آجال الديون. وأما الأجل في قوله كفلت لك بنفسك إلى شهر اختلف في أنها البيان ابتداء المدة أو لانتهائها، فعن أبي يوسف لانتهاء المطالبة فلا يلزم بإحضاره بعد الشهر وإلحاقاها بآجال الديون فجعلاها لبيان ابتدائها فلا يلزم بإحضارها قبل الشهر وهو أحسن، لأن الأجل في مثله للترفية؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي البدائع : ولو حلف لا يكلمه شهراً يقع على ثلاثين يوماً، ولو قال الشهر يقع على بقية الشهر، ولو حلف لا يكلمه السنة يقع على بقية السنة .
His statement: ('He does not speak to him for a month' means it begins from the time he swore, because if he did not mention the month, the oath would be permanent. So mentioning the month is to exclude what is beyond it, thus the period immediately following his oath is included, acting on contextual indication. Contrary to if he said 'By Allah, I will fast a month' or 'I will perform i'tikaf a month', because if he did not mention the month, the oath would not be permanent, so his mention of it is to estimate the fast with it, and it is indefinite, so the specific determination is left to him. Contrary to if he said 'If I abandon fasting for a month', it encompasses a month from the time of swearing, because absolute abandonment encompasses perpetuity, so mentioning the time is to exclude what is beyond it, similar to his saying 'If I abandon speaking to him for a month' or 'If I do not cohabit with him for a month'. Its parallel is if he rents to him for a month, and likewise the terms of debts. As for the term in his statement 'I guarantee you yourself until a month', there is disagreement whether it indicates the beginning of the term or its end. From Abu Yusuf, it is for the end of the claim, so he is not obliged to produce him after the month. And attaching it to the terms of debts makes it indicate the beginning, so he is not obliged to produce him before the month, which is better, because the term in such cases is for ease; as in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Bada'i': If he swears not to speak to him for a month, it applies to thirty days. And if he said 'the month', it applies to the remainder of the month. And if he swore not to speak to him for a year, it applies to the remainder of the year.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the interpretation of an oath not to speak for a specified period. The author begins by stating the general rule: if one swears 'I will not speak to him for a month', the period starts from the moment the oath is taken. The reason is that mentioning the month serves to limit the otherwise perpetual oath. The author then contrasts this with oaths to perform an act like fasting or i'tikaf for a month, where the month is indefinite and the person has discretion in choosing when to start. The key difference is the nature of the act: abstaining from speech is inherently capable of being perpetual, while fasting is not. The text also compares this to contractual terms like rent and debt deadlines, illustrating the same principle. Finally, al-Bada'i' adds that if one says 'a month' (indefinite), it means thirty consecutive days, while if one says 'the month' (definite), it means the remainder of the current month.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath swearing by Allah to do or not do something.
شهر (shahr): A month; when indefinite it refers to 30 consecutive days, when definite it refers to the current lunar month.
منكر (nakirah): Indefinite noun; here it refers to an unspecified month allowing choice.
أجل (ajal): A fixed term or deadline, especially in contracts and debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a person swears 'I will not speak to Zaid for a month,' when does the period begin?
A) From the next day.
B) From the moment of swearing.
C) From the beginning of the next lunar month.
D) From the time he sees Zaid.

Key Takeaway
When an oath includes a term that limits a potentially perpetual act, the term starts immediately upon swearing.

Determining the Period for Oaths Involving a Day or Night
Defines oath durations for day/night

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the exact time period for an oath involving a day or night, applying the Hanafi rule that indefinite time units must be completed and definite ones end at sunset or dawn.

Core Idea
An indefinite day extends to sunset next day; an indefinite night extends to dawn.

When someone swears not to speak for a day or night, the precise start and end depend on when the oath is uttered and whether the time unit is definite or indefinite. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq elaborates the rules for various scenarios, ensuring that the oath is fulfilled correctly without unintended violations.

Guided Path
1
Classify the time of swearing
Identify whether the oath was made during the day or night. This determines the baseline for calculating the period.
Swearing at night vs. day triggers different end points.
2
Identify the phrase: indefinite or definite
Determine if the time unit is indefinite ('a day', 'a night') or definite ('today', 'tonight'). Indefinite requires completion of the unit; definite only covers the remainder.
Also note if multiple days are mentioned and whether the negation is repeated.
3
Apply the rule for indefinite day or night
For an indefinite day sworn at night, the oath lasts until sunset the next day, including the remainder of the night. For an indefinite day sworn during part of the day, the oath lasts until the same hour the next day, including the intervening night as subsidiary. For an indefinite night sworn during the day, the oath lasts until dawn; if sworn at night, until the same hour the next night, including the intervening day.
The key is completing the full unit starting from the time of swearing.
4
Apply the rule for definite 'today' or 'tonight'
If the phrase is 'today' (sworn during the day), the oath only covers the remainder of that day, ending at sunset. If 'tonight' (sworn at night), it covers the remainder of that night, ending at dawn.
Definite phrases are limited to the current unit.
5
Handle multiple days and repeated negation
If the oath is 'today and tomorrow' with a single negation, the night between is included (as part of a single continuous period). If the negation is repeated ('today nor tomorrow nor the day after'), each is a separate oath, and the nights are excluded, allowing speech at night.
Repetition of the negation creates separate oaths; single negation treats them as one period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو حلف بالليل لا يكلمه يوماً فإنه يحنث بكلامه من حين حلف إلى أن تغيب الشمس من الغد يدخل في يمينه بقية الليل حتى لو كلمه فيما بقي من الليل أو الغد يحنث لأن ذكر اليوم للإخراج، وكذا لو حلف بالنهار لا يكلمه ليلة حنث بكلامه من حين حلف إلى طلوع الفجر، ولو قال في بعض النهار لا أكلمه يوماً فاليمين على بقية اليوم والليلة المستقبلة إلى مثل تلك الساعة التي حلف فيها من الغد لأنه حلف على يوم منكر فلا بد من استيفائه ولا يمكن استيفاؤه إلا باتمامه من اليوم الثاني فيدخل الليل بطريق التبع. وكذا إذا حلف لا يكلمه ليلة فاليمين من تلك الساعة إلى أن يجيء مثلها من الليلة المستقبلة فيدخل النهار الذي بينهما في ذلك لأنه حلف على ليلة منكرة فلا بد من الاستيفاء. فإن قال في بعض اليوم والله لا أكلمك اليوم فاليمين على ما بقي من اليوم، فإذا غربت الشمس سقطت اليمين، وكذلك إذا قال بالليل والله لا أكلمك الليلة فإذا طلع الفجر سقطت، ولو قال والله لا أكلمك اليوم ولا غداً فاليمين على بقية اليوم وعلى غد، ولا تدخل الليلة التي بينهما في اليمين كذا في البدائع وفي الواقعات : حلف لا يكلمه اليوم ولا غداً ولا بعد غد فله أن يكلمه بالليل لأنها أيمان ثلاثة ولو لم يكرر حرف النفي فهي يمين واحدة فيدخل الليل بمنزلة قوله ثلاثة أيام
The author indicated that if he swears at night 'I will not speak to him for a day', he breaks the oath by speaking from the time he swore until the sun sets the next day. The remainder of the night enters his oath, so that if he speaks during the remainder of the night or the next day, he breaks the oath because mentioning the day serves to exclude beyond it. Similarly, if he swears during the day 'I will not speak to him for a night', he breaks the oath by speaking from the time he swore until dawn. And if he says during part of the day 'I will not speak to him for a day', the oath is on the remainder of the day and the coming night until the same hour the next day, because he swore on an indefinite day, so it must be completed, and it can only be completed by finishing it from the second day, so the night enters as subsidiary. Similarly, if he swears not to speak to him for a night, the oath is from that hour until the same hour of the next night, so the day between enters, because he swore on an indefinite night, so it must be completed. If he says during part of the day 'By Allah, I will not speak to you today', the oath is on what remains of the day; when the sun sets, the oath falls. Similarly, if he says at night 'By Allah, I will not speak to you tonight', when dawn appears, it falls. And if he says 'By Allah, I will not speak to you today nor tomorrow', the oath is on the remainder of today and on tomorrow, and the night between them does not enter the oath, as in al-Bada'i'. And in al-Waqi'at: He swore not to speak to him today, nor tomorrow, nor the day after; he may speak to him at night, because they are three oaths. If he did not repeat the negation, it is one oath, so the night enters, like his saying 'three days'.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage details the precise time boundaries for oaths involving a day or night. The core principle is that when the time unit (day or night) is mentioned as an indefinite noun, it must be completed in full, including the remainder of the current period and the next period as needed. For example, swearing at night not to speak for a day includes the rest of the night and all of the next day until sunset. If sworn during the day not to speak for a night, it includes the rest of the day? Actually the text says: 'if he swears during the day not to speak for a night, he breaks the oath from that time until dawn.' So it covers the remainder of the day and the night until dawn. The author then explains various scenarios: partial day swearing for a day (indefinite) requires completion from the next day. Multiple days: 'today and tomorrow' does not include the night between, but if the negation is repeated, they become separate oaths, and the night can be used for speech.

Key Terms
يوم (yawm): Day; when indefinite, it means from the time of oath to sunset the next day if sworn at night, or to the same hour next day if sworn during the day.
ليلة (laylah): Night; when indefinite, it means from the time of oath to dawn (if sworn during the day) or to the same hour next night (if sworn at night).
تبع (taba'): Subsidiary; the night enters the day oath as a subsidiary element to complete the full day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears at night 'I will not speak to you for a day', when does the oath end?
A) At dawn.
B) At sunset the next day.
C) At the same time the next night.
D) At noon the next day.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets indefinite time units in oaths by completion of the unit, while definite units restrict only the remainder.

Oath with an Exception: Choosing the Excepted Day
Ruling on an oath with an unspecified exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for an oath that excludes an unspecified day and distinguish it from the case where the exception is worded as 'except a day's deficiency'.

Core Idea
In an oath with an exception, the swearer chooses the excepted day; wording matters.

This lesson examines a concise ruling on oaths with exceptions. The Hanafi school gives the swearer flexibility when the excepted day is unspecified, but a slight change in wording alters the calculation. You'll learn how to apply this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath phrase
The phrase is 'لا يكلمك شهراً إلا يوماً' meaning 'I will not speak to you for a month except a day'.
The exception is made using 'illa'.
2
Note the absence of intention
The swearer has no specific day in mind (لا نية له). This triggers the rule.
If there was an intention, the ruling might differ.
3
Apply the principle of choice
The swearer may choose any day as the exception. The excepted day is not part of the oath period.
This gives flexibility to the swearer.
4
Contrast with alternative phrasing
If the exception is worded as 'except a day's deficiency' (إلا نقصان يوم), the period is fixed at 29 days without choice.
The difference is subtle but legally significant.
5
Derive the final ruling
In the first case, the oath covers 29 days (30 minus 1 chosen day); in the second, it covers exactly 29 days with no choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يكلمك شهراً إلا يوماً ولا نية له فله أن يختار أي يوم شاء، ولو قال شهراً إلا نقصان يوم فهو على تسعة وعشرين يوماً وهو مخالف للأول ا هـ .
He does not speak to you for a month except a day, and he has no intention, then he may choose whichever day he wishes. And if he said 'a month except a day's deficiency', then it is for twenty-nine days, and this contradicts the first.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This is a brief ruling on an oath that excludes a day from a month. If the swearer says 'I will not speak to you for a month except a day', without specifying which day, he has the right to choose any day to be the exception. This gives him flexibility. However, if he says 'except a day's deficiency' (i.e., except a deficit of a day), the calculation changes: instead of a full month (30 days) minus one day (29 days), it means the oath is only for 29 days, but the wording is different and the author notes this contradicts the first case.

Key Terms
إلا (illa): Except; used to make an exception from the oath.
نقصان يوم (nuqsan yawm): Deficiency of a day; meaning the period is 29 days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not speak to you for a month except a day' without specifying the day, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The oath is void.
B) The oath is for 30 days minus one day, and he must choose the excepted day.
C) The oath is for 29 days, and he cannot speak on any day.
D) The oath is for a full month.

Key Takeaway
In a Hanafi oath with an unspecified exception, the swearer chooses the excepted day; a different wording like 'except a day's deficiency' fixes the period.

Quran Recitation and Tasbih in an Oath of Silence
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether Quran/tasbih breaks an oath of silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether reciting Quran or tasbih breaks an oath of silence, identify the intra-madhhab dispute, and apply the principle of custom (urf) in oath interpretation.

Core Idea
Reciting Quran or tasbih does not break an oath not to speak because custom excludes them from 'speech' (kalam) in oath contexts.

When a person swears not to speak, what counts as 'speech'? This lesson explores the Hanafi perspective, which gives significant weight to custom (urf). The source text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses whether reciting Quran or tasbih breaks such an oath, revealing a nuanced intra-madhhab difference between al-Quduri and Khwarzadeh, and the preferred fatwa variations based on language and context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The passage begins with the general rule: if someone recites Quran or says tasbih after swearing not to speak, the oath is not broken (la yahnath). The reason given: such a person is not called a 'speaker' customarily (urf) or legally (shar'an).
Note the dual basis: urf and shariah.
2
Locate the intra-madhhab dispute
The text reveals a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school: al-Quduri held that recitation outside prayer does break the oath, while Khwarzadeh said it does not. Identify their respective proofs: al-Quduri relied on the literal meaning of speech outside worship, Khwarzadeh on custom and legal definition.
This is an intra-ikhtilaf, not between madhabs.
3
Analyze the role of custom (urf)
The text emphasizes that 'oaths are built on custom' (mabna al-ayman ala al-urf). Later custom treats tasbih and Quran as not speech. Even a person who recites all day is not said to have 'spoken'. This custom overrides literal definitions in oath interpretation.
Urf is a key principle in Hanafi usul for ambiguous terms.
4
Examine the fatwa variations
The text mentions later fatwa differences: in Fath al-Qadir, no distinction between Arabic/Persian; in al-Waqi'at, a distinction based on language and context. The author suggests 'giving fatwa according to the apparent madhhab (al-Quduri's distinction) is better.' Trace how the same principle (urf) leads to different conclusions when the custom varies.
This shows the evolution of fatwa within the school.
5
Apply the principle to a related example
The passage ends with an example: a man swears 'whenever I speak good words, you are divorced.' If he says four tasbih phrases separately, divorce occurs once; if said without pause, three times. This illustrates that tasbih is still considered speech in a different context (good words are a subset of speech), showing that the classification depends on the oath's wording and custom.
Contrast this with the oath of silence case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لا يتكلم فقرأ القرآن أو سبح لم يحنث لأنه لا يسمى متكلماً عادة وشرعاً. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان في الصلاة أو خارجها، فإن كان في الصلاة فهو متفق عليه، وإن كان خارجها فاختار القدوري الحنث، واختار خواهر زاده عدمه لما ذكرنا. وفي فتح القدير أنه اختير للفتوى من غير تفصيل بين عقد اليمين بالعربية أو بالفارسية وإن كان ظاهر المذهب التفصيل الذي ذكره القدوري لأن مبنى الأيمان على العرف وفي العرف المتأخر لا . التسبيح والقرآن كلاماً. حتى إنه يقال لمن يسبح طول يومه أو يقرأ لم يتكلم اليوم بكلمة ا هـ . لكن في الواقعات المختار للفتوى أن اليمين إذا كانت بالعربية لم يحنث بالقراءة في ويحنث بالقراءة خارجها، وإن كانت بالفارسية لا يحنث مطلقاً ا.هـ. فقد اختلفت الفتوى والإفتاء بظاهر المذهب أولى. وفي التهذيب للقلانسي: الكلام في الحقيقة مفهوم ينافي الخرس والسكوت وهو اختيار محققي أهل السنة لكن في العرف صوت مقطوع مفهوم يخرج من الفم ولا تدخل فيه القراءة والتسبيح في الصلاة في عرفهم وفي عرفنا لا تدخل في غير الصلاة أيضاً، وكذا قراءة الكتب ظاهراً وباطناً في عرفنا ا.هـ. فأفاد أنه لا يحنث إذا قرأ كتاباً أي كتاب كان قيد بكونه حلف أنه لا يتكلم لأنه لو قال كلما تكلمت كلاماً حسناً فأنت طالق ثم قال سبحان الله والحمد لله ولا إله إلا الله والله أكبر طلقت واحدة، ولو قال سبحان الله الحمد لله لا إله إلا الله الله أكبر طلقت ثلاثاً؛ كذا في الظهيرية.
His statement: ('He does not speak, and he recites the Quran or says tasbih, he does not break the oath' because he is not called a speaker customarily and legally. It is absolute, so it includes whether he is in prayer or outside it. If in prayer, it is agreed upon. If outside prayer, al-Quduri chose breaking the oath, and Khwarzadeh chose not breaking due to what we mentioned. In Fath al-Qadir, it is chosen for fatwa without distinction between the oath being in Arabic or Persian, although the apparent madhhab is the distinction mentioned by al-Quduri, because oaths are based on custom, and in later custom, tasbih and Quran are not considered speech. So much so that it is said of one who glorifies all day or recites, 'He did not speak today at all.' But in al-Waqi'at, the chosen for fatwa is that if the oath is in Arabic, he does not break by recitation inside prayer, but breaks outside; if in Persian, he does not break at all. So the fatwa differed, and giving fatwa according to the apparent madhhab is better. In al-Tahdhib of al-Qalanisi: Speech in reality is a concept contrary to muteness and silence, and this is the choice of the verifiers of Ahl al-Sunnah. But customarily, it is a broken sound with meaning coming out of the mouth, and Quran recitation and tasbih in prayer are not included in their custom, and in our custom they are not included outside prayer either. Likewise, reading books outwardly and inwardly in our custom. So it indicates that he does not break the oath if he reads a book, any book, provided he swore not to speak. Because if he said 'Whenever I speak good words, you are divorced,' then he says 'Subhan Allah, Alhamdulillah, La ilaha illa Allah, Allahu Akbar,' she is divorced once. And if he says 'Subhan Allah Alhamdulillah La ilaha illa Allah Allahu Akbar,' she is divorced three times; as in al-Zahiriyyah.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage addresses a key question: if someone swears not to speak, and then recites Quran or says tasbih (glorification of Allah), does that break the oath? The general rule is that these acts do not break the oath because customarily and legally they are not considered 'speech' (kalam) in the context of oaths. The author notes that this ruling is agreed upon if the recitation occurs during prayer, but there is a difference of opinion when it occurs outside prayer: al-Quduri held that it does break the oath, while Khwarzadeh held it does not. The author mentions that the later fatwa from Fath al-Qadir chooses the general rule without distinction between Arabic and Persian, but al-Waqi'at distinguishes: if the oath was in Arabic, recitation outside prayer breaks the oath; if in Persian, never. The author suggests that following the apparent madhhab (the distinction of al-Quduri) is preferable. The passage also cites al-Tahdhib defining speech as meaningful sound, and notes that in later custom, reading books is also not considered speech. A related example: if one swears 'whenever I speak good words, you are divorced,' and then says the four phrases of tasbih separately, she is divorced once; if he says them all without pausing, she is divorced three times—this illustrates how the rulings apply in practice.

Key Terms
كلام (kalam): Speech; in oath contexts, excludes Quran recitation and tasbih due to custom and legal definition; defined by al-Qalanisi as 'broken sound with meaning coming from the mouth' but custom excludes dhikr.
عرف (urf): Custom; the common understanding of people regarding what constitutes speech; oaths are built on custom (mabna al-ayman ala al-urf).
متكلم (mutakallim): One who speaks; a person is not customarily called a speaker if he recites Quran or tasbih.
حنث (hanath): Breaking or violating an oath, triggering kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is true regarding reciting Quran outside prayer after an oath not to speak?
A) It always breaks the oath.
B) It never breaks the oath.
C) Al-Quduri said it breaks the oath; Khwarzadeh said it does not.
D) It breaks the oath only if recited in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, custom (urf) determines the meaning of 'speech,' so Quran recitation and tasbih are not considered speech unless the oath explicitly includes them, with an intra-madhhab dispute on recitation outside prayer.
