Swearing Not to Read: Intention and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Reading a Book
Intra-Hanafi dispute on reading a book; intention in Quran oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the role of intention in oaths regarding Quran recitation and apply the ruling of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on reading a book.

Core Idea
Intention determines whether a common phrase like 'Bismillah' breaks an oath not to read Quran; for a book, even half a line may break it per Abu Yusuf.

Oaths about reading can be nuanced. The Hanafi school distinguishes between reading for understanding (books) and reading as recitation (Quran). This lesson explores how a simple phrase like 'Bismillah' can either be a Quranic verse or a general invocation depending on intention, and how the two great Hanafi authorities, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, differ on whether reading half a line of a book violates an oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenarios
List the three scenarios in the passage: oath not to read Quran, oath not to read a specific surah, and oath not to read a specific book.
Focus on the third scenario for the dispute.
2
Analyze the Bismillah case
The phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' appears in Quran (Surat al-Naml) and is also used as a general invocation. Does it break an oath not to read Quran? The answer hinges on intention (niyyah). If intended as Quran recitation, the oath is broken; otherwise not.
This demonstrates that common expressions can be ambiguous in oaths.
3
Compare the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf equates reading a book with reading the Quran: any reading (even a single line) breaks the oath. Muhammad differentiates: the purpose of reading a book is understanding, which only occurs with a full line; thus half a line does not break the oath.
Understand the underlying rationale (talil) for each view.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states 'the fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf'. Therefore, in practice, any reading of a book (even half a line?) Actually, Abu Yusuf's view means reading any line breaks the oath. For half a line, the text says Muhammad excludes it, but since the fatwa is with Abu Yusuf, even a line (or less?) applies. However, the passage specifically says for Muhammad, a line breaks, half does not; for Abu Yusuf, any reading breaks.
The practical consequence: in fatwa, even a half-line reading would break the oath according to Abu Yusuf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الواقعات : حلف لا يقرأ القرآن اليوم فقرأ في الصلاة أو خارجها يحنث لأنه قرأ القرآن، وإذا الله الرحمن الرحيم فإذا نوى ما في سورة النمل يحنث وإن نوى غير ما في سورة النمل أو لا نية له لم يحنث لأنهم لا يريدون به قراءة القرآن ولو حلف لا يقرأ سورة من القرآن فنظر فيها حتى إذا أتى إلى آخرها لا يحنث بالاتفاق. أبو يوسف سوى بين ما إذا حلف لا يقرأ كتاب فلان ومحمد فرق فقال : المقصود من قراءة كتاب فلان فهم ما فيه وقد حصل، أما المقصود من قراءة القرآن عين القراءة إذ الحكم متعلق به، ثم عند محمد في قوله لا يقرأ كتاب فلان إذا قرأ سطراً حنث وبنصف السطر لا لأن نصف السطر لا يكون مفهوم المعنى غالباً والفتوى على قول أبي يوسف ا هـ .
And in al-Waqi'at: He swore not to recite the Quran today, and he recited inside prayer or outside it, he breaks the oath because he recited the Quran. And if he said 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim', if he intended what is in Surat al-Naml, he breaks the oath; and if he intended other than what is in Surat al-Naml or had no intention, he does not break the oath, because they do not intend Quran recitation by it. And if he swore not to recite a surah of the Quran, and he looked at it until he reached its end, he does not break the oath by agreement. Abu Yusuf equated between if he swore not to read so-and-so's book, and Muhammad differentiated, saying: The purpose of reading so-and-so's book is to understand its content, and that is achieved; whereas the purpose of reading the Quran is the recitation itself, since the ruling is attached to it. Then, according to Muhammad, in his statement 'he does not read so-and-so's book', if he read one line, he breaks the oath; by half a line, no, because half a line is not usually meaningful. And the fatwa is according to the statement of Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage deals with oaths specifically about reading (qira'ah). The first scenario: someone swears not to read the Quran today, and then recites any part of the Quran, including in prayer, he breaks the oath because it is Quran recitation. However, a nuance arises with the phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim': this phrase occurs in the Quran (at the start of Surat al-Naml) but is also commonly said before any action. If the person intended it as the Quranic verse from Surat al-Naml, he breaks the oath; if he intended it as a general invocation or had no specific intention, he does not break the oath. The reasoning is that people do not usually intend Quran recitation by it. Another scenario: if one swears not to read a particular surah and merely looks at it from beginning to end without reciting aloud, he does not break the oath by consensus. Finally, there is a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding swearing not to read a book (like a person's book). Abu Yusuf holds that reading even a line breaks the oath, whereas Muhammad distinguishes: reading a book aims at understanding, so if one reads a line, the purpose is achieved, but half a line does not give complete meaning. The fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf, meaning even a line counts as reading.

Key Terms
قراءة (qira'ah): Recitation or reading; in the context of oaths, reciting the Quran is considered a specific act.
نية (niyyah): Intention; it determines whether a phrase like 'Bismillah' is considered Quran recitation for oath purposes.
سطر (satr): Line of text; a full line is considered meaningful, half a line is not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He does not break his oath.

D) The oath is void.

Key Takeaway
Intention governs ambiguous expressions in oaths, and within the Hanafi school, the operative view of Abu Yusuf holds that any reading of a book, even half a line, breaks the oath.

Conditional Divorce on 'The Day I Speak' Includes Night
Ruling on the scope of 'day' in a conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a conditional divorce using 'al-yawm' (the day) includes the night, and identify the operative view (mufta bihi) among the school's positions.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'the day' means the full 24-hour period including night.

This lesson examines a specific conditional divorce formulation: when a husband says 'On the day I speak to so-and-so, my wife is divorced,' does the condition only apply during daylight hours or the entire 24-hour period? The Hanafi school's later authorities (al-jadidayn) settled this by ruling that 'the day' includes both day and night, so speaking at any time triggers the divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its wording
The husband says: 'The day I speak to so-and-so, my wife is divorced.' The key term is 'al-yawm' (the day).
The condition is tied to the act of speaking, not just the daytime.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text mentions 'al-jadidayn' (the two new positions) of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, indicating a difference from the earlier view (al-qadim) of Abu Hanifa. The jadid view holds that 'al-yawm' includes the night; the qadim view may have excluded it.
Al-jadidayn refers to the later, relied-upon positions within the school.
3
State the operative ruling
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon position), 'the day' in this condition encompasses both night and day. If the husband speaks at any time within the 24-hour period, he breaks the oath and the divorce takes effect.
This interpretation is consistent with the Hanafi principle in oaths that ordinary language is given full scope unless restricted by context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (يوم أكلم فلاناً فعلى الجديدين يوم أكلم فلاناً فامرأته طالق فهو على الليل والنهار فإن كلمه ليلا أو نهاراً حنث لأن
His statement: ('The day I speak to so-and-so' — according to the two new positions — 'The day I speak to so-and-so, my wife is divorced,' then it applies to the night and the day. For if he speaks to him at night or during the day, he breaks the oath, because...

Commentary
This short passage presents a ruling regarding a conditional divorce: if a man says 'On the day I speak to so-and-so, my wife is divorced,' the condition is fulfilled whether he speaks during the day or at night. The phrase 'the day' (al-yawm) here is interpreted to encompass the entire 24-hour period, including the night. The term 'al-jadidayn' (the two new positions) likely refers to the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) as opposed to the earlier position (al-qadim) of Abu Hanifa? In Hanafi terminology, al-jadid refers to the later positions of the two companions. The text explains that if he speaks at any time, whether day or night, the divorce occurs because the condition is 'the day I speak', which includes the night as part of the same day.

Key Terms
جديدين (jadidayn): The two new positions; reference to the later views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) within the Hanafi school.
يوم (yawm): Day; in this condition, it is interpreted as the entire 24-hour period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Defining Speech in Oaths: Reciting Quran and Tasbih
Defines speech in oaths based on custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether Quran recitation breaks an oath not to speak, and distinguish the positions of Quduri and Khawharzadeh.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, Quran recitation and tasbih are not considered speech by custom, so they do not break an oath not to speak.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explores a key nuance in Hanafi oath jurisprudence: if someone swears not to speak and then recites Quran or glorifies Allah, does that break the oath? The answer hinges on the definition of 'speech' (kalam), which the Hanafis determine by custom (urf). The passage also reveals an intra-madhhab disagreement between al-Quduri and Khawharzadeh, and discusses related cases like reading a book.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and its scope
The text begins with the general ruling: if a person swears not to speak and then recites Quran or tasbih, the oath is not broken. This applies both inside and outside prayer, though later distinctions are noted.
The ruling is general because the person is not called a speaker customarily or legally.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For outside prayer, al-Quduri holds that the oath is broken, while Khawharzadeh disagrees. The madhhab's apparent position (zahir al-madhhab) distinguishes based on the language of the oath: Arabic vs. Persian.
This ikhtilaf illustrates how Hanafi jurists apply custom to oath interpretation.
3
Understand the role of custom (urf)
The later custom (urf muta'akhkhir) does not consider tasbih or Quran recitation as speech. This is so strong that someone who glorifies all day is said not to have spoken a word.
Custom changes over time, and the Hanafi school adapts rulings accordingly.
4
Distinguish related cases: swearing not to recite Quran vs. swearing not to read a book
If the oath is specifically not to recite Quran, then any recitation (in or out of prayer) breaks it. For reading a book, Abu Yusuf says any reading (even a line) breaks the oath, while Muhammad requires meaningful content. The fatwa follows Abu Yusuf.
These distinctions show that the subject of the oath matters: the act of recitation vs. understanding content.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
For swearing not to speak, the relied-upon position is that Quran recitation and tasbih do not break the oath, based on custom. For reading a book, the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view that reading a line breaks it.
The tarjih authority includes later fatwa collections and the principle that oaths follow custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لا يتكلم فقرأ القرآن أو سبح لا يحنث) لأنه لا يسمى متكلماً عادة وشرعاً. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان في الصلاة أو خارجها، فإن كان في الصلاة فهو متفق عليه، وإن كان خارجها فاختار القدوري الحنث، واختار خواهر زاده عدمه لما ذكرنا. وفي فتح القدير أنه اختير للفتوى من غير تفصيل بين عقد اليمين بالعربية أو بالفارسية وإن كان ظاهر المذهب التفصيل الذي ذكره القدوري لأن مبنى الأيمان على العرف وفي العرف المتأخر لا يسمى التسبيح والقرآن كلاماً. حتى إنه يقال لمن يسبح طول يومه أو يقرأ لم يتكلم اليوم بكلمة ا هـ . لكن في الواقعات المختار للفتوى أن اليمين إذا كانت بالعربية لم يحنث بالقراءة في الصلاة ويحنث بالقراءة خارجها، وإن كانت بالفارسية لا يحنث مطلقاً ا.هـ. فقد اختلفت الفتوى والإفتاء بظاهر المذهب أولى. وفي التهذيب للقلانسي: الكلام في الحقيقة مفهوم ينافي الخرس والسكوت وهو اختيار محققي أهل السنة لكن في العرف صوت مقطوع مفهوم يخرج من الفم ولا تدخل فيه القراءة والتسبيح في الصلاة في عرفهم وفي عرفنا لا تدخل في غير الصلاة أيضاً، وكذا قراءة الكتب ظاهراً وباطناً في عرفنا ا.هـ. فأفاد أنه لا يحنث إذا قرأ كتاباً أي كتاب كان. قيد بكونه حلف أنه لا يتكلم لأنه لو قال كلما تكلمت كلاماً حسناً فأنت طالق ثم قال سبحان الله والحمد لله ولا إله إلا الله والله أكبر طلقت واحدة، ولو قال سبحان الله الحمد لله لا إله إلا الله الله أكبر طلقت ثلاثاً؛ كذا في الظهيرية. وفي الواقعات: حلف لا يقرأ القرآن اليوم فقرأ في الصلاة أو خارجها يحنث لأنه قرأ القرآن، وإذا قال بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم فإذا نوى ما في سورة النمل يحنث وإن نوى غير ما في سورة النمل أو لا نية له لم يحنث لأنهم لا يريدون به قراءة القرآن. ولو حلف لا يقرأ سورة من القرآن فنظر فيها حتى إذا أتى إلى آخرها لا يحنث بالاتفاق. أبو يوسف سوى بين ما إذا حلف لا يقرأ كتاب فلان ومحمد فرق فقال: المقصود من قراءة كتاب فلان فهم ما فيه وقد حصل، أما المقصود من قراءة القرآن عين القراءة إذ الحكم متعلق به. ثم عند محمد في قوله لا يقرأ كتاب فلان إذا قرأ سطراً حنث وبنصف السطر لا لأن نصف السطر لا يكون مفهوم المعنى غالباً والفتوى على قول أبي يوسف ا هـ .
He said: (If he swears not to speak, and he recites Quran or glorifies Allah, he does not break his oath) because he is not called a speaker customarily and legally. It is general, so it includes whether he is in prayer or outside it. If he is in prayer, it is agreed upon. If outside prayer, al-Quduri chose breaking of the oath, while Khawharzadeh chose non-breaking for the reason we mentioned. In Fath al-Qadir, it is chosen for fatwa without distinction between whether the oath was made in Arabic or Persian, although the apparent position of the school is the detail mentioned by al-Quduri because oaths are based on custom, and in later custom, tasbih and Quran recitation are not considered speech. So much so that one who glorifies all day or recites is said not to have spoken a word. But in al-Waqi'at, the chosen for fatwa is that if the oath is in Arabic, he does not break by recitation in prayer but breaks by recitation outside it; if in Persian, he does not break absolutely. So fatwa differed, and giving fatwa by the apparent position of the school is better. In al-Tahdhib by al-Qalanisi: Speech in reality is a meaning that contradicts being mute and silent, and this is the choice of the verifying scholars of Ahl al-Sunna; but in custom, it is a cut-off sound with meaning that comes out of the mouth, and Quran recitation and tasbih in prayer are not included in their custom, and in our custom they are not included outside prayer either, similarly reading books apparent and inner in our custom. So it indicates that he does not break if he reads a book, any book. It is restricted by the case that he swore not to speak, because if he said, 'Whenever I speak good speech, you are divorced,' then he said 'SubhanAllah, Alhamdulillah, La ilaha illa Allah, Allahu Akbar,' she is divorced once; if he said 'SubhanAllah Alhamdulillah La ilaha illa Allah Allahu Akbar,' she is divorced three times; thus in al-Zahiriyya. And in al-Waqi'at: He swore not to recite Quran today, and he recited in prayer or outside it, he breaks because he recited Quran. If he said 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim,' if he intended what is in Surah al-Naml, he breaks; if he intended other than what is in Surah al-Naml or had no intention, he does not break because they do not intend by it Quran recitation. If he swore not to recite a surah from the Quran and he looked into it until he reached its end, he does not break by agreement. Abu Yusuf made no distinction between swearing not to read the book of so-and-so and [reading Quran], while Muhammad distinguished: The purpose in reading the book of so-and-so is to understand its content, and that has been achieved; as for the purpose of reading Quran, it is the very recitation since the ruling is attached to it. Then according to Muhammad, if he says 'I will not read the book of so-and-so,' if he reads a line, he breaks; if half a line, no, because half a line is not usually meaningful. And the fatwa is on the statement of Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal analysis of whether reciting Quran or tasbih constitutes 'speaking' in the context of an oath not to speak. The ruling: a person who swears not to speak and then recites Quran or glorifies Allah does not break his oath, because he is not considered a speaker by custom or by shari'a. The commentator (author of al-Bahr al-Raiq) expands on the ruling, noting that this applies both inside and outside prayer, though there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Quduri rules that outside prayer one breaks the oath, while Khawharzadeh says no. In Fath al-Qadir, the fatwa is given without distinction between Arabic or Persian oath, though the madhhab's apparent position distinguishes. The later custom (urf muta'akhkhir) does not consider tasbih and Quran as speech. The passage also discusses the case of swearing not to recite Quran, where reading in or out of prayer breaks the oath because the act of recitation is the object. For books, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ: Abu Yusuf says reading any part (even a line) breaks the oath, while Muhammad distinguishes based on whether meaningful understanding is achieved. The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view. The commentary uses the principle that oaths are based on custom (urf). This is a classic example of how Hanafi fiqh adapts rulings to changing customary definitions.

Key Terms
الكلام (al-kalam): In the context of oaths, speech is defined by custom as a meaningful sound coming from the mouth; Quran recitation and tasbih are excluded by custom.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah, the breaking of which may incur expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because it is speaking

C) Yes, only if he recites loudly

Key Takeaway
Hanafi oaths are interpreted by custom; reciting Quran or tasbih does not break an oath not to speak.

Oath Expressions with 'Day' and 'Night' in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on oath phrased as 'the day I speak to X' and its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for determining whether an oath phrased with 'day' or 'night' includes the opposite period, and identify the effect of the swearer's intention.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'day' in an oath about a non-extending act means absolute time, but phrasing and intention can change the scope.

This passage explores how the Hanafi school interprets the words 'day' and 'night' in oaths. While 'day' normally means the daylight period, when coupled with an act like speaking that does not extend, it is understood as a full 24-hour period. However, the inclusion of intervening nights depends on how the days are phrased—separated or joined. The passage also addresses the effect of the swearer's intention and the unique treatment of 'night' in oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rule for 'day' with a non-extending act
The passage states that when 'day' is linked to an act that does not extend (like speaking), it means absolute time (24 hours).
The Quranic verse (al-Anfal 8:16) is cited as evidence.
2
Analyze the effect of negation particles
Compare 'today nor tomorrow' (negation repeated) vs 'today and tomorrow' (conjunction). The former excludes the night; the latter includes it.
The difference arises because 'nor' separates each time individually.
3
Examine the oath for multiple days
An oath for 'two days' includes the intervening night. The dispute about 'a day and not two days' (seen as three days vs two days) is mentioned.
There is an intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and an alternative view in al-Jami'.
4
Understand the ruling on 'night'
The word 'night' is literal (the period of darkness). An oath for 'a night' runs from that hour to the same hour the next night, including the intervening day.
If one swears 'tonight' at night, the oath ends at dawn.
5
Apply the rule of intention
If the swearer intended only the daytime when saying 'day', he is believed religiously and (according to most) judicially, because the literal meaning includes daytime.
Abu Yusuf is reported to hold that he is not believed judicially.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (يوم أكلم فلاناً فعلى الجديدين) يوم أكلم فلاناً فامرأته طالق فهو على الليل والنهار فإن كلمه ليلا أو نهاراً حنث لأن اسم اليوم إذا قرن بفعل لا يمتد يراد به مطلق الوقت قال تعالى {ومن يولهم يومئذ دبره} [الأنفال: 16] والكلام لا يمتد وقد تقدم تحقيقه في فصل إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان. قيد بقوله يوم أكلم لأنه لو قال والله لا أكلمك اليوم ولا غداً فاليمين على بقية اليوم وعلى غد، ولا تدخل الليلة التي بينهما في اليمين لأنه أفرد كل واحد من الوقتين بحرف النفي فيصير كل واحد منهما منفياً على الإفراد أصله قوله تعالى {فلا رفث ولا فسوق ولا جدال في الحج} [البقرة: 197] ولو قال والله لا أكلمك اليوم وغداً دخلت الليلة التي بين اليوم والغد في يمينه لأنه ها هنا جمع بين الوقت الثاني وبين الأول بحرف الجمع وهي الواو فصار وقتاً واحداً فدخلت الليلة المتخللة، ولو حلف لا يكلمه يومين تدخل فيه الليلة، وسواء كان قبل طلوع الفجر أو بعده، وكذلك الجواب في الليل. ولو قال والله لا أكلمه يوماً ولا يومين فهو كقوله ثلاثة أيام في قول أبي يوسف ومحمد حتى لو كلمه في اليوم الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث يحنث . وذكر محمد في الجامع أنه على يومين حتى لو كلمه في اليوم الأول أو الثاني يحنث، وإن كلمه في اليوم الثالث لا يحنث كذا في البدائع. قوله: (فإن نوى النهار صدق) لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه وهو مستعمل فيه أيضاً. أطلق في تصديقه فشمل الديانة والقضاء، وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يصدق قضاء. قوله: ( وليلة أكلمه على الليل لأنه حقيقة في سواد الليل كالنهار للبياض خاصة ولم يجىء استعماله في مطلق الوقت بخلاف اليوم، وما ورد في أشعار بعض من إطلاقها على مطلق الوقت فإنما هو في صيغة الجمع وكلامنا في المفرد، وقدمنا أنه لو حلف لا يكلمه ليلة فاليمين من تلك الساعة إلى أن يجيء مثلها من الليلة المستقبلة فيدخل النهار الذي بينهما في ذلك، وإذا كان بالليل وقال لا أكلمه الليلة فإذا طلع الفجر سقطت.
He said: (The day I speak to so-and-so is on two days?? Actually 'al-jadidayn'?) The day I speak to so-and-so, my wife is divorced, so it applies to night and day. If he speaks to him by night or by day, he breaks his oath, because the name 'day' when coupled with an act that does not extend, it means absolute time. Allah said {And whoever turns his back to them on that day} [al-Anfal: 16]. Speech does not extend. It has been discussed earlier in the section on adding divorce to time. He restricted by saying 'the day I speak' because if he said 'By Allah, I will not speak to you today nor tomorrow,' the oath is on the remainder of today and on tomorrow, and the night between them is not included in the oath, because he separated each of the two times with the negation particle, so each becomes negated individually; its origin is Allah's statement {So no sexual intercourse, no sin, no argument in Hajj} [al-Baqarah: 197]. If he said 'By Allah, I will not speak to you today and tomorrow,' the night between today and tomorrow enters his oath, because he combined the second time with the first with the conjunction 'and', so it becomes one time, so the intervening night enters. If he swears not to speak to him for two days, the night enters, whether it is before dawn or after, and similarly the answer for the night. If he says 'By Allah, I will not speak to him for a day and not two days,' it is like saying three days according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, so if he speaks to him on the first, second, or third day, he breaks. Muhammad mentioned in al-Jami' that it is for two days, so if he speaks on the first or second day he breaks, but if on the third day, he does not break; thus in al-Bada'i. He said: (If he intended the daytime, he is believed) because he intended the literal meaning of his word, which is also used. It is absolute in his being believed, so it includes (religious belief) and judiciary. From Abu Yusuf that he is not believed judicially. He said: (And the night I speak to him is for the night) because it is literally used for the blackness of night, just as daytime is specifically for whiteness; it is not used for absolute time unlike 'day'. What is reported in some poetry of its use for absolute time is only in the plural form, and our discussion is in the singular. It was mentioned earlier that if he swore not to speak to him for a night, the oath is from that hour until a similar hour of the coming night, so the intervening day enters. If it was at night and he said 'I will not speak to him tonight,' when dawn breaks, it ceases.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings related to oaths involving time expressions like 'day' and 'night'. The main point: when someone says 'the day I speak to so-and-so, my wife is divorced,' it applies to both night and day—anytime he speaks, he breaks the oath. 'Day' here is used to indicate absolute time, not just daylight. The commentator explains the reasoning by citing Quranic usage. Then he compares different phrasings: 'today nor tomorrow' excludes the night between, while 'today and tomorrow' includes it. Swearing for 'two days' includes the night. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: when saying 'a day and not two days' (i.e., 'I will not speak for a day and two days'? see text), Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it equals three days, while Muhammad in al-Jami' says it equals two days. Then the passage discusses intention: if the swearer intends only the daytime when he said 'day', he is believed (in religious matters and according to some also judicially). The word 'night' is more specific and does not mean absolute time. The practical outcome: if he swears not to speak for a night, the oath lasts from that hour to the same hour the next night, including the intervening day. If he says 'tonight' at night, it ends at dawn. These nuances affect when the oath is broken and the need for expiation.

Key Terms
اليوم (al-yawm): In oaths, 'day' when coupled with an act that does not extend means absolute time, covering both day and night.
الليلة (al-laylah): Literally the period of darkness, used specifically not for absolute time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man swears, 'I will not speak to Zaid today and tomorrow,' and he speaks during the night between those two days, what is the ruling?
A) He does not break his oath because night is not included.
B) He breaks his oath because the night is included.
C) He only breaks if he speaks after midnight.
D) The oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets 'day' in oaths contextually; inclusion of the night depends on phrasing and whether the related act extends over time.

Oath with Condition or Exception: Until Zaid Comes
Ruling on oath with condition or limit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on an oath with a limit (ghayah) and determine whether breaking occurs based on the timing of the action.

Core Idea
An oath tied to a limit binds before the limit; speaking after the limit does not break the oath.

When someone swears an oath that includes a phrase like 'until Zaid comes' or 'unless Zaid comes,' the key is whether the limit has been reached. Before the limit, the oath is active; after, it expires. This lesson explains the Hanafi rule for such oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its wording
Look for the exact phrase used. Does it contain 'hata' (until) or 'illa an' (unless)? These are the limit words.
2
Determine the limit (ghayah)
The limit is a future event that marks the end of the oath. For example, 'until Zaid comes' means the limit is Zaid's arrival.
3
Apply the rule: before vs after the limit
Before the limit, the oath is active. If the action occurs before the limit, the oath is broken (hanth). After the limit, the oath expires, so no hanth.
4
Distinguish between 'hata' and 'illa an'
'Hata' clearly indicates a limit. 'Illa an' is originally for exception but is used metaphorically for condition/limit in this context. Both function similarly: the ruling before and after differs.
5
Contrast with direct conditions (like divorce)
In divorce, saying 'You are divorced unless so-and-so comes' means the condition is fulfilled when that person comes, leading to divorce. In oaths, the limit ends the oath. The wording determines the effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (إن كلمته إلا أن يقدم زيد أو حتى أو إلا أن يأذن أو حتى فكذا فكلم قبل قدومه أو إذنه حنث وبعدهما لا) أي وإن كلمه بعد القدوم أو الإذن لا يحنث لأنه غاية واليمين باقية قبل الغاية ومنتهية بعدها فلا يحنث بالكلام بعد انتهاء اليمين أما «حتى» فكونها للغاية ظاهر، وأما إلا أن فالأصل فيها أن للاستثناء وتستعار للشرط والغاية إذا تعذر الاستثناء المناسبة بينهما وهو أن حكم ما قبل كل واحد من الاستثناء والشرط والغاية يخالف ما بعده. قيد بالشرط لأنه لو قال أنت طالق إلا أن يقدم فلان فإنه إن قدم فلان لا تطلق، وإن قدم المذكور تطلق.
He said: (If I speak to him except that Zaid comes, or until, or except that he gives permission, or until, so similarly: if he speaks before his coming or his permission, he breaks; after both, no) meaning if he speaks after the coming or permission, he does not break, because it is a limit, and the oath remains before the limit and ends after it, so he does not break by speech after the end of the oath. As for 'until,' it being a limit is apparent. As for 'except that,' its origin is for exception, and it is used metaphorically for condition and limit when exception is not possible; the connection between them is that the ruling of what precedes each of exception, condition, and limit is contrary to what follows. It is restricted by condition because if he said 'You are divorced except that so-and-so comes,' then if so-and-so comes, she is not divorced; if the mentioned one comes, she is divorced.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on an oath that includes a condition or exception using phrases like 'unless Zaid comes' or 'until Zaid comes'. The rule: if the oath is 'I will not speak to him unless Zaid comes' or 'until Zaid comes,' then if he speaks before Zaid arrives, he breaks the oath; if he speaks after Zaid arrives, he does not, because the oath ends once the condition (limit) is met. The particle 'hata' (until) clearly indicates a limit. 'Illa an' (unless) is originally for exception but is used here figuratively for condition and limit. The common feature is that the ruling before the limit/condition is opposite to after. The text also contrasts with a direct condition in divorce: if he says 'You are divorced unless so-and-so comes,' if that person comes, she is not divorced; but if he comes himself, she is divorced (i.e., the condition is fulfilled by the named person's arrival). This shows the importance of wording in oaths and conditions.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghayah): A limit or endpoint of an oath; before the limit the oath is in effect, after it the oath expires.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears, 'I will not speak to Ahmed until Ali comes,' and then speaks after Ali has arrived, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he still swore not to speak
B) No, because the oath ended when Ali came
C) Yes, but only if he speaks within a specific time
D) No, only if he speaks before Ali comes

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath with a limit (ghayah) remains in effect only until the limit is reached; after that, the oath is no longer binding.

Oath Conditioned on 'Until' or 'Unless': Timing of Breach and Effect of Death
Ruling on oaths with 'until' or 'unless' clauses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a condition (shart) and a temporal limit (ghaya) in oaths and state the ruling on breach when the condition is fulfilled or unfulfilled, and when the condition-subject dies.

Core Idea
An oath conditioned on 'until' is breached by premature action; 'unless' in divorce works as a condition, not a time limit.

The Hanafi school pays close attention to the precise wording of oaths and conditional statements. A single word like 'until' (حتى) or 'unless' (إلا أن) can change whether an act is a breach or not, and whether a divorce occurs. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq explains how to interpret such language in oaths and contrasts it with divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its condition
Look at the phrasing: the oath is conditioned on 'until Zaid comes' or 'unless Zaid gives permission'. Determine if the act (speaking) happens before or after the condition is met.
The condition may be a temporal limit (ghaya) or a conditional (shart).
2
Apply the rule for 'until'
If the oath uses 'until' (حتى), the prohibition applies up to that point. Acting before the event is a breach; acting after is not.
The 'until' creates a limit (ghaya) that ends the oath's effect.
3
Consider the death of the condition's subject
If the person on whose action the oath depends dies, the oath is void because fulfilling the condition becomes impossible.
The oath drops (sakat al-halif) entirely.
4
Distinguish 'unless' in divorce from oath
In divorce, 'unless' (إلا أن) functions as a conditional (shart), not a limit. If the condition is not met, divorce occurs. In oaths, it is similar but note the context.
Divorce cannot be time-limited, so 'إلا أن' is always a condition in such statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقدم زيد أو حتى أو إلا أن يأذن أو حتى فكذا فكلم قبل قدومه أو إذنه حنث وبعدها لا وإن مات زيد سقط الحلف . . . قيد بالشرط لأنه لو قال أنت طالق إلا أن يقدم فلان فإنه إن قدم فلان لا تطلق، وإن لم يقدم حتى مات فلان طلقت وهي هنا للشرط كأنه قال إن لم يقدم فلان فأنت طالق. ولا تكون للغاية لأنها إنما تكون لها فيما يحتمل التأقيت والطلاق مما لا يحتمله معنى فتكون فيه للشرط وتمامه في فتح القدير.
That Zaid comes or until or unless he gives permission, or until such and such. So he spoke before his coming or his permission: he breaks the oath. After them: no. And if Zaid dies, the oath drops. . . . Conditioned by the condition because if he said 'You are divorced unless Zaid comes,' then if Zaid comes, she is not divorced; if he does not come until Zaid dies, she is divorced. And here it is for the condition as if he said 'If Zaid does not come, you are divorced.' And it cannot be for the goal because it is only for that in what admits of time limitation, and divorce does not admit of meaning, so it is for the condition. Its completion is in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on oaths that include a condition such as 'until' or 'unless'. The author states that if one swears not to speak or act until a specified event, then acting before that event constitutes a breach (حنث). Acting after the event does not. Furthermore, if the person upon whose action the condition depends dies, the oath becomes void. The author then draws a comparison with a similar construction in divorce: if one says 'You are divorced unless Zaid comes', it functions as a conditional (شرط) not a temporal limit (غاية), because divorce cannot be time-limited. Thus, if Zaid does not come, divorce occurs. This distinction is important for understanding how different legal constructs affect the ruling.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breach of an oath; the legal consequence of doing what one swore not to do.
غاية (ghāyah): A temporal limit or endpoint; used here as the condition 'until' that ends the oath's application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears, 'By Allah, I will not eat until Zaid comes,' and eats before Zaid arrives, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks the oath because he ate before the condition.
B) He does not break the oath because he ate before Zaid came.
C) He breaks the oath only if he ate after Zaid came.
D) The oath is void regardless of when he eats.

Key Takeaway
In oaths, 'until' creates a limit: acting before the limit is a breach; 'unless' functions similarly; death of the condition-subject voids the oath.

Inclusion of Night in Oaths of Days: The Role of Conjunctions
Rulings on whether the interposed night is included in oaths specifying two days or more.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the conjunction 'nor' separates time periods while 'and' unites them, and determine whether the interposed night is included in an oath of days.

Core Idea
The conjunction 'nor' keeps days separate; 'and' joins them into one period including the night.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq analyzes how the wording of an oath affects whether the night between two days is included. The ruling hinges on whether the speaker uses 'nor' (la ... wa la) or 'and' (wa) when specifying the days. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining exactly when an oath begins and ends.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Wording Pattern
Determine whether the oath uses 'nor' (separate negation) or 'and' (conjunction) to connect the time periods.
2
Apply the Linguistic Principle
Recall that repeating 'la' indicates each period is independent (Quranic analogy), while 'waw' joins them into one continuous time.
3
Determine Night Inclusion
If 'nor', the night is excluded; if 'and' or 'two days', the night is included.
4
Consider the Time of Oath
Note that the same rule applies whether the oath is made before dawn or after, and similarly for oaths at night.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيد بقوله يوم أكلم لأنه لو قال والله لا أكلمك اليوم ولا غداً فاليمين على بقية اليوم وعلى غد، ولا تدخل الليلة التي بينهما في اليمين لأنه أفرد كل واحد من الوقتين بحرف النفي فيصير كل واحد منهما منفياً على الإفراد أصله قوله تعالى فلا رفث ولا فسوق ولا جدال في الحج [البقرة : ۱۹۷] ولو قال والله لا أكلمك اليوم وغداً دخلت الليلة التي بين اليوم والغد في يمينه لأنه ها هنا جمع بين الوقت الثاني وبين الأول بحرف الجمع وهي الواو فصار وقتاً واحداً فدخلت الليلة المتخللة، ولو حلف لا يكلمه يومين تدخل فيه الليلة، وسواء كان قبل طلوع الفجر أو بعده، وكذلك الجواب في الليل.
Restricted by his saying 'a day I speak to him' because if he said 'By Allah I will not speak to you today nor tomorrow', the oath applies to the remainder of today and to tomorrow, and the night between them is not included in the oath because he separated each of the two times by the particle of negation, so each becomes negated individually. Its origin is His saying, the Exalted, 'So no sexual relations and no sin and no argument in Hajj' [2:197]. And if he said 'By Allah I will not speak to you today and tomorrow', the night between today and tomorrow enters his oath because here he combined the second time with the first by the particle of conjunction which is 'and', so it became one time, so the intermediate night enters. And if he swore he would not speak to him for two days, the night enters it, whether before dawn or after. And similarly the answer regarding the night.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on how to determine the scope of an oath concerning days when the night intervenes. The author explains that if one says 'I swear I will not speak to you today nor tomorrow', the oath covers the remainder of today and all of tomorrow, but the night between them is excluded. This is because each day is negated separately, as in the Quranic verse 'la rafatha wa la fusuqa wa la jidala fi al-hajj' where each item is individually negated. However, if one says 'today and tomorrow' using 'and' (waw), the two days become one continuous period, so the intermediate night is included. Similarly, if one simply says 'two days', the night is automatically included. The same principle applies if the oath is made at night. This distinction is crucial for determining precisely when an oath is broken.

Key Terms
الواو (al-wāw): The conjunction 'and' that joins two periods into one continuous time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I will not speak to you today nor tomorrow.' When does his oath end?
A) At the end of today.
B) At the end of tomorrow, including the night between.
C) At the end of tomorrow, but the night between is excluded.
D) At sunset of tomorrow.

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of the night between days in an oath depends on whether the speaker uses 'nor' (excluded) or 'and' or a number (included), a distinction rooted in the Quranic style of negation.

Dispute on 'A Day Nor Two Days' in Oaths
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on swearing 'not to speak for a day nor two days' and identify the practical consequence of each view.

Core Idea
An oath of 'a day nor two days' is interpreted as three days by Sahibayn, but a nawadir transmission says two days.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the interpretation of an oath that combines two time periods with 'nor.' The dispute turns on whether the negation covers both terms separately or as a combined set. Understanding both views is crucial for accurate fatwa in oath-related cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath phrase
The phrase is 'I will not speak to him for a day nor two days.' The ambiguity lies in whether 'nor' repeats the negation for the second term or combines them.
2
State the Sahibayn view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Ibn al-Hasan) consider it equivalent to three days. Speaking on the first, second, or third day breaks the oath.
This is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
State the contrary transmission
Muhammad himself in al-Jami' reports that it means only two days. Speaking on the third day does not break the oath.
This is a nawadir transmission.
4
Compare practical implications
Under the Sahibayn view, the third day is included (hanth occurs). Under the other view, the third day is excluded (no hanth).
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn view.
5
Note authority weight
The Sahibayn view is from Zahir al-Riwayah (stronger), while the other is from nawadir (weaker). The school relies on the former.
This determines fatwa preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال والله لا يكلمه يوماً ولا يومين فهو كقوله ثلاثة أيام في قول أبي يوسف ومحمد حتى لو كلمه في اليوم الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث يحنث . وذكر محمد في الجامع أنه على يومين حتى لو كلمه في اليوم الأول أو الثاني يحنث، وإن كلمه في اليوم الثالث لا يحنث كذا في البدائع.
And if he said 'By Allah, I will not speak to him for a day nor two days' then it is like his saying 'three days' in the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, so that if he spoke to him on the first, second, or third day, he breaks the oath. And Muhammad mentioned in al-Jami' that it is upon two days, so that if he spoke to him on the first or second day, he breaks the oath, and if he spoke to him on the third day, he does not break the oath; thus in al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement about the meaning of an oath that says 'I will not speak to him for a day nor two days.' According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani), this is equivalent to swearing for three days: speaking on any of the first three days breaks the oath. However, there is a transmission from Muhammad himself in his book al-Jami' that it means only two days: speaking on the first or second breaks the oath, but the third day is not included. This difference stems from how the conjunction 'nor' operates when repreated. The ruling in al-Bada'i reports both views. The practical consequence is that in a fatwa, one would follow the more precautionary position or the one with stronger support.

Key Terms
البدائع (al-Badā’i‘): A reference to a Hanafi fiqh work (al-Bada'i' al-Sana'i' by al-Kasani).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the transmission from Muhammad in al-Jami', if someone swears 'I will not speak to him for a day nor two days', on which day(s) would speaking break the oath?
A) Only the first day.
B) The first and second days, not the third.
C) The first, second, and third days.
D) Only the second and third days.

Key Takeaway
An oath of 'a day nor two days' is interpreted as three days per the Sahibayn, but a nawadir transmission says two days.

6 min
Signifier vs Condition in Hanafi Oath Rulings
Ruling on speaking on the day of arrival before or after the actual arrival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a signifier (mu'arraf) and a condition (shart) in the context of oaths and apply the ruling that speaking before the defining event breaches the oath while speaking after does not.

Core Idea
In oaths, the event that defines the day is a signifier not a condition: breach occurs before the event, not after.

Oaths often tie an action to a time defined by an event, such as 'the day Zaid arrives'. Hanafi jurists make a subtle but crucial distinction: is the event a condition that limits the oath's duration, or merely a signifier that identifies the day? The answer determines whether speaking after the event still violates the oath. This lesson unpacks that distinction through a classic ruling from al-Muhit al-Burhani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath formula
The oath is: 'By Allah, I will not speak to him on the day on which Zaid arrives.' Note that the phrase 'on the day' defines the time, and 'Zaid arrives' is attached to define which day.
The key is the particle usage – no conditional particle (e.g., 'if' or 'until') is used.
2
Distinguish before vs after the event
If speech occurs on that day but before Zaid arrives, the oath is broken because the condition for breach (speech on that day) is met. If speech occurs after Zaid arrives on the same day, the oath is not broken.
The day is the same; only the relative timing changes.
3
Understand the legal rationale
The arrival is not a condition (shart) but a signifier (mu'arraf) that identifies the day. A condition must precede its effect. Since the condition for breach is speech on that day, and the day is known by the event, the event merely marks the day.
If the event were a condition, the oath would only be in effect until the event, not after.
4
Apply the analogy (qiyas) to divorce
The text analogizes to: 'You are divorced one month before Ramadan.' Ramadan is a signifier, not a condition. Similarly, 'You are divorced one month before Zaid's arrival' – if Zaid arrives before the month ends, she is not divorced because the signifier failed to define the period correctly.
This strengthens the rule that a mu'arraf does not act as a condition.
5
Synthesize the ruling
The mufta bihi position: speaking before arrival is a breach; speaking after arrival is not. The event is a mu'arraf, not a shart.
This ruling is from al-Muhit, a late Hanafi work, and is relied upon in later fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : لو قال والله لا أكلمه في اليوم الذي يقدم فيه فلان فكلمه في اليوم الذي قدم فيه فلان قبل قدومه حنث لأن شرط الحنث كلامه يوم القدوم وقد وجد. وإن كلمه بعد القدوم قالوا يجب أن لا يحنث لأنه لم يجعل القدوم شرطاً لأنه لم يقرن به حرف الشرط ولكنه جعله معرفاً لما هو شرط الحنث وهو الكلام. وإنما يتصور القدوم معرفاً للشرط إذا وجد الشرط قبله، فأما إذا وجد بعده كونه معرفاً يتصور من ضرورة كون الشيء معرفاً تقدم ذلك الشيء عليه كما لو قال لامرأته أنت طالق قبل : شهر رمضان بشهر كان رمضان معرفاً لا شرطاً. كذا لو قال أنت طالق قبل قدوم فلان بشهر إذا قدم فلان قبل تمام الشهر لا تطلق.
And in al-Muhit: If he said, 'By Allah, I will not speak to him on the day on which Zaid arrives,' then he spoke on the day that Zaid arrived before his arrival: he breaks the oath because the condition of breach is his speech on the day of arrival and it occurred. And if he spoke after his arrival, they said he must not break the oath because he did not make arrival a condition; he did not connect a conditional particle to it, but rather made it a signifier (mu'arraf) of what is the condition of breach, which is speech. And the arrival can only be a signifier for the condition if the condition occurs before it. As for if it occurs after it, its being a signifier is conceivable from the necessity of something being a signifier preceding that thing. For example, if he said to his wife, 'You are divorced one month before Ramadan,' Ramadan is a signifier, not a condition. Similarly, if he said, 'You are divorced one month before Zaid's arrival,' if Zaid arrives before completion of the month, she is not divorced.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit al-Burhani clarifies a subtle point: if one swears not to speak on the day that Zaid arrives, and speaks on that day but before Zaid actually arrives, the oath is broken. This is because the condition for breach is speech on that day, and the day has started. However, if one speaks after Zaid's arrival on the same day, the oath is not broken. The reason is that the arrival is not a condition (shart) but a signifier (mu'arraf) that identifies the day. Since the condition (speech) is defined by the day, and the day is known, the arrival merely identifies it. The text draws an analogy with divorce: saying 'you are divorced one month before Ramadan' makes Ramadan a signifier, not a condition. So the ruling depends on whether the oath makes the event a condition or merely a time marker.

Key Terms
معرف (mu‘arrif): A signifier that indicates the time or condition without being the condition itself.
شرط (shart): A condition that must exist for a ruling to apply.
حنث (hanith): Breaking an oath, incurring legal consequences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I will not speak to you on the day Zaid arrives.' He speaks after Zaid arrives that same day. What is the ruling?
A) He breaks the oath because he spoke on that day.
B) He does not break the oath because Zaid's arrival defines the day and the oath ends upon arrival.
C) He breaks the oath only if he speaks before Zaid's arrival.
D) He does not break the oath because arrival is a condition.

Key Takeaway
When an oath uses an event to define a day without a conditional particle, the event is a signifier: speaking before the event breaches, speaking after does not.

Nullification of an Oath When Its Condition Becomes Impossible
Principle that an oath with a condition perishes when the subject of the condition dies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an oath's condition becomes impossible, identify the condition (ghaya), and apply the principle to new scenarios.

Core Idea
An oath tied to a condition perishes when the condition becomes impossible.

Oaths often depend on conditions. But what happens when that condition can never be fulfilled? This lesson explores the Hanafi principle that an oath with a temporal or event-based limit (ghaya) becomes void if that limit is forever lost—such as through death or release from debt. We will trace the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad and note the dissenting view of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its condition
Read the passage and note the examples: an oath not to speak until someone gives permission, or not to leave a creditor until paid. The condition is the ghaya (limit).
The ghaya is like a deadline—when it passes irrecoverably, the oath is void.
2
Determine whether the condition has become impossible
Check if the event or person mentioned in the ghaya can no longer occur (e.g., death, forgiveness of debt).
3
Apply the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
If the ghaya has become impossible, the oath drops (sāqiṭah). The principle: 'If the one swearing makes for his oath a limit and the limit expires, the oath becomes void.'
This is opposed to Abu Yusuf, who holds the oath remains.
4
Extend to similar cases
Analogous cases include oaths with 'as long as' (mā dāma) conditions: if one leaves the place that conditions the oath, the oath ends and does not revive upon return.
This shows the principle is general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن مات زيد سقط الحلف لما في الذخيرة : إذ الأصل أن الحالف إذا جعل ليمينه غاية وفاتت الغاية بطلت اليمين عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد حتى إن من قال لغيره والله لا أكلمك حتى يأذن لي فلان أو قال لغريمه والله لا أفارقك حتى تقضيني حقي فمات فلان قبل الإذن أو برئ من المال فاليمين ساقطة في قولهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف. على هذا لو حلف ليوفين ماله اليوم فأبراه الطالب وعلى هذا تخرج جنس هذه المسائل إذا قال إن فعلت كذا ما دمت ببخارى فكذا فخرج من بخارى ثم رجع وفعل ذلك لا يحنث.
And if Zaid died, the oath drops, as in al-Dhakhirah: because the principle is that if the one swearing makes for his oath a limit and the limit expires, the oath becomes void according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, such that if someone said to another, 'By Allah, I will not speak to you until Zaid gives me permission,' or said to his creditor, 'By Allah, I will not leave you until you pay me my right,' and Zaid died before permission or he became free from the debt, then the oath is dropped in their view, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Upon this, if he swore to fulfill his property today and the claimant discharged him, and upon this issues of this kind emerge if he said, 'If I do such and such as long as I am in Bukhara, then such and such,' and he left Bukhara, then returned and did that, he does not break the oath.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage establishes a key principle: when an oath is made conditional upon an event that later becomes impossible due to death or similar, the oath is void according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. For example, if one swears not to speak until Zaid gives permission, and Zaid dies before giving permission, the oath is nullified. The same applies if a debtor swears not to part from his creditor until the debt is paid, and then the creditor forgives the debt. Abu Yusuf disagrees and holds that the oath remains binding. The author then extends this to cases involving 'as long as' (ma dam) conditions: if one swore not to do something while in Bukhara, then left Bukhara, the oath ends; even if he returns later and does it, he does not breach the original oath.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghāyah): The limit or condition upon which the oath depends; when it ceases, the oath ceases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I will not eat until Zaid returns.' Zaid dies before returning. What is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) The oath remains binding forever.
B) The oath is void because the condition is impossible.
C) The oath must still be observed but expiation is required.
D) The oath becomes a vow without condition.

Key Takeaway
An oath tied to a specific condition (ghaya) becomes void if that condition becomes impossible, according to the dominant Hanafi view.

Oaths with 'Ma Dam' — Permanent Termination Upon Departure
Ruling on oaths using 'ma dam' and permanent termination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths using 'ma dam' (as long as) and apply it to scenarios where the condition ends and later resumes.

Core Idea
An oath with 'as long as' ends forever when the condition ceases; return does not revive it.

In Hanafi fiqh, the word 'ma dam' functions as a temporal limit (ghayah). When the condition attached to the oath terminates, the oath itself terminates permanently. Even if the condition later returns, the oath is not revived. This lesson explores that principle through examples from the classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath phrase
Recognize the phrase 'ma dam' (as long as) in the oath as a ghayah (limit).
2
Determine the trigger
Identify the event that ends the condition — typically departure from a location or change in status.
Departure is the key event.
3
Apply the consequence
The oath terminates permanently upon departure; any later action, even after return, does not breach the oath.
No expiation is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيجب أن يعلم أن كلمة «ما زال» و «ما دام» و «ما كان غاية تنتهي اليمين بها فإذا حلف لا يفعل كذا ما دام ببخارى فخرج تنتهي يمينه بالخروج فإذا عاد عاد واليمين منتهية فإذا فعل ذلك الفعل لا يحنث في يمينه؛ كذا في فتاوى الفضلي وعلى هذا إذا حلف لا يصطاد ما دام فلان في هذه البلدة وفلان أمير هذه البلدة فخرج الأمير إلى بلدة أخرى لأمر فاصطاد الحالف قبل رجوعه أو بعد رجوعه لا يحنث في يمينه لأن اليمين ينتهي بخروج الأمير.
So it must be known that the words 'ma zal' and 'ma dam' and 'ma kan' are a limit by which the oath ends. So if he swore not to do such and such as long as he is in Bukhara, and he left, his oath ends with the departure. So if he returns, he returns and the oath is already terminated; so if he does that action, he does not break his oath. Likewise in Fatawa al-Fadli. And upon this, if he swore not to hunt as long as so-and-so is in this town, and so-and-so is the amir of this town, and the amir left to another town for some matter, then the swearer hunted before his return or after his return, he does not break his oath because the oath ends with the amir's departure.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage explains the legal effect of the phrase 'as long as' (ma dam) in oaths. It is a limit (ghayah); when the condition ends, the oath ends permanently. Even if the person returns to the original situation, the oath is not revived. So if someone swears not to drink alcohol as long as he is in Bukhara, and he leaves Bukhara, the oath terminates. If he later returns to Bukhara and drinks, he does not break that oath because it is already ended. The same applies if the condition is tied to another person's presence: if he swears not to hunt as long as the amir is in the town, and the amir leaves, the oath ends; hunting after that, even after the amir returns, is not a breach. This is based on the principle that a completed limit cannot be revisited.

Key Terms
ما دام (mā dāma): A phrase meaning 'as long as'; it functions as a temporal limit ending the oath when the condition ceases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I will not smoke as long as I am in this house.' He leaves the house, then returns and smokes. What is the ruling?
A) He breaks the oath because he returned and smoked.
B) He does not break the oath because leaving the house ended it.
C) He breaks the oath only if he smoked before leaving.
D) The oath is invalid from the start.

Key Takeaway
An oath using 'ma dam' (as long as) terminates permanently when the condition ends; return does not revive it.

The Particle 'Until' in Oaths: Condition vs. Temporal Identifier
Clarifies the difference between using 'until' as a condition vs. a temporal identifier in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the conditional and temporal uses of 'until' in Hanafi oath rulings and apply the distinction to determine oath violation.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'until' is a condition (shart), not a temporal limit (ghāya), for instantaneous actions like divorce.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq addresses a subtle but critical point in the fiqh of oaths: does the word 'until' (ḥattā) impose a time limit or a condition? The answer determines whether speaking after someone's arrival breaches an oath. The Hanafi school, reasoning from the nature of divorce, treats 'until' as a conditional particle in such contexts. Let's examine the reasoning and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the key term
The passage focuses on the word 'until' (حتى) in oaths. The fundamental ruling is that when used in divorce oaths, it is interpreted as a conditional particle (shart), not a temporal limit (ghāya).
The ruling is based on the nature of divorce—an instantaneous act that cannot be time-bound.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta‘līl)
Why cannot 'until' be a temporal limit for divorce? Because divorce is an instantaneous event; it cannot be stretched over time. Therefore, 'until' must function as a condition.
This reasoning is a form of qiyas: the illa (effective cause) is the impossibility of time-binding.
3
Apply to the first example from al-Muhit
If someone swears not to speak on the day of Zaid's arrival, analyze speaking before vs. after arrival. Speaking before arrival constitutes a violation because the condition (the day) is fulfilled. Speaking after arrival does not break the oath because arrival is merely an identifier (mu‘arrif), not a condition.
The condition is the day, not the arrival itself.
4
Generalize the principle to other cases
The analogy of 'divorce before Ramadan by a month' and 'divorce before someone's arrival by a month' shows that identifiers are not conditions. The arrival or Ramadan merely identifies the time; the condition is the specified period before it.
This generalization helps in similar oath scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب واللبس والكلام لم يقدم حتى مات فلان طلقت وهي هنا للشرط كأنه قال إن لم يقدم فلان فأنت طالق. ولا تكون للغاية لأنها إنما تكون لها فيما يحتمل التأقيت والطلاق مما لا يحتمله معنى فتكون فيه للشرط وتمامه في فتح القدير. وفي المحيط : لو قال والله لا أكلمه في اليوم الذي يقدم فيه فلان فكلمه في اليوم الذي قدم فيه فلان قبل قدومه حنث لأن شرط الحنث كلامه يوم القدوم وقد وجد. وإن كلمه بعد القدوم قالوا يجب أن لا يحنث لأنه لم يجعل القدوم شرطاً لأنه لم يقرن به حرف الشرط ولكنه جعله معرفاً لما هو شرط الحنث وهو الكلام. وإنما يتصور القدوم معرفاً للشرط إذا وجد الشرط قبله، فأما إذا وجد بعده كونه معرفاً يتصور من ضرورة كون الشيء معرفاً تقدم ذلك الشيء عليه كما لو قال لامرأته أنت طالق قبل : شهر رمضان بشهر كان رمضان معرفاً لا شرطاً. كذا لو قال أنت طالق قبل قدوم فلان بشهر إذا قدم فلان قبل تمام الشهر لا تطلق ولو عجل الكفارة قبل القدوم لا يصح لأنه لا حنث قبل القدوم ا هـ
Because in the chapter of oaths, section on oaths concerning eating, drinking, clothing, and speaking: if he did not arrive until so-and-so died, she is divorced, and it (the word 'until') is for the condition, as if he said, 'If so-and-so does not arrive, then you are divorced.' It is not for the termination (ghaya) because termination only applies to what can be time-bound, and divorce cannot be time-bound in meaning, so it is for the condition. Its completion is in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Muhit: If he said, 'By Allah, I will not speak to him on the day when so-and-so arrives,' and he spoke to him on the day when so-and-so arrived before his arrival, he breaks his oath because the condition of breaking is speaking on the day of arrival, and it occurred. If he spoke after arrival, they said it is necessary that he does not break his oath because he did not make the arrival a condition, as he did not pair it with a conditional particle, but rather made it an identifier for what is the condition of breaking, namely speaking. The arrival can be an identifier for the condition only if the condition occurs before it; if it occurs after, it can be an identifier due to the necessity of something being an identifier preceding that thing, as if he said to his wife, 'You are divorced before Ramadan by a month,' Ramadan is an identifier, not a condition. Similarly, if he said, 'You are divorced before the arrival of so-and-so by a month,' and so-and-so arrives before the month completes, she is not divorced. And if he hastens the expiation before arrival, it is invalid because there is no violation before arrival.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on oaths, specifically about the phrase 'until' (حتى) when used in an oath. The author clarifies that when a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced until Zaid dies' or 'unless Zaid arrives,' the word 'until' is interpreted as a conditional particle meaning 'if not,' not as a temporal limit. This is because divorce, being an instantaneous event, cannot be stretched over time. The author draws from al-Muhit to illustrate: if someone swears not to speak on the day of X's arrival, speaking before arrival constitutes a violation because the day of arrival is the condition; speaking after arrival does not break the oath because arrival is merely an identifier (معرف) of the day, not a condition. The author analogizes this to a husband saying 'you are divorced before Ramadan by a month' – Ramadan serves only as an identifier. The same applies if one says 'you are divorced before the arrival of so-and-so by a month.' The consequence: hastening expiation before the arrival is invalid because no violation has occurred.

Key Terms
حتى (ḥattā): In oaths, it functions as a conditional particle (shart) meaning 'unless' or 'until', not as a temporal limit (ghāya) when the action cannot be time-bound.
شرط (sharṭ): A condition that triggers the effect of the oath upon fulfillment.
غاية (ghāya): A temporal end point that cannot apply to instantaneous events like divorce.
معرف (mu‘arrif): An identifier that specifies the condition but is not itself the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the oath 'By Allah, I will not speak to him on the day Zaid arrives,' if the person speaks after Zaid's arrival, does he violate the oath?
A) Yes, because it is on the same day.
B) No, because 'day' is a condition and arrival only identifies the day.
C) Yes, because the condition of the day is fulfilled.
D) No, because the oath expired at Zaid's arrival.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, 'until' functions as a condition (shart) for instantaneous events, not a temporal limit (ghāya); thus, identifiers do not trigger violation after the event occurs.

Oaths on Specific Property: Effect of Pointing and Ownership Change
Ruling on oaths with addition and pointing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state whether an oath is broken when a pointed or non-pointed item changes ownership or a new similar item is acquired, and distinguish between cases involving friends, wives, and slaves.

Core Idea
An oath tied to a relationship ends when ownership changes; an oath tied to a specific object by pointing may persist depending on the object's nature.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi rulings on oaths that involve a specific person's property, such as 'I will not eat Zaid's food' or 'I will not enter his house'. The key distinction is whether the swearer pointed to the item or not, and whether the item is of a type that is typically the direct target of enmity. Understanding this distinction helps determine when an oath remains binding after changes in ownership or acquisition of new items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text distinguishes between oaths with pointing (isharah) and without pointing. Determine which scenario is being discussed.
2
Apply the rule for ownership change
If the property changes ownership after the oath, check whether the swearer pointed to it. Without pointing, the oath ends; with pointing, the oath may continue depending on the type of object.
3
Distinguish by object type
For objects like a friend or wife (objects of enmity), pointing means the oath survives ownership change. For objects like a slave or house, pointing also means the oath ends with ownership change.
The slave is considered like a house according to the madhhab.
4
Apply the rule for newly acquired items
For items that come into existence or ownership after the oath (mutajaddid), if there was no pointing, the oath applies to new items. If there was pointing, it depends on the object type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب واللبس والكلام

ثوبه أو لا يركب دابته أو لا يكلم عبده إن أشار وزال ملكه وفعل لم يحنث كما في المتجدد وإن لم يشر لا يحنث بعد الزوال وحنث بالمتجدد وفي الصديق والزوجة حنث المشار بعد الزوال وفي غير المشار لا وحنث بالمتجدد لا يكلم صاحب هذا
عليه أو ما زال عليه فنزعه ثم لبسه وكلمه لا يحنث ولو قال لا أكلمه وعليه هذا الثوب فنزعه ثم لبسه وكلمه حنث لأن في هذه الصورة ما جعل اليمين موقتة بوقت بل قيده بصفة فتبقى اليمين ما بقيت تلك الصفة. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : إذا قال لأبويه إن تزوجت ما دمتما حيين فكذا فتزوج امرأة في حياتهما حنث، فلو تزوج امرأة أخرى في حياتهما لا يلزمه الحنث، ولو كان قال كل امرأة أتزوجها ما دمتما حيين يلزمه الحنث بكل امرأة يتزوجها ما داما حيين؛ فإذا مات أحدهما سقط اليمين حتى لو تزوج امرأة بعد ذلك لا يلزمه حكم الحنث لأن شرط الحنث الزوج ما داما حين ولا يتصور ذلك بعد موت أحدهما فيسقط، وإذا حلف لا يأكل هذا الطعام ما دام في ملك فلان فباع فلان بعضه ثم أكل الحالف الباقي لا يحنث لأن اليمين قد انتهى ببيع البعض. ولو قال لغريمه والله لا أفارقك حتى تقضيني حقي اليوم ونيته أن لا يترك لزومه حتى يعطيه حقه فمضى اليوم ولم يفارقه ولم يعطه حقه لا يحنث، فإن فارقه بعد مضي المدة يحنث وكذلك إذا قال لا أفارقك حتى أقدمك إلى السلطان اليوم أو حتى يخلصك السلطان مني فمضى اليوم ولم يفارقه ولم يقدمه إلى السلطان ولم يخلصه السلطان فهو سواء لا يحنث إلا بتركه ولو قدم اليوم فقال لا أفارقك اليوم حتى تعطيني حقي فمضى اليوم ولم يفارقه ولم يعطه حقه لم يحنث، وإن فارقه بعد مضي اليوم لا يحنث لأنه وقت للفراق ذلك اليوم وتمام مسائلها فيها .

قوله : (لا يأكل طعام زيد أو لا يدخل داره أو لا يلبس ثوبه أو لا يركب دابته إن أشار وزال ملكه وفعل لم يحنث كالمتجدد وإن لم يشر لا يحنث بعد الزوال وحنث بالمتجدد وفي الصديق والزوجة حنث في المشار بعد الزوال وفي غير المشار وحنث بالمتجدد) بيان لمسائل الأصل فيها أنه إذا حلف على هجران محل مضاف إلى فلان كلا يكلم عبد فلان أو زوجته أو صديقة أو لا يدخل داره أو لا يلبس ثوبه أو لا يركب فرسه أو لا يأكل طعامه، فلا شك أن
فتعمل نيته لأنه شدد على نفسه والظاهر أن يقال هنا كذلك قوله : (ثم أكل الباقي لا يحنث) الذي يطهر تقييده بما إذا كان يمكنه أكل كله، وقد تقدم ما يدل على ذلك؛ كذا في حواشي مسكين لأبي السعود. قلت: لكن علل المسألة في الخانية بقوله لأن شرط الحنث الأكل حال بقاء الكل في ملك فلان ولا يوجد ا هـ. ومفاده عدم الحنث مطلقاً لفقد الشرط قول المصنف : (أو لا يركب دابته) قال الرملي : في النسخ التي لدينا متوناً وشروحاً بعد هذا ولا يكلم عبده والذي يظهر أن النسخة التي شرح عليها ليس فيها ذلك فلذا قال فيما يأتي ولم يذكر المصنف العبد فتأمل .

يهجر

هذه الإضافة في الكل معرفة لعين ما عقد اليمين على هجره، سواء كانت إضافة ملك كعبده وداره ودابته ، أو إضافة نسبه أخرى غير الملك كزوجته وصديقه، فالإضافة مطلقاً تفيد النسبة والنسبة أعم من كونها نسبة ملك أو غيره فلا يصح جعل إضافة النسبة تقابل إضافة الملك كما في الهداية وغيرها لأنه لا تقابل بين الأعم والأخص إلا أن يكون مخصوص عرف اصطلاحي . وإذا كانت هذه الإضافة مطلقاً للتعريف فبعد ذلك إما أن يقرن به لفظ الإشارة كقوله لا أكلم عبده هذا أو لا، فعلى تقدير عدم الإشارة الظاهر أن الداعي في اليمين كراهته في المضاف إليه وإلا لعرفه باسمه العلم ثم أعقبه بالإضافة إن عرض اشترك مثل لا أكلم راشداً عبد فلان ليزيل الاشتراك العارض في اسم راشد، فلما اقتصر على الاضافة ولم يذكر اسمه ولا أشار إليه كان الظاهر أنه لمعنى في المضاف إليه، وإن احتمل أن : . بغضاً لذاته أيضاً كالزوجة والصديق فلا يصار إليه بالاحتمال ، وحينئذ فاليمين منعقدة على هجر المضاف حال قيام الإضافة وقت الفعل بأن كان موجوداً وقت اليمين ودامت الإضافة إلى وقت الفعل أو انقطعت ثم وجدت بأن باع وطلق ثم استرد أو لم يكن وقت اليمين فاشترى عبداً فكلمه حنث، وكذا لو لم تكن له زوجة فاستحدث زوجة. والحاصل أنه إذا أضاف ولم يشر لا يحنث بعد الزوال في الكل لانقطاع الإضافة، ويحنث في المتجدد بعد اليمين في الكل لوجودها. وإذا أضاف وأشار فإنه لا يحنث بعد الزوال والتجدد إن كان المضاف لا يقصد بالمعاداة وإلا حنث . ولم يذكر المصنف العبد للاختلاف فالمذهب أنه كالدار لأنه لا يقصد بالمعاداة . وروى ابن سماعة أنه كالصديق . ووجه الظاهر أنه العبد ساقط الاعتبار عند الأحرار فإنه يباع في الأسواق كالحمار، فالظاهر أنه إن كان منه أذى إنما بقصد هجران سیده بهجرانه، ، وفي بعض الشروح : لا أتزوج بنت فلان لا يحنث بالبنت التي تولد بعد اليمين بالإجماع وهو مشكل فإنها إضافة نسبية فينبغي أن تنعقد على الموجود حال التزوج فلا جرم أن في التفاريق عن أبي يوسف إن تزوجت بنت فلان أو أمته على الموجود والحادث ؛ كذا في فتح القدير .

أن
وأطلق المصنف في زوال الملك في المسألة الأولى فشمل ما إذا زالت الملك من المحلوف عليه إلى الحالف كما إذا حلف لا يأكل طعامك هذا فأهداه له فأكله لم يحنث في قياس قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وعند محمد يحنث وكذلك في بقية المسائل لا فرق في الزوال بين يكون إلى الحالف أو لا ؛ كذا في الذخيرة. ولو حلف لا يأكل من غلة أرضه فأكل من ثمن الغلة حنث لأنه في العرف يسمى أكلاً غلة أرضه، وإن نوى أكل نفس ما يخرج منها صدق ديانة وقضاء لأنه نوى الحقيقة ؛ كذا في الذخيرة أيضاً. ولو حلف لا يأكل من كسب فلان فالكسب ما صار له بفعله كأخذ المباحات أو بقبوله في العقود، فأما الميراث فليس بكسبه لأن قوله : (وإلا حنث ظاهره يحنث في المتجدد أيضاً مع أن الزيلعي عند قول المصنف «وحنث بالمتجدد أي حنث بالمتجدد من العبدين والزوجة في هذه الصورة وهي ما إذا حلف لا يكلم صديق
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oaths Regarding Eating, Drinking, Clothing, and Speech

... (his garment) or not to ride his animal, or not to speak to his slave; if he pointed and his ownership ceased and he did (the act), he does not break the oath, as in the case of something newly acquired. If he did not point, he does not break the oath after the cessation (of ownership) but breaks it with something newly acquired. Regarding a friend and a wife: he breaks the oath for the pointed one after cessation, and for the non-pointed one he does not break it, but breaks it with something newly acquired... (continues in similar literal style)
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It deals with oaths that involve a specific item or person identified by addition (idafah) to another, such as 'I will not eat Zaid's food' or 'I will not enter his house.' The key issue is: does the oath bind the specific object as it is at the time of the oath, or does it bind the relationship (ownership, association) so that if ownership changes, the oath may be broken or not? The commentator explains that if the person swearing points to the specific item (e.g., 'this garment'), the oath is tied to that exact item, so if ownership changes and he then wears it, he does not break the oath. If he does not point, the oath is tied to the relationship (e.g., being Zaid's garment), so if ownership changes, the oath is no longer effective. He distinguishes between items that are typically objects of enmity (like a friend or wife) and those that are not (like a slave or house). The passage also includes a fatwa from Abu al-Layth about oaths conditioned on parents being alive, and rulings about oaths not to separate until payment within a certain time. The commentary uses principles like istihsan and qiyas, and cites intra-Hanafi differences: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad regarding when ownership passes to the swearer. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated, but the preference for the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is indicated by 'qiyas qawl Abi Hanifa wa Abi Yusuf'.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-ishārah): Pointing to a specific object or person in an oath, making the oath attached to that particular entity.
زوال الملك (zawāl al-milk): Cessation of ownership from the person to whom the item is added in the oath.
المتجدد (al-mutajaddid): Something newly acquired after the oath, such as a new garment or a new wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone swears 'I will not eat your food' (without pointing), and then the food is sold to a third party, what happens if the swearer eats it?
A) He breaks the oath because the food is still the same.
B) He does not break the oath because ownership has changed.
C) He breaks the oath only if he pointed to the food.
D) He must seek a fatwa to determine the ruling.

Key Takeaway
Without isharah, an oath on property ends when ownership changes; with isharah, it ends only for non-enmity objects.

8 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Does an Oath Break When Ownership Passes to the Swearer?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths when ownership passes to swearer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scenario where the swearer becomes owner of the oath-object and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and state the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
When the oath-object's ownership transfers to the swearer, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the oath ends; Muhammad says it persists.

This lesson zooms in on a specific scenario mentioned in the commentary: the swearer swears not to eat a specific food, and later the owner gives it to him as a gift. Does he break his oath if he eats it? The Hanafi imams differed on this, with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf applying istihsan and Muhammad applying qiyas. Understanding this dispute sharpens your appreciation of how usul principles shape fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Find the case where the swearer says 'I will not eat your food' and the owner gives him the food as a gift. The swearer now owns the food.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: the oath is not broken. Muhammad: the oath is broken. Identify the basis: istihsan vs qiyas.
3
Analyze the istihsan argument
The oath was tied to the relationship of ownership. When the swearer becomes owner, the original relationship is gone, so the oath ceases.
4
Analyze the qiyas argument
If the oath had been with isharah (pointing), it would be attached to the specific item regardless of ownership. Without isharah, the same logic should apply: the item is definite.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as indicated by the text.

Source Text
