كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب واللبس والكلام

ثوبه أو لا يركب دابته أو لا يكلم عبده إن أشار وزال ملكه وفعل لم يحنث كما في المتجدد وإن لم يشر لا يحنث بعد الزوال وحنث بالمتجدد وفي الصديق والزوجة حنث المشار بعد الزوال وفي غير المشار لا وحنث بالمتجدد لا يكلم صاحب هذا
عليه أو ما زال عليه فنزعه ثم لبسه وكلمه لا يحنث ولو قال لا أكلمه وعليه هذا الثوب فنزعه ثم لبسه وكلمه حنث لأن في هذه الصورة ما جعل اليمين موقتة بوقت بل قيده بصفة فتبقى اليمين ما بقيت تلك الصفة. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : إذا قال لأبويه إن تزوجت ما دمتما حيين فكذا فتزوج امرأة في حياتهما حنث، فلو تزوج امرأة أخرى في حياتهما لا يلزمه الحنث، ولو كان قال كل امرأة أتزوجها ما دمتما حيين يلزمه الحنث بكل امرأة يتزوجها ما داما حيين؛ فإذا مات أحدهما سقط اليمين حتى لو تزوج امرأة بعد ذلك لا يلزمه حكم الحنث لأن شرط الحنث الزوج ما داما حين ولا يتصور ذلك بعد موت أحدهما فيسقط، وإذا حلف لا يأكل هذا الطعام ما دام في ملك فلان فباع فلان بعضه ثم أكل الحالف الباقي لا يحنث لأن اليمين قد انتهى ببيع البعض. ولو قال لغريمه والله لا أفارقك حتى تقضيني حقي اليوم ونيته أن لا يترك لزومه حتى يعطيه حقه فمضى اليوم ولم يفارقه ولم يعطه حقه لا يحنث، فإن فارقه بعد مضي المدة يحنث وكذلك إذا قال لا أفارقك حتى أقدمك إلى السلطان اليوم أو حتى يخلصك السلطان مني فمضى اليوم ولم يفارقه ولم يقدمه إلى السلطان ولم يخلصه السلطان فهو سواء لا يحنث إلا بتركه ولو قدم اليوم فقال لا أفارقك اليوم حتى تعطيني حقي فمضى اليوم ولم يفارقه ولم يعطه حقه لم يحنث، وإن فارقه بعد مضي اليوم لا يحنث لأنه وقت للفراق ذلك اليوم وتمام مسائلها فيها .

قوله : (لا يأكل طعام زيد أو لا يدخل داره أو لا يلبس ثوبه أو لا يركب دابته إن أشار وزال ملكه وفعل لم يحنث كالمتجدد وإن لم يشر لا يحنث بعد الزوال وحنث بالمتجدد وفي الصديق والزوجة حنث في المشار بعد الزوال وفي غير المشار وحنث بالمتجدد) بيان لمسائل الأصل فيها أنه إذا حلف على هجران محل مضاف إلى فلان كلا يكلم عبد فلان أو زوجته أو صديقة أو لا يدخل داره أو لا يلبس ثوبه أو لا يركب فرسه أو لا يأكل طعامه، فلا شك أن
فتعمل نيته لأنه شدد على نفسه والظاهر أن يقال هنا كذلك قوله : (ثم أكل الباقي لا يحنث) الذي يطهر تقييده بما إذا كان يمكنه أكل كله، وقد تقدم ما يدل على ذلك؛ كذا في حواشي مسكين لأبي السعود. قلت: لكن علل المسألة في الخانية بقوله لأن شرط الحنث الأكل حال بقاء الكل في ملك فلان ولا يوجد ا هـ. ومفاده عدم الحنث مطلقاً لفقد الشرط قول المصنف : (أو لا يركب دابته) قال الرملي : في النسخ التي لدينا متوناً وشروحاً بعد هذا ولا يكلم عبده والذي يظهر أن النسخة التي شرح عليها ليس فيها ذلك فلذا قال فيما يأتي ولم يذكر المصنف العبد فتأمل .

يهجر

هذه الإضافة في الكل معرفة لعين ما عقد اليمين على هجره، سواء كانت إضافة ملك كعبده وداره ودابته ، أو إضافة نسبه أخرى غير الملك كزوجته وصديقه، فالإضافة مطلقاً تفيد النسبة والنسبة أعم من كونها نسبة ملك أو غيره فلا يصح جعل إضافة النسبة تقابل إضافة الملك كما في الهداية وغيرها لأنه لا تقابل بين الأعم والأخص إلا أن يكون مخصوص عرف اصطلاحي . وإذا كانت هذه الإضافة مطلقاً للتعريف فبعد ذلك إما أن يقرن به لفظ الإشارة كقوله لا أكلم عبده هذا أو لا، فعلى تقدير عدم الإشارة الظاهر أن الداعي في اليمين كراهته في المضاف إليه وإلا لعرفه باسمه العلم ثم أعقبه بالإضافة إن عرض اشترك مثل لا أكلم راشداً عبد فلان ليزيل الاشتراك العارض في اسم راشد، فلما اقتصر على الاضافة ولم يذكر اسمه ولا أشار إليه كان الظاهر أنه لمعنى في المضاف إليه، وإن احتمل أن : . بغضاً لذاته أيضاً كالزوجة والصديق فلا يصار إليه بالاحتمال ، وحينئذ فاليمين منعقدة على هجر المضاف حال قيام الإضافة وقت الفعل بأن كان موجوداً وقت اليمين ودامت الإضافة إلى وقت الفعل أو انقطعت ثم وجدت بأن باع وطلق ثم استرد أو لم يكن وقت اليمين فاشترى عبداً فكلمه حنث، وكذا لو لم تكن له زوجة فاستحدث زوجة. والحاصل أنه إذا أضاف ولم يشر لا يحنث بعد الزوال في الكل لانقطاع الإضافة، ويحنث في المتجدد بعد اليمين في الكل لوجودها. وإذا أضاف وأشار فإنه لا يحنث بعد الزوال والتجدد إن كان المضاف لا يقصد بالمعاداة وإلا حنث . ولم يذكر المصنف العبد للاختلاف فالمذهب أنه كالدار لأنه لا يقصد بالمعاداة . وروى ابن سماعة أنه كالصديق . ووجه الظاهر أنه العبد ساقط الاعتبار عند الأحرار فإنه يباع في الأسواق كالحمار، فالظاهر أنه إن كان منه أذى إنما بقصد هجران سیده بهجرانه، ، وفي بعض الشروح : لا أتزوج بنت فلان لا يحنث بالبنت التي تولد بعد اليمين بالإجماع وهو مشكل فإنها إضافة نسبية فينبغي أن تنعقد على الموجود حال التزوج فلا جرم أن في التفاريق عن أبي يوسف إن تزوجت بنت فلان أو أمته على الموجود والحادث ؛ كذا في فتح القدير .

أن
وأطلق المصنف في زوال الملك في المسألة الأولى فشمل ما إذا زالت الملك من المحلوف عليه إلى الحالف كما إذا حلف لا يأكل طعامك هذا فأهداه له فأكله لم يحنث في قياس قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وعند محمد يحنث وكذلك في بقية المسائل لا فرق في الزوال بين يكون إلى الحالف أو لا ؛ كذا في الذخيرة. ولو حلف لا يأكل من غلة أرضه فأكل من ثمن الغلة حنث لأنه في العرف يسمى أكلاً غلة أرضه، وإن نوى أكل نفس ما يخرج منها صدق ديانة وقضاء لأنه نوى الحقيقة ؛ كذا في الذخيرة أيضاً. ولو حلف لا يأكل من كسب فلان فالكسب ما صار له بفعله كأخذ المباحات أو بقبوله في العقود، فأما الميراث فليس بكسبه لأن قوله : (وإلا حنث ظاهره يحنث في المتجدد أيضاً مع أن الزيلعي عند قول المصنف «وحنث بالمتجدد أي حنث بالمتجدد من العبدين والزوجة في هذه الصورة وهي ما إذا حلف لا يكلم صديق
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oaths Regarding Eating, Drinking, Clothing, and Speech

... (his garment) or not to ride his animal, or not to speak to his slave; if he pointed and his ownership ceased and he did (the act), he does not break the oath, as in the case of something newly acquired. If he did not point, he does not break the oath after the cessation (of ownership) but breaks it with something newly acquired. Regarding a friend and a wife: he breaks the oath for the pointed one after cessation, and for the non-pointed one he does not break it, but breaks it with something newly acquired... (continues in similar literal style)
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It deals with oaths that involve a specific item or person identified by addition (idafah) to another, such as 'I will not eat Zaid's food' or 'I will not enter his house.' The key issue is: does the oath bind the specific object as it is at the time of the oath, or does it bind the relationship (ownership, association) so that if ownership changes, the oath may be broken or not? The commentator explains that if the person swearing points to the specific item (e.g., 'this garment'), the oath is tied to that exact item, so if ownership changes and he then wears it, he does not break the oath. If he does not point, the oath is tied to the relationship (e.g., being Zaid's garment), so if ownership changes, the oath is no longer effective. He distinguishes between items that are typically objects of enmity (like a friend or wife) and those that are not (like a slave or house). The passage also includes a fatwa from Abu al-Layth about oaths conditioned on parents being alive, and rulings about oaths not to separate until payment within a certain time. The commentary uses principles like istihsan and qiyas, and cites intra-Hanafi differences: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad regarding when ownership passes to the swearer. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated, but the preference for the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is indicated by 'qiyas qawl Abi Hanifa wa Abi Yusuf'.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-ishārah): Pointing to a specific object or person in an oath, making the oath attached to that particular entity.
زوال الملك (zawāl al-milk): Cessation of ownership from the person to whom the item is added in the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if a person swears 'I will not eat your food' (without pointing) and the food is given to him as a gift, what is the ruling if he eats it?
A) He breaks his oath and must perform expiation.
B) He does not break his oath.
C) He breaks his oath only if he pointed to the food.
D) He breaks his oath because the food is the same.

Key Takeaway
When ownership of an oath-object passes to the swearer, the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf) holds the oath ends; Muhammad's contrary view is marjuh.

Oath Interpretation: Common Usage and Substance Transformation
Rulings on oaths about planting land, meat bought by someone, and shared food

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of oath interpretation to cases involving shared property and transformed substances, determining whether an oath is broken based on common usage ('urf) and the principle of telashi.

Core Idea
In oaths, every part of land is 'land', but a substance that loses its identity (telashi) breaks the link to the oath.

Oaths about land, meat, and shared food require careful analysis of what the swearer actually meant and whether the object of the oath still exists in its original form. The Hanafi school relies on linguistic custom ('urf) to determine if an oath is broken. This lesson examines several rulings from al-Waqi'at and al-Dhakhirah that illustrate these principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the oath
Determine what the swearer swore about: land, meat, food, wealth, or acquisition. This sets the boundaries for interpretation.
The passage covers multiple subjects; each has a specific rule.
2
Check common usage ('urf) for the term
In Hanafi law, oaths are interpreted according to the common understanding of the term. For example, 'land' includes every part, but 'house' and 'garment' do not.
This difference is key: planting shared land breaks the oath because each part is called land.
3
Examine if the substance has undergone transformation (telashi)
If the original substance has lost its identity (e.g., dough mixed, meat bought alive then slaughtered), the oath is not broken because the object no longer exists as originally understood.
Examples: dough from son-in-law mixed; water and salt added to dough; meat bought as live goat.
4
Ascertain the value of any remaining object
For a crumb in the house of the one sworn against, if it has negligible value (not given to a poor person), no breach; otherwise, breach. This distinguishes between trivial and significant remnants.
Value is assessed by what a poor person would accept.
5
Consider the role of intention (niyyah)
The rulings in al-Mukhtasar apply when there is no specific intention. If the swearer had a particular intent, that governs, as his words can bear that meaning.
Similarly, phrases like 'from the grinding of so-and-so' apply to both past and future unless intention specifies otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب واللبس والكلام الطيلسان فباعه فكلمه حنث الزمان والحين ومنكر هما ستة أشهر والدهر والأبد العمر ودهر مجمل والأيام وأيام كثيرة والشهور والسنون عشرة ومنكرها ثلاثة . يسمى ختني من حصته. ولو حلف لا يزرع أرض فلان فزرع أرضاً بينه وبين غيره حنث لأن كل جزء من الأرض يسمى أرضاً ولا كذلك الثوب والدار فإن كل جزء من الدار لا يسمى داراً وكذلك كل جزء من الثوب لا ثوباً ا هـ. وفي الواقعات : حلف لا يأكل لحماً يشتريه فلان فاشترى سخلة وذبحها فأكله الحالف لا يحنث لأن فلاناً ما اشتراه بعد ما صار لحماً . ولو حلف لا يأكل من طعام فلان فأكل من خله بطعام نفسه أو بزيته أو بملحه حنث لأنه أكل من طعامه، ولو حلف لا يأكل من مال ابنه وكان بينه وبين ابنه حب من خل فأكل منه يحنث لأنه أكل من مال الابن ا هـ . ويحتاج حينئذ إلى الفرق بين الطعام والمال كما لا يخفى . وفي الواقعات أيضاً : قال إن أكلت مال من شيئاً فامرأتي طالق فدفع إليه عجين ختنه فجعل في عجين آخر وخبزه فأكل لأن العجين قد ذهب، وكذا لو حلف لا يشرب من شرابه ولا يأكل من لحمه فأخذ ماء وملحاً للمحلوف عليه وجعلهما في عجين لا يحنث إذا أكل من ذلك الخبز لأن ذلك قد تلاشى ولو حلف لا يأكل من كسب فلان فأكل كسرة مطروحة في بيت المحلوف عليه، فإن كانت الكسرة بحال لا يعطي مثلها الفقير لا يحنث، وإن كان بحال يعطي مثلها الفقير يحنث ا هـ . ثم اعلم أن ما في المختصر إنما هو عند عدم النية، وأما إذا نوى شيئاً فهو على ما نوى لأنه محتمل كلامه وفي الذخيرة حلف لا يأكل من طحن فلان من خبزه فهذا على الماضي والمستقبل، وكذلك قوله مما خبز فلان مما اشترى فلان على الماضي والمستقبل ا هـ .
The chapter of oaths, section on oaths regarding eating, drinking, clothing, and speech: the taylasan (shawl), if he sold it and he spoke to him, he breaks the oath. Time (zaman) and period (hin) – their indefinite forms are six months; the age (dahr) and eternity (abad) are lifetime; a vague age and days, many days, months and years are ten, and their indefinite is three. Called my son-in-law from his share. If he swears not to plant the land of so-and-so, and he plants land shared between him and another, he breaks the oath because every part of land is called land, but not so for garment and house, for every part of house is not called house, and similarly every part of garment is not called garment. In al-Waqi'at: he swore not to eat meat bought by so-and-so, and he bought a young goat and slaughtered it, and the swearer ate it, he does not break the oath because so-and-so did not buy it after it became meat. If he swears not to eat from the food of so-and-so, and he eats from his partner with his own food or his oil or his salt, he breaks the oath because he ate from his food. If he swears not to eat from the wealth of his son, and between him and his son there is a jar of vinegar, and he eats from it, he breaks the oath because he ate from the son's wealth. Then there is need for the distinction between food and wealth, as is not hidden. In al-Waqi'at also: he said 'If I eat anything from the wealth of so-and-so, my wife is divorced', and dough from his son-in-law was given to him, and he put it in other dough and baked it and ate, because the dough has gone. Similarly, if he swore not to drink from his drink nor eat from his meat, and he took water and salt for the one sworn against and put them in dough, he does not break the oath if he eats that bread because that has dissolved. If he swore not to eat from the acquisition of so-and-so, and he eats a crumb thrown in the house of the one sworn against, if the crumb is such that a poor person would not be given its like, he does not break the oath; if it is such that a poor person would be given its like, he breaks the oath. Then know that what is in al-Mukhtasar is only in the absence of intention (niyyah). But if he intended something, then it is according to what he intended because his words are capable. In al-Dhakhirah: he swore not to eat from the grinding of so-and-so from his bread – this applies to past and future. Similarly, his saying 'from what so-and-so baked' and 'from what so-and-so bought' applies to past and future.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on oaths, now focusing on specific objects. The author explains that if someone swears not to plant the land of another, and plants land that is shared, he breaks the oath because each part of land is called 'land' – unlike a house or garment where parts are not called by the whole name. Then the author cites al-Waqi'at with new rulings: if one swears not to eat meat bought by a specific person, and that person buys a live goat and then slaughters it, eating it does not break the oath because the meat was not what he bought. On shared food, if one eats from a partner's food but with his own condiments (oil, salt), he still breaks the oath because he ate from the other's food. However, a distinction is needed between 'food' and 'wealth' – here 'wealth' includes non-edibles. Another case: dough from a son-in-law mixed with other dough – the original dough is considered gone, so no breach. Similarly, if water and salt are added to dough, the original dissolved. A crumb in the house of the one sworn against: if it has negligible value, no breach; otherwise, breach. Finally, the author notes that these rulings apply when the swearer had no specific intention (niyyah). If he had an intention, that governs, as his words can bear that meaning. The source in al-Dhakhirah clarifies that phrases like 'from the grinding of so-and-so' apply to both past and future.

Key Terms
أرض (ard): Land, where every part is called land, unlike a house or garment.
كسرة (kisrah): A small piece or crumb of bread, of negligible value if not given to a poor person.
تلاشى (talasha): To dissolve or vanish, losing original identity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to eat from the wealth of his son, and he eats from a jar of vinegar that is co-owned with his son, does he break the oath?
A) Yes, because the vinegar is from the son's wealth.
B) No, because it is co-owned.
C) Yes, only if the son's share is more than half.
D) No, because vinegar is not considered wealth.

Key Takeaway
Oaths are interpreted by common usage and the physical identity of the object; a substance that loses its identity (telashi) does not trigger a breach.

Oath regarding speech and definitions of time terms
Rulings on oath not to speak to the owner of a garment, and definitions of time periods in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on an oath that identifies a person by a specific object, and state the default duration of indefinite time terms in oaths.

Core Idea
An oath tied to a description follows the identified person; indefinite 'hin' in oaths means six months.

This lesson examines two key issues in Hanafi oath law: the effect of identification through a specific garment (taylasan), and the default meaning of vague time terms like 'zaman' and 'hin'. Understanding these principles is essential for correctly determining when an oath is broken and for interpreting ambiguous language in legal commitments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is swearing an oath not to speak to the owner of a specific item. The ruling is that the oath attaches to the person identified by that item, not to the item itself.
2
Understand the effect of selling the item
If the item is sold, the oath remains tied to the original owner; speaking to the new owner does not break the oath.
3
Define indefinite time terms in oaths
The text states that indefinite 'zaman' and 'hin' default to six months. This is derived from Quranic usage.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence
The verses cited (30:17, 76:1, 14:??) show 'hin' used for different periods; the Hanafi school chooses the middle ground of six months as the default.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools may have different defaults for 'hin'; the Hanafi position is mufta bihi in the school.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the matn phrase about oaths related to speech. The ruling: if someone swears 'I will not speak to the owner of this taylasan', the oath is connected to the person identified by the garment. If he sells the garment and speaks to the new owner, he does not break the oath because the identification changes. The garment's material (silk) could be a reason for enmity, but the principle is that the oath is on the description. The second part defines time terms used in oaths: 'zaman' and 'hin' in their indefinite form (without the definite article or specification) are understood as six months per Hanafi convention. This is based on Quranic usage where 'hin' can mean a short time, forty years, or six months. The Hanafi school adopts the middle interpretation as default. The passage ends abruptly with a note about the taylasan being silk, referencing later commentaries.

Key Terms
الحين (al-hin): A period of time; in its indefinite form, it defaults to six months in Hanafi law.
الطيلسان (al-taylasan): A specific type of round black shawl, often woolen, worn by scholars; used as an example for identification in oaths.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath based on an identifying description follows the identified person, and indefinite time terms 'zaman' and 'hin' are interpreted as six months by default.

intermediate
8 min
Oath on Speaking to the Owner of a Specific Garment: Attribution for Identification
Ruling with rational proof on oath scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath to not speak to the owner of a specific garment after sale, and explain the rationale that the attribution is for identification (idafat al-ta'rif).

Core Idea
An oath attached to 'owner of X' refers to the person, not the item; sale transfers the identification.

In Hanafi fiqh, the phrasing of an oath determines its scope. This lesson unpacks a case from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: a person swears not to speak to 'the owner of this taylasan' then sells the garment. Does speaking to the original owner or the new buyer break the oath? The answer hinges on whether the attribution (idafa) is for defining the person or for the item itself. The Hanafi position, supported by rational proof, clarifies that the oath is tied to the identified individual, not the garment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
State the case: a person swears 'I will not speak to the owner of this taylasan', then sells the taylasan. If he later speaks to the original owner, he breaks the oath; if he speaks to the buyer, he does not. This is the mufta bihi ruling.
The taylasan is a woolen cloak used as an example.
2
Understand the rationale: idafat al-ta'rif
The key reason is that the attribution (idafa) of the taylasan to the owner is purely for identification (ta'rif), not because the garment itself is the reason for the oath. A person does not avoid speaking to someone because of a garment; thus the oath attaches to the identified person (the owner at the time of the oath), not to anyone who later becomes owner.
This distinguishes the oath from one that is about the garment itself, like 'I will not wear So-and-so's taylasan.'
3
Apply the ruling to analogous cases
The same ruling applies if the swearer says 'owner of this house' or 'owner of this food' – the oath is tied to the original owner. The taylasan is just an example; the principle is general for any unique possession identified by ownership.
Source: al-Dhakhira confirms the generality.
4
Distinguish from other oath types
Note the contrast with an oath like 'I will not wear So-and-so's taylasan.' That oath is about the garment itself, and the ruling regarding its breach follows the rules of garments (e.g., if the garment is sold, the oath may or may not transfer – discussed in a previous passage). Do not conflate the two.
The text says: 'it is like his saying not to wear So-and-so's garment, and there is the previous distinction.'
5
Consider the linguistic note (optional)
The taylasan is defined as a round black woolen cloak. A marginal note mentions that if it were silk, the owner might be hostile due to prohibition of silk, but that does not affect the oath ruling.
This note is cultural and not legally determinative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطيلسان فباعه فكلمه حنث . قوله: (لا) يكلم صاحب هذا الطيلسان فباعه فكلمه حنث لأن الإنسان لا يمتنع عن كلام صاحب الطيلسان لأجل الطيلسان فكانت الإضافة للتعريف فتعلقت اليمين بالمعرف، ولهذا لو كلم المشتري لا يحنث . وذكر الطيلسان للتمثيل لأنه لو قال لا أكلم صاحب هذه الدار وهذا الطعام فالحكم كذلك كما في الذخيرة قيد بهذه اليمين لأنه لو حلف لا يلبس طیلسان فلان فهو كقوله لا يلبس ثوب فلان وفيه التفصيل السابق والطيلسان معرب تيلسان أبدلوا التاء طاء من لباس العجم مدور أسود لحمته وسداه صوف قوله : (لأن الإنسان لا يمتنع عن كلام صاحب الطيلسان لأجل الطيلسان) فيه أنه يكون حريراً فيعادي لذلك؛ كذا في حاشية أبي السعود عن الحموي عن البرجندي.
The taylasan: if he sells it and then speaks to (the owner), he breaks the oath. His statement: '(He is not to) speak to the owner of this taylasan, so he sold it and spoke to him, he breaks the oath because a person does not refrain from speaking to the owner of the taylasan for the sake of the taylasan; thus the attribution is for identification, so the oath is attached to the identified person. Hence, if he speaks to the buyer, he does not break the oath. He mentioned the taylasan as an example, because if he says 'I will not speak to the owner of this house' or 'this food', the ruling is the same, as in al-Dhakhira. It is restricted by this oath because if he swears not to wear So-and-so's taylasan, it is like his saying 'not to wear So-and-so's garment', and there is the previous distinction. The taylasan is a Persian word (tilisan) with the 't' replaced by 'ṭā'', a round black garment of the non-Arabs, its warp and weft of wool. His statement: 'Because a person does not refrain from speaking to the owner of the taylasan for the sake of the taylasan' — therein it may be made of silk, so he would be hostile for that reason; thus in the Hashiya of Abu al-Su'ud from al-Hamawi from al-Barjandi.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on oaths regarding specific individuals identified by their ownership of an item. The matn states: If someone swears not to speak to the owner of a specific taylasan (a traditional cloak), then sells the taylasan, and later speaks to the original owner, he breaks his oath. The commentary explains why: the oath is tied to the person who owns the taylasan at the time of the oath, and the identification (idafa) is merely for definition (ta'rif). Since the prohibition is not due to the taylasan itself—one does not avoid speaking to someone because of a garment—the reference is to the owner as an identified individual. Once ownership transfers, the new owner is the identified person, so speaking to him does not break the oath. The commentator uses the taylasan as an example; the same applies to any unique possession like a house or specific food. He also distinguishes this from an oath not to wear someone's taylasan, which follows the rules of garments (the previous discussion on wearing). The definition of taylasan: a Persian word for a round black woolen cloak. A marginal note notes that if the taylasan were made of silk, the owner might be hostile (due to prohibition of silk), but that does not change the ruling.

Key Terms
طيلسان (taylasan): A round black woolen cloak, originally Persian, worn by non-Arabs.
الإضافة للتعريف (al-idāfatu lil-ta'rīf): Attribution (idafa) that serves only to identify the person, not to specify the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Someone swears 'I will not speak to the owner of this food.' He then gives the food to a friend. Does speaking to the friend (the new owner) break the oath?
A) Yes, because the friend is now the owner.
B) No, because the oath was about the original owner.
C) Yes, because the food is the same.
D) No, because the food is no longer owned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath phrased as 'not to speak to the owner of X' is about the identified person, not the item; sale does not transfer the oath to the new owner.

Oaths on Property: Land vs. House/Garment – The Principle of Naming Parts
A ruling comparing land with house and garment in oath contexts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that every part of land is called 'land' for oath purposes, and distinguish this from houses and garments where parts are not called by the whole name, thereby determining when an oath is broken.

Core Idea
Every part of land is land; parts of a house or garment are not the whole.

When a person swears not to cultivate So-and-so's land, any cultivation of land belonging to So-and-so—even a joint share—breaks the oath because each part is called 'land.' But for houses and garments, parts are never called by the full name, so the principle is different. This lesson examines the legal reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and property type
In the case presented, the oath is about cultivating land. Determine what type of property is involved: land is divisible, while a house or garment is not.
Divisibility here is conceptual, not physical.
2
Apply the naming principle
According to Hanafi fiqh, every portion of land retains the name 'land,' so cultivating any part breaks the oath. But for a house or garment, a brick is not 'a house' and a thread is not 'a garment.'
The same principle applies to oaths regarding other divisible items like food (as seen in other lessons).
3
Differentiate the ruling
If the oath were about a house, cultivating a part would not break the oath. This shows that the classification of the property determines the outcome.
This principle is specific to oaths; it does not apply to contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حلف لا يزرع أرض فلان فزرع أرضاً بينه وبين غيره حنث لأن كل جزء من الأرض يسمى أرضاً ولا كذلك الثوب والدار فإن كل جزء من الدار لا يسمى داراً، وكذلك كل جزء من الثوب لا يسمى ثوباً ا هـ. وفي الواقعات : حلف لا يأكل لحماً يشتريه فلان فاشترى سخلة وذبحها فأكله الحالف لا يحنث لأن فلاناً ما اشتراه بعد ما صار لحماً . ولو حلف لا يأكل من طعام فلان فأكل من خله بطعام نفسه أو بزيته أو بملحه حنث لأنه أكل من طعامه، ولو حلف لا يأكل من مال ابنه وكان بينه وبين ابنه حب من خل فأكل منه يحنث لأنه أكل من مال الابن ا هـ . ويحتاج حينئذ إلى الفرق بين الطعام والمال كما لا يخفى . وفي الواقعات أيضاً : قال إن أكلت مال من شيئاً فامرأتي طالق فدفع إليه عجين ختنه فجعل في عجين آخر وخبزه فأكل لأن العجين قد ذهب، وكذا لو حلف لا يشرب من شرابه ولا يأكل من لحمه فأخذ ماء وملحاً للمحلوف عليه وجعلهما في عجين لا يحنث إذا أكل من ذلك الخبز لأن ذلك قد تلاشى ولو حلف لا يأكل من كسب فلان فأكل كسرة مطروحة في بيت المحلوف عليه، فإن كانت الكسرة بحال لا يعطي مثلها الفقير لا يحنث، وإن كان بحال يعطي مثلها الفقير يحنث ا هـ . ثم اعلم أن ما في المختصر إنما هو عند عدم النية، وأما إذا نوى شيئاً فهو على ما نوى لأنه محتمل كلامه وفي الذخيرة حلف لا يأكل من طحن فلان من خبزه فهذا على الماضي والمستقبل، وكذلك قوله مما خبز فلان مما اشترى فلان على الماضي والمستقبل ا هـ .
And if he swears not to cultivate the land of So-and-so, and he cultivates land between him and another, he breaks the oath because every part of the land is called land; but not so with a garment or house, for every part of a house is not called a house, and likewise every part of a garment is not called a garment. In al-Waqi'at: He swore not to eat meat that So-and-so buys, so he bought a lamb and slaughtered it, and the swearer ate it – he does not break the oath because So-and-so did not buy it after it became meat. And if he swore not to eat from So-and-so's food, and he ate from his vinegar with his own food or his oil or his salt, he breaks the oath because he ate from his food. And if he swore not to eat from his son's property, and between him and his son there was a jar of vinegar, and he ate from it, he breaks the oath because he ate from the son's property. It is then necessary to distinguish between food and property, as is not hidden. Also in al-Waqi'at: He said 'If I eat anything of So-and-so's property, my wife is divorced', so he was given dough from his in-law, and he put it in other dough, baked it, and ate it – he breaks the oath because the dough has disappeared. Similarly, if he swore not to drink from his drink nor eat from his meat, and he took water and salt belonging to the person sworn about, and put them in dough, he does not break the oath if he eats from that bread because that has disintegrated. And if he swore not to eat from So-and-so's earnings, and he ate a piece of bread thrown in the house of the person sworn about – if the piece is such that a beggar would not be given its like, he does not break the oath; but if it is such that a beggar would be given its like, he breaks the oath. Then know that what is in al-Mukhtasar is only when there is no intention; but if he intends something, it is according to his intention because his speech is probable. In al-Dhakhira: He swore not to eat from So-and-so's grinding of his bread – this applies to past and future; similarly his saying 'from what So-and-so baked' or 'from what So-and-so bought' applies to past and future.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage collects several illustrative rulings about oaths related to food, property, and earnings. The first ruling: if one swears not to cultivate So-and-so's land, but cultivates land that is jointly owned by the swearer and another, the oath is broken. The reasoning is that every part of the land is called 'land' (so the land as a whole is present), whereas in the case of a house or garment, parts are not called by the whole name (e.g., a brick is not called a house). This distinction matters in oaths about property. Then from al-Waqi'at: if one swears not to eat meat that So-and-so buys, and So-and-so buys a lamb and slaughters it, the meat is not considered 'bought' after slaughter because the purchase was of the live animal, so eating it does not break the oath. Next, if one swears not to eat from So-and-so's food, but adds his own food, oil, or salt to So-and-so's vinegar, he still breaks the oath because the base food (vinegar) belongs to So-and-so. Similarly, if a father swears not to eat from his son's property, and they share a jar of vinegar, eating from it breaks the oath because it includes the son's share. The commentator notes the need to distinguish between 'food' and 'property' — here both involve consumption, but property includes any owned item. Another case: if one swears that eating any of So-and-so's property triggers divorce, and he receives dough from his in-law and mixes it with other dough, he breaks the oath because the original dough is no longer distinct. But if he takes water and salt belonging to the person and puts them in dough, he does not break the oath because those ingredients have disintegrated and are no longer identifiable. For earnings: if he eats a discarded bread piece in the house of the person sworn about, it depends on whether the piece is considered of significant value such that a beggar would accept it; if yes, it breaks the oath; if no, it does not. Finally, a general principle: these rulings apply only when the swearer has no specific intention; if he intends something particular, that governs. For example, swearing not to eat from So-and-so's grinding of his bread applies to both past and future actions (the grinding is a present attribute). The same for baking or buying — the oath covers all instances.

Key Terms
أرض (arḍ): Land; in this context, every part of it is called land.
دار (dār): House; parts of a house are not called house.
ثوب (thawb): Garment; similarly, parts are not called garment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person swears not to cultivate Zaid's land, and then cultivates a small portion of a field co-owned by Zaid and another, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because every part of the land is called land.
B) No, because he only cultivated a small part.
C) Yes, but only if he cultivated the part belonging entirely to Zaid.
D) No, because the land is co-owned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths on property, naming parts is crucial: for land, every part is land; for houses and garments, parts are not the whole.

Oaths on Food and Earnings: Mixed Ingredients, Disintegration, and Intention
Rulings on oaths regarding food, earnings, and mixed items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rulings on oath-breaking when food or property is mixed, disintegrated, or has nominal value, and understand the role of intention in oaths.

Core Idea
Adding one's own ingredients does not avoid breaking an oath if the base belongs to the other; disintegration removes the breach.

Oaths concerning food and earnings often involve complex scenarios where ownership of ingredients is shared or where items lose their identity. The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance on when mixing, disintegration, or low value affects the oath. This lesson explores several cases from al-Waqi'at and al-Dhakhira, culminating in the principle that intention governs when present.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the meat purchase case
An oath not to eat meat bought by So-and-so: if So-and-so buys a live lamb and slaughters it, the meat was not bought as meat; eating it does not break the oath.
Timing of purchase matters: the meat existed after purchase.
2
Examine the vinegar case with added ingredients
Swearing not to eat from So-and-so's food: adding own oil, salt, or food to the vinegar does not negate the breach because the base food (vinegar) is still identifiable.
The added ingredients do not change ownership of the base.
3
Consider joint property (jar of vinegar)
If a father and son share a jar of vinegar, eating from it breaks an oath not to eat from the son's property because it includes his share.
Distinction: 'property' vs 'food' is needed.
4
Apply the disintegration principle
When someone's dough is mixed into other dough and baked, the original is no longer distinct, so the oath is broken (the dough disappeared). However, adding water and salt from the other person to one's own dough does not break the oath because those ingredients disintegrate and lose identity.
Key criterion: whether the original substance is still identifiable.
5
Evaluate the bread piece (value threshold)
For oaths on earnings, a discarded bread piece breaks the oath only if it has sufficient value that a beggar would accept it; otherwise, it does not.
Value is determined by 'urf (customary practice).
6
State the role of intention
All the above rulings apply when there is no specific intention. If the swearer intended something particular, that governs because the wording is probable.
Intention overrides default interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A) No, because he added his own ingredients.
B) Yes, because he ate from So-and-so's food (the vinegar).

Default Durations for Time Terms in Oaths (Zaman, Hin, Dahr, etc.)
Rulings on the default durations for indefinite time terms in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default durations for indefinite time expressions (zaman, hin, dahr, ayyam, etc.) in Hanafi oaths and apply the principle of defaulting to the middle meaning when no intention is specified.

Core Idea
When an oath uses an ambiguous time term, Hanafi law defaults to the middle duration—six months for 'hin' and 'zaman'—unless the swearer had a specific intention.

The matn states clear defaults for indefinite time terms in oaths. The commentary explains the reasoning by citing Quranic uses of 'hin' and establishing that the default is the middle meaning. This lesson will walk through these defaults and their proofs, enabling you to apply them to real oath scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the time term
Recognize the specific time expression used in the oath (e.g., 'hin', 'zaman', 'dahr', 'ayyam', etc.). Determine if it is a singular indefinite (nakira) or plural/compound form.
Singular 'hin' defaults to six months; 'dahr' is ambiguous (mujmal).
2
Check for intention (niyyah)
If the swearer had a specific intention regarding the duration, that intention overrides the default. The default only applies when no intention is present.
Intention is considered the true meaning of the speaker's words.
3
Apply the default duration
For terms without intention: 'zaman' and 'hin' (indefinite) = 6 months; plural forms like 'al-ahayyin' = 10 times 6 months; 'ayyam kathirah' = 10; nakira of 'yaum' = 3; 'kadha wa kadha yawman' = 21; 'kadha kadha' = 11; 'bid'ata 'ashara' = 13; seasons determined by local custom; first part of month up to before half (15th inclusive/exclusive debated).
The defaults are derived from Quranic usage and lexicography.
4
Understand the Quranic proof for 'hin'
The word 'hin' appears in three Quranic verses: short (evening, 30:17), long (40 years, 76:1), and medium (6 months per tafsir of 'kulla hin' in 14:25). The Hanafis choose the medium because the short is too brief to be meaningful for a prohibition and the long is like eternity.
This is an example of istidlal (inference) from multiple Quranic contexts.
5
Handle special cases and differences
Note the ambiguity of 'dahr' (Abu Hanifa said 'I do not know what it is'), the definition of seasons by local custom, and the disagreement on whether the 15th is part of the first half of the month. Also note that the plural 'ahayyin' defaults to ten periods of six months, etc.
These details show the practical application and internal Hanafi differences.

الزمان والحين منكرهما ستة أشهر والدهر والأبد العمر ودهر مجمل والأيام وأيام كثيرة والشهور والسنون عشرة ومنكرها ثلاثة . قوله: (الزمان والحين منكرهما ستة أشهر) لأن الحين قد يراد به الزمان القليل قال الله تعالى ﴿فسبحان الله حين تمسون﴾ [الروم:١٧] وقد يراد به أربعون سنة قال تعالى ﴿هل أتى على الإنسان حين من الدهر﴾ [الإنسان:١] وقد يراد به ستة أشهر قال تعالى ﴿تؤتي أكلها كل حين﴾ [إبراهيم:٢٥] وهذا هو الوسط فينصرف إليه. وهذا لأن القليل لا يقصد بالمنع لوجود الامتناع فيه عادة والمديد لا يقصد غالباً لأنه بمنزلة الأبد، ولو سكت عنه يتأبد فتعين ما ذكرناه. وكذا الزمان يستعمل استعمال الحين فيقال ما رأيتك منذ حين ومنذ زمان بمعنى واحد. وهذا إذا لم تكن له نية، أما إذا نوى شيئاً فهو على ما نوى لأنه حقيقة كلامه ولا فرق في ذلك بين الزمان والحين وهو الصحيح كما في البدائع. أطلقه فشمل الإثبات والنفي فإذا قال لأصومن حيناً أو الحين فهو كقوله لا أكلمه حيناً أو الحين. وفي فتح القدير ويعتبر ابتداء الستة أشهر من وقت اليمين بخلاف قوله لأصومن حيناً أو زماناً كان له أن يعين أي ستة أشهر شاء وتقدم الفرق ا هـ . وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قال لا أكلمه الأحايين أو الأزمنة بالجمع فهو على عشر مرات ستة أشهر كما في شرح الطحاوي ولو قال لا أكلمه كذا وكذا يوماً فهو على أحد وعشرين يوماً، ولو قال كذا كذا فهو على أحد عشر . ولو حلف لا يكلمه بضعة عشر يوماً فهو على ثلاثة عشر يوماً لأن البضع من ثلاثة إلى تسعة فيحمل على أقلها. ولو حلف لا يكلمه الشتاء فأول ذلك إذا لبس الناس الحشو والفراء وآخره إذا ألقوها في البلد الذي حلف فيه والصيف على ضده وهو من حين القاء الحشو، وإلى لبسه، والربيع آخر الشتاء ومستقبل الصيف إلى أن ييبس العشب، والخريف فصل ما بين الشتاء والصيف، قوله : (وأول الشهر إلى ما دون النصف) ظاهر أن الخامس عشر ليس من أول الشهر. وفي التتارخانية عن المحيط : أول الشهر من اليوم الأول إلى خمسة عشر يوماً وآخر الشهر من اليوم السادس عشر إلى آخر الشهر، وآخر أول الشهر اليوم الخامس عشر، وأول آخر الشهر السادس عشر، وإن كان الشهر تسعة وعشرين يوماً فأول الشهر إلى وقت الزوال من الخامس عشر وما بعده إلى آخر الشهر ا هـ . ومثله في الفتح آخر الباب. وفي البزازية : أول الشهر قبل مضي النصف وعن الثاني فيمن قال لا أكلمك آخر يوم من أول الشهر وأول يوم من آخره فعلى الخامس عشر والسادس عشر ا هـ . وهذا ربما يفيد الخلاف فتأمل قوله : (فقد قال أبو حنيفة لا أدري ما هو) يعني إذا لم يكن له نية كما في البرهان فإن قيل : ذكر في الجامع الكبير أجمعوا فيمن قال إن كلمته دهوراً أو شهوراً أو سنيناً أو جمعاً أو أياماً يقع على ثلاثة من هذه المذكورات، فكيف قال أبو حنيفة لا أدري ما الدهر ؟ قلنا هذا تفريع لمسألة الدهر على قول من يعرف الدهر كما فرع مسائل المزارعة على قول من يرى جوازها قاله ابن الضياء رحمه الله تعالى؛ كذا في الشرنبلالية قوله :
Al-zaman (time) and al-hin (period): their indefinite form is six months. Al-dahr (age) and al-abad (eternity) are ambiguous; al-umr (lifespan) and al-dahr are vague. Al-ayyam (days) and 'ayyam kathirah (many days) and al-shuhur (months) and al-sunun (years) are ten, and their indefinite form is three. His statement: 'Al-zaman and al-hin: their indefinite form is six months' because al-hin can refer to a short time, as Allah said 'So exalt Allah [in prayer] when you enter the evening' (30:17); and it can refer to forty years, as He said 'Has there [not] come upon man a period of time' (76:1); and it can refer to six months, as He said 'It produces its fruit every season' (14:25); and this is the middle, so it is the default. This is because a short period is not intended for prohibition, since abstaining is usually present, and a long period is not usually intended because it is like eternity, and if left unspecified it would become perpetual, so what we mentioned is determined. Likewise, al-zaman is used like al-hin; one says 'I have not seen you for a hin' and 'for a zaman' with the same meaning. This is when he has no intention; if he intends something, then it is according to his intention because it is his actual speech, and there is no difference between al-zaman and al-hin in that regard, and this is the correct view as in al-Bada'i'. He left it general, so it includes affirmation and negation; so if he says 'I will surely fast for a hin' or 'al-hin', it is like his saying 'I will not speak to him for a hin' or 'al-hin'. In Fath al-Qadir, the beginning of the six months is considered from the time of the oath, unlike his saying 'I will surely fast for a hin' or 'zaman' where he can specify any six months he wishes, and the difference has been mentioned. The author indicated that if he says 'I will not speak to him for al-ahayyin' (plural) or 'al-azminah' (plural), it is for ten times of six months, as in Sharh al-Tahawi. If he says 'I will not speak to him for so-and-so days', it is for twenty-one days; if he says 'so-and-so so-and-so', it is for eleven. If he swears not to speak to him for 'bid'ata 'ashara yawman' (a number of days between three and nine plus ten), it is for thirteen days because 'bid' (a number) is from three to nine, so it is taken as the least. If he swears not to speak to him during winter, its beginning is when people wear padded garments and fur, and its end when they remove them in the country where he swore; summer is the opposite, from when they remove padded garments to when they wear them; spring is the end of winter and the beginning of summer until the grass dries; autumn is the season between winter and summer. His statement: 'And the first part of the month is up to less than half' — it is apparent that the fifteenth is not from the first part of the month. In al-Tatar Khaniyya from al-Muhit: the first part of the month is from the first day to fifteen days, and the last part of the month is from the sixteenth day to the end of the month; the end of the first part is the fifteenth day, and the beginning of the last part is the sixteenth; if the month is twenty-nine days, then the first part is up to the time of zawal (noon) of the fifteenth, and what follows is the last part. Similarly in al-Fath at the end of the chapter. In al-Bazzaziyya: the first part of the month is before half has passed; and from the second (Abu Yusuf) regarding one who said 'I will not speak to you on the last day of the first part and the first day of the last part' — it is the fifteenth and sixteenth. This may indicate a difference of opinion, so contemplate. His statement: 'For Abu Hanifa said: I do not know what it is' — meaning when he has no intention, as in al-Burhan. It might be said: It is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Kabir that they agreed that if someone says 'I have spoken to him for dahuran (ages) or shuhuran (months) or sininan (years) or jam'an (plural) or ayyaman (days)', it applies to three of these mentioned; so how can Abu Hanifa say 'I do not know what al-dahr is'? We say: This is a branching of the issue of al-dahr on the view of one who knows al-dahr, just as the issues of muzara'ah are branched on the view of one who considers it permissible; said by Ibn al-Diya' may Allah have mercy on him; thus in al-Sharnablaliyya. His statement:
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage deals with the interpretation of ambiguous time expressions in oaths. The matn states the default meanings: for 'zaman' and 'hin' (indefinite) it is six months; for 'dahr' and 'abad' it is ambiguous (mujmal); for 'ayyam' (days), 'shuhur' (months), and 'sunun' (years) the plural (indefinite number) is ten, and their indefinite singular (nakira) is three. The commentary explains the reasoning for 'zaman' and 'hin': the word 'hin' is used in the Quran with various durations—short (evening), long (forty years), and medium (six months from the parable of a tree producing fruit every season). Since the short is not typically intended for a prohibition (because one would naturally avoid the person for a short while anyway) and the long is like eternity, the medium (six months) is chosen as the default. The same applies to 'zaman'. If the swearer has a specific intention, that overrides the default. Then the passage discusses plural forms: 'al-ahayyin' or 'al-azminah' (plural of hin or zaman) means ten periods of six months (i.e., the number ten is taken as indefinite plural). For numbers like 'kadha wa kadha yawman' (so-and-so days), it defaults to twenty-one; 'kadha kadha' to eleven; 'bid'ata 'ashara' (a number between thirteen and nineteen) defaults to thirteen, taking the minimum of the range (bid' is 3-9, so bid'ata 'ashara = 10+3=13). For seasons, winter is defined by local custom (wearing padded clothes), summer the opposite, spring from end of winter to drying of grass, autumn between winter and summer. For parts of the month, 'first part' is up to the 15th (excluding the 15th according to some, including it according to others). Finally, Abu Hanifa's statement 'I do not know what dahr is' indicates that 'dahr' is ambiguous and cannot be given a fixed default; the ruling branches on the view that 'dahr' is known (i.e., a very long time). This shows the Hanafi method: even an imam may not have a definite opinion on certain terms, and later scholars fill in based on prevailing understanding.

Key Terms
حين (ḥīn): A period of time; in oaths, defaults to six months when indefinite.
زمان (zamān): Time; treated like 'hin' in oaths, default six months.
بضع (biḍ‘): A number from 3 to 9; in oaths, the minimum is taken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not speak to Zaid for a hin' without any intention, how long is the oath valid?
A) One day
B) Forty years
C) Six months
D) Eternity

Key Takeaway
When an oath uses an indefinite time term without intention, the Hanafi default applies: six months for 'hin' and 'zaman', and special rules for other terms, ensuring oaths are enforceable.

Meaning of 'al-Hīn' and 'al-Zamān' in Oaths
Ruling with rational justification on default oath duration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default duration for 'hīn' and 'zamān' in Hanafi oaths, explain the rational justification, and apply the rule to both affirmative and negative oaths, distinguishing the start of the period.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'hīn' and 'zamān' in oaths default to six months, unless intention specifies otherwise.

When someone swears an oath using the word 'hīn' (a time) without specifying the duration, how long is the oath binding? This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqāʾiq explains that the default is six months, based on a rational analysis of customary usage. It also clarifies that intention overrides the default and highlights a key difference between affirmative and negative oaths regarding when the six-month period begins.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is the interpretation of ambiguous time words ('hīn', 'zamān') in oaths where no specific duration is mentioned.
This is a matter of lexical interpretation (tafsīr) within oath jurisprudence.
2
State the default ruling
Without intention, the default duration is six months for both 'hīn' and 'zamān'. This is the established Hanafi position.
The text explicitly says 'wa hādhā hwa al-wasaṭ fa-yanṣarif ilayh'.
3
Analyze the rational justification
The text gives a rational argument (qiyas) based on customary usage: a very short period is not usually intended (because one naturally avoids for a short time), and a very long period is like eternity and also not intended. The middle (six months) is the default.
This is a dalil aqli (rational evidence) rooted in urf (custom).
4
Recognize the role of intention
If the oath-taker had a specific intention for the duration, that intention governs because it represents the true meaning of his speech. The default only applies when there is no intention.
The text cites al-Badā'i' as support: 'wa hwa al-ṣaḥīḥ ka-mā fī al-badā’i‘'.
5
Apply the distinction from Fatḥ al-Qadīr
In a negative oath (e.g., 'I will not speak to you for a hīn'), the six months count from the moment of the oath. In a positive oath (e.g., 'I will fast for a hīn'), the person may choose any six-month period. This difference stems from the nature of the act: forbearance is immediate while performance can be deferred.
The text explicitly mentions this contrast: 'wa yu‘tabaru ibtidā’ al-sittah ashhur min waqt al-yamīn bikhilāf qawlihi la-aṣūmanna ḥīnan...'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا هو الوسط فينصرف إليه. وهذا لأن القليل لا يقصد بالمنع لوجود الامتناع فيه عادة والمديد لا يقصد غالباً لأنه بمنزلة الأبد، ولو سكت عنه يتأبد فتعين ما ذكرناه. وكذا الزمان يستعمل استعمال الحين فيقال ما رأيتك منذ حين ومنذ زمان بمعنى واحد. وهذا إذا لم تكن له نية، أما إذا نوى شيئاً فهو على ما نوى لأنه حقيقة كلامه ولا فرق في ذلك بين الزمان والحين وهو الصحيح كما في البدائع. أطلقه فشمل الإثبات والنفي فإذا قال لأصومن حيناً أو الحين فهو كقوله لا أكلمه حيناً أو الحين. وفي فتح القدير ويعتبر ابتداء الستة أشهر من وقت اليمين بخلاف قوله لأصومن حيناً أو زماناً كان له أن يعين أي ستة أشهر شاء وتقدم الفرق ا هـ .
And this is the middle [amount], so it is referred to it. This is because the little is not intended for prohibition because abstention from it is usually present, and the lengthy is not usually intended because it is like eternity, and if he remained silent about it, it would become eternal, so what we mentioned is determined. Similarly, 'zamān' is used like 'hīn'; one says 'I have not seen you since a hīn' and 'since a zamān' with the same meaning. This is when he has no intention. But if he intends something, then it is according to his intention because it is the reality of his speech. There is no difference in this between 'zamān' and 'hīn', and this is the correct view as in al-Badā'i'. He made it general so it includes affirmation and negation. So if he says 'I will surely fast a hīn' or 'al-hīn', it is like his saying 'I will not speak to him a hīn' or 'al-hīn'. In Fatḥ al-Qadīr: the beginning of the six months is counted from the time of the oath, contrary to his saying 'I will surely fast a hīn' or 'zamān' where he has the choice to specify any six months he wishes, and the difference was mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with rational justification from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqā'iq. The author explains that when someone swears an oath using the word 'hīn' (a period) without specifying a number, the default meaning is a middle duration, which is six months. This is because a very short period is not usually intended for avoiding, as abstention is already present, and a very long period is like eternity and not intended. Thus, six months is the moderate default. The same applies to 'zamān' (time), as it is used synonymously with 'hīn'. However, if the oath-taker has a specific intention, that intention governs because it is the true meaning of his speech. This rule applies equally to affirmative and negative oaths. The author notes a distinction from Fatḥ al-Qadīr: in a negative oath (e.g., 'I will not speak to him for a hīn'), the six months start from the time of the oath; in a positive oath (e.g., 'I will fast for a hīn'), the person may choose any six-month period. This difference hinges on the nature of the act—fasting can be delayed, while not speaking is immediate.

Key Terms
الحين (al-ḥīn): A period of time that defaults to six months in oaths when no intention is specified.
الزمان (al-zamān): Synonymous with al-ḥīn in oaths, also defaults to six months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not speak to you for a hīn' without any intention, how long is the oath binding?
A) One day
B) Six months
C) One year
D) Until he dies

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the words 'hīn' and 'zamān' in oaths are interpreted as six months by default, subject to the oath-taker's intention and the nature of the oath (affirmative/negative).

Default Interpretations of Plural Time Words and Numbers in Oaths
Rulings on interpreting ambiguous time and number expressions in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi interpretation of plural time words (al-aḥāyīn, al-azminah) and numerical expressions (kadhā wa kadhā, biḍ'ata 'ashara) in oaths.

Core Idea
When an oath uses a plural time word or an indefinite number, the Hanafi school assigns the minimum possible interpretation.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq, we learn how the Hanafi school interprets certain ambiguous expressions in oaths. The author provides default rulings for plural time words like 'aḥāyīn' and 'azminah', and for numerical expressions such as 'kadhā wa kadhā' and 'biḍ'ata ʿashara'. These rulings ensure that the oath is understood in a specific, enforceable manner when the swearer's intent is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression
Determine which ambiguous expression the swearer used: plural time word (aḥāyīn/azminah) or numerical phrase (kadhā wa kadhā, kadhā kadhā, biḍ'ata ʿashara).
The passage covers four distinct expressions.
2
Apply the default interpretation
For each expression, recall the Hanafi default interpretation: aḥāyīn = ten periods of six months; kadhā wa kadhā = 21 days; kadhā kadhā = 11 days; biḍ'ata ʿashara = 13 days.
These defaults apply when the swearer did not specify a different intent.
3
Understand the reasoning
Note the reasoning: plurals imply a minimum of three? Actually, for aḥāyīn, it's ten periods because 'ḥīn' is six months and plural 'aḥāyīn' is interpreted as ten according to the commentary. For kadhā wa kadhā, the repetition suggests two piles, each at least ten? The text says 21. For biḍ'ata ʿashara, 'biḍʿ' ranges from 3-9, so the minimum (3) is taken, giving 13.
The Hanafi school prioritizes certainty by taking the smallest possible number.
4
Apply to a new oath
Given a new oath using one of these expressions, state the default duration without relying on the swearer's intent.
If the swearer actually meant a different number, the default still binds unless a genuine excuse or interpretation is provided.

وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قال لا أكلمه الأحايين أو الأزمنة بالجمع فهو على عشر مرات ستة أشهر كما في شرح الطحاوي ولو قال لا أكلمه كذا وكذا يوماً فهو على أحد وعشرين يوماً، ولو قال كذا كذا فهو على أحد عشر . ولو حلف لا يكلمه بضعة عشر يوماً فهو على ثلاثة عشر يوماً لأن البضع من ثلاثة إلى تسعة فيحمل على أقلها.
And the author indicated that if he said 'I will not speak to him the aḥāyīn' or 'the azminah' in the plural, then it is upon ten times of six months, as in Sharḥ al-Ṭaḥāwī. And if he said 'I will not speak to him kadhā wa kadhā yawman', then it is upon twenty-one days. And if he said 'kadhā kadhā', then it is upon eleven days. And if he swore not to speak to him for 'biḍ'ata 'ashara yawman', then it is upon thirteen days, because 'biḍ'' is from three to nine, so it is interpreted as the least of them.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage states rulings from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqā'iq regarding oaths using plural time words or specific numerical expressions. When someone swears using the plural of 'hīn' (aḥāyīn) or 'zamān' (azminah), it is interpreted as ten occurrences of six months each, i.e., a total of five years. For the expression 'kadhā wa kadhā yawman' (such and such days), it is taken as twenty-one days, because the smallest number that can be expressed by repetition (kadhā wa kadhā) is twenty-one. Similarly, 'kadhā kadhā' (without the conjunction) is eleven days. For 'biḍ'ata 'ashara yawman' (a dozen-odd days), since 'biḍ'' ranges from three to nine, the minimum (three) is adopted, making it thirteen days. These are default interpretations without intention.

Key Terms
الأحايين (al-aḥāyīn): The plural of 'ḥīn', interpreted as ten periods of six months in oaths.
بضعة عشر (biḍ'ata 'ashara): A number between thirteen and nineteen inclusive, interpreted as the minimum (thirteen) in oaths.
الأزمنة (al-azminah): The plural of 'zamān', interpreted the same as aḥāyīn in oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not speak to you for azminah' (plural of zamān), how long is the oath?
A) 6 months
B) 5 years
C) 10 days
D) 21 days

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, ambiguous time plurals and indefinite numbers are given a fixed minimum default to ensure enforceability.

Defining Seasons for Oaths by Custom
Defines seasons by custom for oath validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the start and end of each season according to the Hanafi custom-based method for oath interpretation, using both clothing and foliage markers.

Core Idea
Seasons for oaths are defined by local custom, not astronomical dates.

When someone swears an oath involving a season like winter or spring, how do we know exactly when that season begins and ends? The Hanafi school does not rely solely on astronomical calendars but looks to observable local customs. In this lesson, we examine two methods: one based on clothing (padded clothes and fur), and another based on tree conditions (leaves and fruits). The preferred view is the latter, as it is more precise and consistent across regions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Recognize that the oath involves a specific season (e.g., winter). The legal question is: when does that season start and end for oath purposes?
The oath is valid; we are determining its temporal scope.
2
Recognize the primary method: local custom
The Hanafi school defaults to 'urf (custom). The beginning of winter is when people in that locality start wearing padded clothes (al-ḥashw) and fur, and the end when they discard them.
This method is location-specific.
3
Understand the alternative: tree-based definition
Another method defines seasons by the presence or absence of leaves and fruits on trees. Summer: leaves and fruits; autumn: leaves only; winter: neither; spring: leaves but no fruits.
This is considered more precise and less variable.
4
Apply the preferred view
According to al-Khāniyyah and other authorities, the tree-based definition is the most accurate and comprehensive. It should be applied unless the locality has a customary astronomical calculation.
The preferred view is used for fatwa.
5
Consider special cases
If the oath-taker is in a country that uses a fixed calendar for seasons, that calendar is followed. Custom may also vary slightly in timing but not in essence.
Exception when fixed custom exists.

ولو حلف لا يكلمه الشتاء فأول ذلك إذا لبس الناس الحشو والفراء وآخره إذا ألقوها في البلد الذي حلف فيه والصيف على ضده وهو من حين القاء الحشو، وإلى لبسه، والربيع آخر الشتاء ومستقبل الصيف إلى أن العشب، والخريف فصل ما الشتاء والصيف، يابس العشب. قوله : (ولو حلف لا يكلمه الشتاء الخ قال بعضهم : الصيف ما يكون على الأشجار الورق والثمار، والخريف ما يكون على الأشجار الأوراق دون الثمار والشتاء ما لا يكون على الأشجار الثمار والأوراق، والربيع ما يخرج من الأشجار الأوراق ولا يخرج الثمار . وفي الخانية : وهذا أقرب الأقاويل إلى الضبط والإحاطة وقلما يختلف باختلاف البلدان إلا أنه يتقدم في البعض ويتأخر في البعض. وفي الصغرى والمختار إذا كان الحالف في بلد لهم حساب يعرفون الصيف والشتاء بالحساب مستمراً يصرف إليه ؛ كذا في التتارخانية
And if he swore not to speak to him during winter, then its beginning is when people wear padded clothes and fur, and its end is when they discard them in the country where he swore. And summer is the opposite of it, from the time of discarding padded clothes until wearing them. And spring is the end of winter and the beginning of summer until grass dries. And autumn is the season between winter and summer, when grass dries. His statement: 'And if he swore not to speak to him during winter, etc.' Some said: Summer is when trees have leaves and fruits, autumn is when trees have leaves without fruits, winter is when trees have neither fruits nor leaves, and spring is when trees put forth leaves but not fruits. In al-Khāniyyah: This is the closest of the sayings to accuracy and comprehensiveness, and it rarely differs by country except that it comes early in some and late in others. In al-Ṣughrā and al-Mukhtār: If the oath-taker is in a country where they have a customary calculation by which they consistently know summer and winter, then it is referred to that; as in al-Tātārkhāniyyah.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage from the commentary defines the seasons for the purpose of interpreting oaths. For winter, the beginning is marked by common practice: when people start wearing padded clothes (ḥashw) and fur (furā'), and the end when they discard them in the locality of the oath. Summer is the reverse: from discarding padded clothes to wearing them. Spring is the transition from winter's end to summer's start, lasting until grass dries. Autumn is the interval between summer and winter when grass is dry. An alternative definition based on tree foliage is also given: summer: leaves and fruits; autumn: leaves only; winter: no leaves or fruits; spring: leaves but no fruits. The preferred view (in al-Khāniyyah) is that this foliage-based definition is more precise and varies little by region, although timing may differ. If the locality has a fixed astronomical calendar for seasons, that is to be followed.

Key Terms
الشتاء (al-shatā'): Winter, defined by customary clothing or tree conditions for oath purposes.
الحشو (al-ḥashw): Padded clothing, used as a marker for the start of winter.
الفراء (al-furā'): Fur clothing, used as a marker for the start of winter.
الربيع (al-rabī'): Spring, defined by the end of winter to the drying of grass, or by trees putting forth leaves but no fruits.
الخريف (al-kharīf): Autumn, the season between summer and winter when grass dries, or when trees have leaves but no fruits.
العرف (al-'urf): Custom, the basis for defining seasons in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in al-Khāniyyah, how are seasons best defined for oaths?
A) By astronomical calculations only
B) By the condition of trees (foliage and fruit)
C) By temperature
D) By the position of the sun

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the boundaries of seasons for oaths are determined by local custom, with the foliage-based definition being the most precise.

Defining the First and Last of a Month for Oaths
Defines 'awwal al-shahr' and 'akhir al-shahr' for oath purposes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions for the first and last parts of a month and determine which days fall under each for the purpose of an oath.

Core Idea
The first of the month is up to the 15th (or until noon on the 15th if the month has 29 days); the last starts from the 16th.

Oaths involving months require precise definition of 'first' and 'last'. The Hanafi school sets clear boundaries based on half the month, with an important exception for 29-day months. This lesson clarifies those boundaries and addresses a common nuance from the texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
Recognize that the passage sets definitions for oaths that refer to parts of a month (e.g., 'first of the month', 'last of the month').
These definitions are used when no specific intent is expressed by the swearer.
2
State the primary rule
For a standard 30-day month, the first part is days 1-15, and the last part is days 16-30. The 15th is the last day of the first part, and the 16th is the first day of the last part.
This is the ruling from al-Muhīt and other Hanafi sources.
3
Handle the 29-day month exception
When the month has 29 days, the first part extends only until noon (zawāl) on the 15th. After noon, the time falls into the last part.
This ensures that the first part is exactly half the month (14.5 days).
4
Apply to combined expressions
If someone swears using a phrase like 'the last day of the first of the month and the first day of the last of it', the days in question are the 15th and the 16th respectively.
This example appears in al-Bazzāziyyah.
5
Note the resolution of Abu Hanifa's statement
The passage also addresses why Abu Hanifa said 'I do not know what al-dahr is' – this is not a contradiction but a reference to the indefinite term; for definite intervals like months, the definitions are clear.
This is a separate issue mentioned to avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأول الشهر إلى ما دون النصف) ظاهر أن الخامس عشر ليس من أول الشهر. وفي التتارخانية عن المحيط : أول الشهر من اليوم الأول إلى خمسة عشر يوماً وآخر الشهر من اليوم السادس عشر إلى آخر الشهر، وآخر أول الشهر اليوم الخامس عشر، وأول آخر الشهر السادس عشر، وإن كان الشهر تسعة وعشرين يوماً فأول الشهر إلى وقت الزوال من الخامس عشر وما بعده إلى آخر الشهر ا هـ . ومثله في الفتح آخر الباب. وفي البزازية : أول الشهر قبل مضي النصف وعن الثاني فيمن قال لا أكلمك آخر يوم من أول الشهر وأول يوم من آخره فعلى الخامس عشر والسادس عشر ا هـ . وهذا ربما يفيد الخلاف فتأمل قوله : (فقد قال أبو حنيفة لا أدري ما هو) يعني إذا لم تكن له نية كما في البرهان فإن قيل : ذكر في الجامع الكبير أجمعوا فيمن قال إن كلمته دهوراً أو شهوراً أو سنيناً أو جمعاً أو أياماً يقع على ثلاثة من هذه المذكورات، فكيف قال أبو حنيفة لا أدري ما الدهر ؟ قلنا هذا تفريع لمسألة الدهر على قول من يعرف الدهر كما فرع مسائل المزارعة على قول من يرى جوازها قاله ابن الضياء رحمه الله تعالى؛ كذا في الشرنبلالية
His statement: (And the first of the month is up to less than half). It is apparent that the fifteenth is not part of the first of the month. And in al-Tātārkhāniyyah from al-Muhīṭ: The first of the month is from the first day to fifteen days, and the last of the month is from the sixteenth day to the end of the month. The end of the first of the month is the fifteenth day, and the beginning of the last of the month is the sixteenth. If the month is twenty-nine days, then the first of the month is up to the time of zawāl (noon) on the fifteenth, and what follows it is until the end of the month. The same is in Fatḥ al-Bāb. And in al-Bazzāziyyah: The first of the month is before the passing of half. And from [the second] regarding one who said 'I will not speak to you on the last day of the first of the month and the first day of the last of it', then it is upon the fifteenth and sixteenth. This perhaps indicates a difference of opinion, so consider. His statement: (For Abu Hanifa said: I do not know what it is) is when he has no intention, as in al-Burhān. For if it is said: It is mentioned in al-Jāmi' al-Kabīr that they agreed on the one who says 'If I speak to you for dahr or months or years or a plural or days', it falls on three of these mentioned [numbers]. So how can Abu Hanifa say 'I do not know what al-dahr is'? We say: This is a subdivision of the issue of al-dahr according to the one who knows al-dahr, just as the issues of muzāra'ah are subdivided according to the one who deems it permissible. Said by Ibn al-Ḍiyā' (raḥimahullāh); as in al-Shurunbulāliyyah.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage clarifies what constitutes the first and last parts of a month in the context of oaths. The text from al-Tātārkhāniyyah states: the first of the month is from day 1 to day 15, and the last is from day 16 to the end. The last of the first is day 15, and the first of the last is day 16. For a 29-day month, the first portion extends to noon on the 15th. In al-Bazzāziyyah, the first is before half has passed. If someone swears using combined expressions, the 15th and 16th are specified. The passage then addresses a potential objection regarding Abu Hanifa's statement that he does not know what 'al-dahr' (eternity) means in the indefinite form. The response is that this is a subdivision based on the opinion of those who recognize a meaning for 'dahr', not a contradiction.

Key Terms
أول الشهر (awwal al-shahr): The first part of the month, defined as days 1-15 (or up to noon on the 15th in a 29-day month) for oath purposes.
آخر الشهر (ākhir al-shahr): The last part of the month, from day 16 to the end.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines the first of the month as days 1-15 (or until noon on the 15th in a 29-day month) and the last of the month as days 16-30.

The Meaning of 'al-Dahr' and 'al-Abad' in Oaths: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains the interpretation of 'dahr' and 'abad' in oaths, including Abu Hanifa's stance on indefinite 'dahr'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on indefinite 'dahr' in oaths and explain why Abu Hanifa refrained from defining it while his disciples fixed it at six months.

Core Idea
Indefinite 'dahr' in an oath means six months, per the two disciples—but Abu Hanifa's 'I don't know' is a sign of scholarly caution, not ignorance.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq, we encounter a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: how long does an oath of indefinite duration ('dahr') actually bind? While Abu Hanifa refrained from specifying, his two disciples settled on six months, and their view became the relied-upon position. Understanding their reasoning reveals key principles of Hanafi legal interpretation: the use of linguistic custom ('urf) and the limits of qiyas in language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath phrasing and its grammatical form
Determine whether the oath uses the definite (al-dahr, al-abad) or indefinite (dahr, abad) form. The ruling depends on this distinction.
The passage explicitly contrasts definite and indefinite forms.
2
Note the agreed meaning for definite forms
Both 'al-dahr' and 'al-abad' in the definite form, as well as indefinite 'abad', mean the lifespan of the oath-taker. This is undisputed.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute on indefinite 'dahr'
Abu Hanifa said 'I do not know' what indefinite 'dahr' means, while his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it means six months, like 'hīn'.
This is a classic ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Examine the disciples' dalil (evidence)
The disciples rely on linguistic usage: 'dahr' is used in Arabic like 'hīn' and 'zamān', e.g., 'I have not seen him since a dahr' meaning a long but finite time. They analogize it to 'hīn', which is six months by default.
Their proof is based on common usage and qiyas.
5
Understand Abu Hanifa's rationale and the tarjih
Abu Hanifa paused because languages are not known by analogy, and custom varied. His pause is considered perfection ('al-tawaqquf min al-kamāl'). The text declares the disciples' view as the correct one (al-ṣaḥīḥ), and it is the mufta bihi.
The tarjih is based on the strength of linguistic evidence and consistent custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والدهر والأبد العمر ودهر محمل) يعني لو حلف لا يكلمه الدهر معرفاً أو الأبد معرفاً أو منكراً فهو العمر أي مدة حياة الحالف، وأما الدهر منكراً فقد قال أبو حنيفة : لا أدري ما هو. وقالا هو كالحين وهذا هو الصحيح خلافاً لما يقوله بعضهم من أن الاختلاف بينهم في العرف أيضاً. لهما أن دهراً يستعمل استعمال الحين والزمان يقال ما رأيته منذ دهر ومنذ حين بمعنى واحد. وأبو حنيفة توقف في تقديره لأن اللغات لا تدرك قياساً والعرف لم يعرف استمراره لاختلاف في الاستعمال والتوقف عند عدم المرجح من الكمال، وقد توقف أبو جنيفة في أربعة عشر مسألة كما في السراج الوهاج وقد نقل لا أدري عن الأئمة الأربعة بل عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وعن جبريل عليه السلام كما في الشرح. وبهذا علم أن العلم بجميع المسائل الشرعية ليس بشرط في الفقيه أي المجتهد لأن الشرط التهيؤ القريب كما بيناه أول الكتاب. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قال لا أكلمه العمر فهو على الأبد. واختلف جواب بشر بن الوليد في المنكر نحو عمراً فمرة قال في الله علي صوم عمر يقع على يوم واحد، ومرة قال هو مثل الحين ستة أشهر إلا أن ينوي أقل أو أكثر. وفي البدائع الأظهر أنه يقع على ستة أشهر .
His statement: (And al-dahr and al-abad are the lifespan, and dahr in the indefinite form is problematic) means: if he swears not to speak to him using 'al-dahr' (definite) or 'al-abad' (definite) or in the indefinite form, it means the lifespan, i.e., the duration of the oath-taker's life. As for 'dahr' in the indefinite form, Abu Hanifa said: 'I do not know what it is.' The two disciples said it is like 'hīn' (six months). And this is the correct view, contrary to what some say that the difference between them is also in custom. For them (the two disciples): 'dahr' is used like 'hīn' and 'zamān'; one says 'I have not seen him since a dahr' and 'since a hīn' with the same meaning. Abu Hanifa paused in estimating it because languages are not known by analogy, and custom did not confirm its usage due to variation, and pausing when there is no preponderant factor is from perfection. And Abu Hanifa paused in fourteen issues as in al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj, and 'I do not know' has been narrated from the four imams, indeed from the Prophet (peace be upon him) and from Jibrīl (peace be upon him) as in the commentary. By this, it is known that knowledge of all religious issues is not a condition for the faqīh (mujtahid), because the condition is proximate readiness, as we explained at the beginning of the book. And the author indicated that if he says 'I will not speak to him for al-'umr', it is like al-abad. And the answer of Bishr ibn al-Walīd differed regarding the indefinite form like 'umran': sometimes he said if he vows 'upon me is to fast for 'umr' it falls on one day, and sometimes he said it is like 'hīn' (six months) unless he intends less or more. And in al-Badā'i', the most apparent is that it falls on six months.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage contains an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding the meaning of 'dahr' (time/eternity) in oaths. When used in the definite form ('al-dahr') or 'al-abad' (eternity), or even in the indefinite form, the agreed meaning is the lifespan of the oath-taker. However, for indefinite 'dahr' (dahr), Abu Hanifa refrained from defining it, saying 'I do not know what it is.' The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) held that it is like 'hīn', meaning six months. This is considered the correct view (al-ṣaḥīḥ). The disciples' proof is that 'dahr' is used linguistically like 'hīn' and 'zamān'. Abu Hanifa's reluctance is because language is not derived by analogy, and custom varies, so pausing when there is no clear evidence is a sign of perfection. He paused in fourteen similar issues. The passage also clarifies that a scholar (mujtahid) need not know every ruling, only have the capacity. Additionally, the indefinite 'umr' (age) is treated like 'abad' (eternity) in oaths, and Bishr's rulings on indefinite 'umr' vary, but the predominant view (in al-Badā'i') is six months.

Key Terms
الدهر (al-dahr): In the definite form, it means the lifespan of the oath-taker. In the indefinite form, the two disciples interpret it as six months; Abu Hanifa does not specify.
الأبد (al-abad): Eternity or lifespan, interpreted as the remainder of the oath-taker's life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what does indefinite 'dahr' mean in an oath?
A) One year
B) The entire lifespan
C) Six months
D) It is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, indefinite 'dahr' in oaths is interpreted as six months, based on the authoritative view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad ibn al-Hasan.
