Definite Plurals in Oaths: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the minimum number for definite plurals in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the differing Hanafi views on the minimum number implied by definite plural terms in oaths and apply the ruling to scenarios involving 'al-ayyam', 'al-shuhur', 'al-sinin', and 'al-jam''.

Core Idea
The definite article in plural oaths: Abu Hanifa sets the default at ten; the Sahibayn tailor it to common usage.

When a person swears using a definite plural like 'the days' or 'the months', what commitment have they made? Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students differ sharply. This lesson unpacks their linguistic arguments and shows how the ruling changes based on the term used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definite plural terms in the oath
Note the words used: al-ayyam, al-shuhur, al-sinin, al-jam', etc. They all have the definite article 'al'.
The text covers multiple terms.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that such plurals imply ten of whatever is counted, because the definite article for a known (ahd) refers to the farthest known true plural which is ten.
He considers the linguistic norm: plural in Arabic goes from three to ten.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their reasoning
For al-ayyam, they say the known is the week (seven days). For al-shuhur, the known is the year (twelve months). For other plurals like al-sinin, al-jam', al-duhur, they say it means the entire lifespan because the definite article here indicates generality (istighraq) as no specific known is available.
They differentiate based on common usage: 'the days' often means the week, 'the months' means a year.
4
Analyze the core linguistic argument
Central to the dispute is the function of 'al' for 'ahd (known reference). Abu Hanifa says the known for all plurals is the maximum range of the true plural (ten). The Sahibayn say the known varies by term.
This is a difference in usul al-fiqh methodology.
5
Apply the ruling to a sample case
If someone swears 'Wallahi la ukallimuka al-ayyam' without intention, what is the scope of the oath? According to Abu Hanifa, he may not speak for ten days. According to the Sahibayn, he may not speak for seven days (a week).
This has a direct practical impact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والأيام وأيام كثيرة والشهور والسنون عشرة ومنكرها ثلاثة) بيان لأقل الجمع في باب الأيمان وهو على وجهين : إما أن يكون معرفاً أو منكراً ، فإذا كان معرفاً كما إذا حلف لا يكلمه الأيام أو الجمع أو الشهور أو السنين انصرف إلى عشرة من تلك المعدودات، وكذلك لا يكلمه الأزمنة انصرف إلى خمس سنين لأن كل زمان ستة أشهر عند عدم النية . وهذا كله عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا في الأيام ينصرف إلى أيام الأسبوع، وفي الشهور إلى اثني عشر شهراً، وفي الجمع والسنين والدهور والأزمنة إلى الأبد لأن اللام للعهد إذا أمكن، وإن لم يمكن فهي للاستغراق والعهد ثابت في الأيام والشهور كما ذكرنا ولا عهد في خصوص ما سواهما فكان للاستغراق وهو استغراق سني العمر وجمعه وله أنه جمع معرف باللام فينصرف إلى أقصى ما عهد مستعملاً فيه لفظ الجمع على اليقين وهو عشرة لأنه يقال ثلاثة رجال وأربعة رجال إلى عشرة رجال، فإذا جاوز العشرة ذهب الجمع فيقال عشر رجلاً إلى آخره. وإنما اعتبر أقصى المعهود وإن كان ما دونه معهود أيضاً لأنها لاستغراق المعهود لأن المعهود كل مرتبة من المراتب التي أولها ثلاثة وأقصاها عشرة ولا معين. فالحاصل أنهم اتفقوا على أنها للعهد لكن اختلفوا في المعهود فهما قالا المعهود الأسبوع والسنة، وهو قال العشرة نظراً إلى أنها أقصى المعهود، وقد أطال في فتح القدير في بيانه إطالة حسنة وتعرض للرد على ابن العز ولسنا بصدد ذلك. وفي الذخيرة : لو قال والله لا أكلمك الجمع ولا نية له فله أن يكلمه في غير يوم الجمعة لأن الجمع جمع جمعة وهو اسم خاص لليوم الذي تقام فيه الجمعة، به لاجتماع الناس سمي . فيه لإقامة هذا الأمر فيه فلا يتناول غيره من الأيام كما لو قال لا أكلمك إلا خمسة والآحاد والأثانين، وإن نوى أيام الجمعة نفس الأسبوع فهو على ما نوى . وذكر في النوادر أن من قال علي صوم جمعة إن نوى يوم الجمعة يلزمه صوم يوم الجمعة لا غير، وإن نوى أيام الجمعة يعني الأسبوع أو لم تكن له نية يلزمه صوم الأيام السبعة بحكم غلبة الاستعمال يقول الرجل لغيره لم أراك منذ جمعة، فعلى رواية النوادر صرف الجمعة إلى أيامها دون يوم الجمعة خاصة، وعلى رواية الجامع الصغير صرف الجمعة المطلقة غير مقرونة باليوم إلى يوم الجمعة خاصة لأن هذا الاستعمال فيما إذا ذكرت الجمعة مطلقة بلفظ الواحد أي لا بلفظ الجمع حتى قال مشايخنا : إذا قال والله لا أكلمك جمعة ينصرف اليمين إلى الأيام السبعة لا إلى يوم الجمعة خاصة، كما ذكر في النوادر ا هـ . فتبين بهذا أنه إذا حلف لا يكلمه الجمع يترك كلامه عشرة أيام كل يوم هو يوم الجمعة لا أنه يترك كلامه عشرة أسابيع كما قد يتوهم . قال في التبيين : ثم الجمع معرفاً ومنكراً يقع على أيام الجمعة في المدة وله أن يكلمه فيما بين الجمعات. وأما الجمع المنكر فذكر المصنف أنه إن وصفه بالكثرة فهو كالمعرف كقوله لا أكلمه أياماً كثيرة لأنه لما وصفه بالكثرة علم أنه لم يرد به الأقل وهو ا فينصرف إلى المعهود كالمعرف باللام، فعنده للعشرة، وعندهما للأسبوع. وعلى هذا لو قال إن خدمتني أياماً كثيرة فأنت حر فعنده للعشرة، وعندهما للأسبوع، وإن لم يصفه بالكثرة انصرف إلى ثلاثة على ما ذكر في الجامع من غير خلاف وهو الصحيح لأنه ذكر لفظ الجمع منكراً فيقع على أدنى الجمع الصحيح وهو ثلاثة. وذكر في الأصل أنه على عشرة أيام وسوى بين منكر الأيام ومعرفها بخلاف السنين منكراً فإنه على ثلاثة اتفاقاً كما في البدائع .
He said: (And al-ayyam, and ayyam kathirah, and al-shuhur, and al-sinin are ten, and their indefinite is three). An explanation of the minimum of the plural in the chapter of oaths, and it is of two types: either definite or indefinite. If definite, as if he swore not to speak to him 'al-ayyam' or 'al-jam'' or 'al-shuhur' or 'al-sinin', it turns to ten of those numbers. Similarly, 'al-azminah' turns to five years because each time (zaman) is six months when there is no intention. All this according to Abu Hanifa. And the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: regarding 'al-ayyam' it turns to the days of the week, regarding 'al-shuhur' to twelve months, and regarding 'al-jam'', 'al-sinin', 'al-duhur', and 'al-azminah' it turns to forever (al-abad) because the 'lam' is for the known (al-'ahd) if possible, and if not, it is for generality (al-istighraq). And there is a known (ahd) in 'al-ayyam' and 'al-shuhur' as we mentioned, and there is no known in anything else besides them, so it is for generality, which is the generality of the years of one's age and its entirety. His proof is that it is a plural definite with the lam, so it turns to the farthest extent that the word 'plural' is definitely used, which is ten, because one says 'three men, four men... up to ten men'. When it exceeds ten, the plural is lost, so one says 'ten men' etc. He considered the farthest known even if what is less than it is also known, because it is for the generality of the known, because the known is every level from three to ten, and there is no specific one. In summary, they agreed that the lam is for 'ahd but differed in what is 'ahd. The two said the 'ahd is the week and the year, and he (Abu Hanifa) said it is ten, considering it the farthest known. He expanded in Fath al-Qadir beautifully and addressed refuting Ibn al-'Izz, but we are not engaged in that. And in al-Dhakhirah: If he said 'Wallahi la ukallimuka al-jam'' without intention, he may speak to him on other than Friday because 'al-jam'' is the plural of 'jumu'ah' and is a specific name for the day on which the Friday prayer is held, named for the gathering. So it does not include other days, as if he said 'I will not speak to you except on five days, Sundays and Mondays.' If he intends the days of the week (the entire week), it is according to his intention. And in al-Nawadir it is mentioned that if someone says 'upon me is the fast of a Jumu'ah', if he intends Friday, he must fast Friday only; if he intends the days of Jumu'ah (i.e., the week) or has no intention, he must fast the seven days due to prevalent usage: a man says to another 'I haven't seen you for a Jumu'ah'. According to the narration of al-Nawadir, 'Jumu'ah' is turned to its days (week) not specifically Friday. According to the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, the absolute 'Jumu'ah' not coupled with 'day' is turned specifically to Friday, because this usage is when 'Jumu'ah' is mentioned absolutely in the singular form, not plural. Our teachers said: if he says 'Wallahi la ukallimuka jumu'ah', the oath turns to the seven days, not specifically Friday, as mentioned in al-Nawadir. It becomes clear from this that if he swore not to speak to him 'al-jam'', he leaves his speech for ten days each being Friday, not that he leaves his speech for ten weeks as might be imagined. He said in al-Tabyin: Then 'al-jam'' definite and indefinite applies to the days of Friday within the period, and he may speak to him between the Fridays. As for the indefinite 'jam'' (without article), the author mentioned that if he describes it with 'kathirah' (many), it is like definite, as if he says 'la ukallimuhu ayyaman kathirah' because when he described it with abundance, it is known that he did not intend the minimum, so it turns to the known like the definite with lam. According to him (Abu Hanifa) it is ten, according to them (Sahibayn) it is the week. Similarly, if he says 'if you serve me many days, you are free', according to him it is ten, according to them it is the week. And if he did not describe it with abundance, it turns to three, as mentioned in al-Jami' without disagreement, and it is correct, because he mentioned the plural indefinite, so it applies to the minimum correct plural which is three. In al-Asl it is mentioned that it is ten days, and he equated indefinite 'ayyam' with definite 'ayyam', unlike indefinite 'sinin' (years) which is three by agreement, as in al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage explains the minimum number implied by plural terms in oaths. The matn states that definite plurals like 'al-ayyam', 'al-shuhur', 'al-sinin' refer to ten, while indefinite ones refer to three. The commentator elaborates that this is the view of Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn disagree: for 'al-ayyam' they say it refers to the days of the week (seven), for 'al-shuhur' to twelve months, and for other definite plurals like 'al-jam'', 'al-sinin', 'al-duhur', 'al-azminah' they say it means the entire lifespan because the definite article implies generality if no specific known is available. Abu Hanifa argues that the definite article ('al') here refers to the known ('ahd), and the farthest known that a plural can refer to is ten, since Arabic plurals in their true form go up to ten. The Sahibayn say the known is the week for days and the year for months, because those are commonly referred to as 'the days' and 'the months'. The commentator also discusses the word 'al-jam'' (the assembly) which specifically refers to Friday, so an oath not to speak on 'al-jam'' means not to speak on the Friday itself, but the plural 'al-jam'' (with al) can also refer to all Fridays. He then cites al-Nawadir and al-Jami' al-Saghir on the word 'jumu'ah' (Friday) used indefinitely: according to al-Nawadir, it means a week (seven days), but according to al-Jami' al-Saghir, it means the single day of Friday. The relied-upon view is that indefinite 'jumu'ah' in oaths refers to the seven days. Next, the commentator addresses the case where the plural is qualified with 'kathirah' (many). Such qualification removes the uncertainty, so it behaves like a definite plural: according to Abu Hanifa it refers to ten, according to the Sahibayn to a week. Without qualification, the indefinite plural ('ayyam' without description) refers to three, and this is agreed upon. For indefinite 'sinin' (years), the minimum is three by consensus.

Key Terms
الجمع المعرف (al-jam' al-mu'arraf): A plural noun with the definite article; in oaths, it implies a specific number according to context and school.
الجمع المنكر (al-jam' al-munkar): A plural noun without the definite article; implies the minimum (three) unless qualified.
العشرة (al-'ashara): Ten, the maximum true plural in Arabic according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the minimum number implied by 'al-ayyam' in an oath?
A) Three
B) Seven
C) Ten
D) Twelve

Key Takeaway
In oaths, definite plurals like 'al-ayyam' are interpreted differently within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa says ten, the Sahibayn say seven, twelve, or forever depending on the term.

Indefinite Plurals and the Term 'Jumu'ah' in Oaths
Intra-Hanafi dispute on indefinite plurals and the term 'jumu'ah' in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for indefinite plurals in oaths, including the effect of the description 'kathirah', and explain the difference between the views in al-Nawadir and al-Jami' al-Saghir regarding 'jumu'ah'.

Core Idea
Indefinite plurals without description imply three; with 'kathirah' they become like definite. 'Jumu'ah' in oaths normally means a week, not just Friday.

The previous lesson covered definite plurals. Now we turn to indefinite plurals, which generally imply the minimum (three). However, when described as 'many', they take on a definite-like meaning. Additionally, a special term 'jumu'ah' (Friday) sparks a minor intra-Hanafi debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of indefinite plural
Note whether the plural is absolute (e.g., 'ayyam') or qualified with 'kathirah' (many).
The text splits into two cases.
2
State the ruling for indefinite without description
According to all Hanafis, an indefinite plural in an oath refers to the minimum of the true plural, which is three.
This is agreed upon in al-Jami'.
3
State the ruling for indefinite with 'kathirah'
When described as 'many', the plural becomes like a definite; thus it follows the same dispute as definite: Abu Hanifa says ten, Sahibayn say a week.
The description removes the ambiguity.
4
Examine the specific term 'jumu'ah'
If someone swears 'la ukallimuka jumu'ah' (without al), does it mean Friday or a week? According to al-Nawadir and the majority of later scholars, it means a week (seven days). According to al-Jami' al-Saghir, it means the single day of Friday.
This is a minor riwayah-based difference.
5
Apply to a sample oath
If someone swears 'la ukallimuka ayyaman kathirah', according to Abu Hanifa the oath covers ten days; according to the Sahibayn, it covers a week.
The practical difference is significant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والأيام وأيام كثيرة والشهور والسنون عشرة ومنكرها ثلاثة) بيان لأقل الجمع في باب الأيمان وهو على وجهين : إما أن يكون معرفاً أو منكراً ، فإذا كان معرفاً كما إذا حلف لا يكلمه الأيام أو الجمع أو الشهور أو السنين انصرف إلى عشرة من تلك المعدودات، وكذلك لا يكلمه الأزمنة انصرف إلى خمس سنين لأن كل زمان ستة أشهر عند عدم النية . وهذا كله عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا في الأيام ينصرف إلى أيام الأسبوع، وفي الشهور إلى اثني عشر شهراً، وفي الجمع والسنين والدهور والأزمنة إلى الأبد لأن اللام للعهد إذا أمكن، وإن لم يمكن فهي للاستغراق والعهد ثابت في الأيام والشهور كما ذكرنا ولا عهد في خصوص ما سواهما فكان للاستغراق وهو استغراق سني العمر وجمعه وله أنه جمع معرف باللام فينصرف إلى أقصى ما عهد مستعملاً فيه لفظ الجمع على اليقين وهو عشرة لأنه يقال ثلاثة رجال وأربعة رجال إلى عشرة رجال، فإذا جاوز العشرة ذهب الجمع فيقال عشر رجلاً إلى آخره. وإنما اعتبر أقصى المعهود وإن كان ما دونه معهود أيضاً لأنها لاستغراق المعهود لأن المعهود كل مرتبة من المراتب التي أولها ثلاثة وأقصاها عشرة ولا معين. فالحاصل أنهم اتفقوا على أنها للعهد لكن اختلفوا في المعهود فهما قالا المعهود الأسبوع والسنة، وهو قال العشرة نظراً إلى أنها أقصى المعهود، وقد أطال في فتح القدير في بيانه إطالة حسنة وتعرض للرد على ابن العز ولسنا بصدد ذلك. وفي الذخيرة : لو قال والله لا أكلمك الجمع ولا نية له فله أن يكلمه في غير يوم الجمعة لأن الجمع جمع جمعة وهو اسم خاص لليوم الذي تقام فيه الجمعة، به لاجتماع الناس سمي . فيه لإقامة هذا الأمر فيه فلا يتناول غيره من الأيام كما لو قال لا أكلمك إلا خمسة والآحاد والأثانين، وإن نوى أيام الجمعة نفس الأسبوع فهو على ما نوى . وذكر في النوادر أن من قال علي صوم جمعة إن نوى يوم الجمعة يلزمه صوم يوم الجمعة لا غير، وإن نوى أيام الجمعة يعني الأسبوع أو لم تكن له نية يلزمه صوم الأيام السبعة بحكم غلبة الاستعمال يقول الرجل لغيره لم أراك منذ جمعة، فعلى رواية النوادر صرف الجمعة إلى أيامها دون يوم الجمعة خاصة، وعلى رواية الجامع الصغير صرف الجمعة المطلقة غير مقرونة باليوم إلى يوم الجمعة خاصة لأن هذا الاستعمال فيما إذا ذكرت الجمعة مطلقة بلفظ الواحد أي لا بلفظ الجمع حتى قال مشايخنا : إذا قال والله لا أكلمك جمعة ينصرف اليمين إلى الأيام السبعة لا إلى يوم الجمعة خاصة، كما ذكر في النوادر ا هـ . فتبين بهذا أنه إذا حلف لا يكلمه الجمع يترك كلامه عشرة أيام كل يوم هو يوم الجمعة لا أنه يترك كلامه عشرة أسابيع كما قد يتوهم . قال في التبيين : ثم الجمع معرفاً ومنكراً يقع على أيام الجمعة في المدة وله أن يكلمه فيما بين الجمعات. وأما الجمع المنكر فذكر المصنف أنه إن وصفه بالكثرة فهو كالمعرف كقوله لا أكلمه أياماً كثيرة لأنه لما وصفه بالكثرة علم أنه لم يرد به الأقل وهو ا فينصرف إلى المعهود كالمعرف باللام، فعنده للعشرة، وعندهما للأسبوع. وعلى هذا لو قال إن خدمتني أياماً كثيرة فأنت حر فعنده للعشرة، وعندهما للأسبوع، وإن لم يصفه بالكثرة انصرف إلى ثلاثة على ما ذكر في الجامع من غير خلاف وهو الصحيح لأنه ذكر لفظ الجمع منكراً فيقع على أدنى الجمع الصحيح وهو ثلاثة. وذكر في الأصل أنه على عشرة أيام وسوى بين منكر الأيام ومعرفها بخلاف السنين منكراً فإنه على ثلاثة اتفاقاً كما في البدائع .

Construct Plural in Oaths: Fungible vs. Distinct Items
Explanation of the construct plural (mudaf) in oath vows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish fungible and distinct items in a construct plural oath and state the ruling for each category.

Core Idea
In oaths, a construct plural of fungible items breaks by three; of distinct items requires all.

When someone swears using a plural in a construct phrase—like 'the mounts of so-and-so'—does the oath obligate riding every mount or just some? The Hanafi school draws a critical distinction based on the nature of the items. This lesson explores the detail provided in al-Dhakhirah and clarifies the reasoning behind the differing rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath construct
Recognize that 'the mounts of so-and-so' is a construct plural (jam' mudaf). The second term is definite, indicating a specific set.
Construct plurals are phrases like 'X of Y' where X is plural and Y is definite.
2
Classify the items as fungible or distinct
Determine whether the items in the set are interchangeable (fungible) or individually distinguished. Mounts, clothes, and slaves are typically fungible; wives, friends, and brothers are distinct individuals.
Fungible items are considered similar and not individually important; distinct items are each unique.
3
Apply the ruling for fungible items
If the items are fungible, the oath is broken by engaging with just three of them, even if the set has many more.
Three is the minimum plural in Arabic; the law uses it as a standard for fungible items.
4
Apply the ruling for distinct items
If the items are distinct, the oath is not broken unless the swearer engages with every single one of them.
Each individual is considered a separate object of the oath.
5
Note the fatwa on indefinite 'dahr'
For the case of indefinite 'dahr' (not relevant to construct plural), the relied-upon position is that of the two companions (Sahibayn) when intention is unknown.
This is a separate ruling; the main lesson focuses on the construct plural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المصنف الجمع المضاف وفيه تفصيل ففي الذخيرة: لو حلف لا يركب دواب فلان أو لا يلبس ثيابه أو لا يكلم عبيده ففعل بثلاثة مما سمى يحنث، وإن كان لفلان ثياب ودواب وعبيد أكثر من ثلاثة فرق بين وبين ما إذا حلف لا يكلم زوجات فلان لا يكلم أصدقاء فلان لا يكلم إخوة فلان حيث لا يحنث ما لم يكلم الكل مما سمى. والفرق أن في هذا الدهر منكر الخ لا أنه تصحيح لقولهما لكن قال في النهر : وغير خاف أنه إذا لم يرد وعن الإمام شيء في مسألة وجب الإفتاء بقولهما اهـ.
And the author did not mention the construct plural (al-jam' al-mudaf), and there is detail regarding it. In al-Dhakhirah: If he swore not to ride the mounts of so-and-so, or not to wear his clothes, or not to speak to his slaves, and he did so with three of what was named, he breaks the oath. And if so-and-so had clothes, mounts, and slaves more than three, there is a difference between this and if he swore not to speak to the wives of so-and-so, not to speak to the friends of so-and-so, not to speak to the brothers of so-and-so, where he does not break the oath unless he speaks to all of what was named. And the difference is that in this... [the text then refers back to the earlier discussion of indefinite dahr, saying that it is a correction of the two companions' view, but in al-Nahr it is said: it is not hidden that if he did not intend and there is something from the Imam in the issue, fatwa must be given according to the two companions.]
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage discusses a detail omitted by the author of the matn: the construct plural (mudaf) in oaths. The commentator cites al-Dhakhirah to explain the rule. When someone swears not to ride 'the mounts of so-and-so' (a construct plural), the oath is broken by riding just three of them, even if the person owns many more than three. However, if the oath is about 'the wives of so-and-so', 'the friends of so-and-so', or 'the brothers of so-and-so', the oath is not broken unless the swearer speaks to all of them (i.e., every single one). The distinction is that in the first case (mounts, clothes, slaves) the items are typically used interchangeably and three is a minimum; in the second case (wives, friends, brothers) each individual is distinct and the plural implies the entire set. This distinction is specific to construct plurals where the second term is definite. The passage ends with a note about the dispute on indefinite 'dahr', stating that if no intention is known, fatwa should be according to the two companions (Sahibayn).

Key Terms
الجمع المضاف (al-jam' al-mudaf): A plural noun in a construct phrase (idafa), e.g., 'dawab fulan'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Dhakhirah, what is the ruling if someone swears 'I will not speak to the brothers of so-and-so' and then speaks to only three of five brothers?
A) He breaks the oath because three is the minimum plural.
B) He does not break the oath because brothers are distinct items requiring all.
C) He breaks the oath only if he speaks to all five.
D) He does not break the oath because he spoke to more than one.

Key Takeaway
The construct plural in oaths requires three of fungible items but all of distinct items.

Rulings on Oaths Using Collective Plurals
Explaining rules for oaths sworn on groups and effect of grammatical definiteness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for an oath sworn on an indefinite plural versus a definite plural, and identify when speaking to one person breaks the oath.

Core Idea
The grammatical definiteness of a plural determines whether an oath is broken by one or requires three.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq clarifies a common point of confusion: when you swear an oath using a plural noun like 'slaves' or 'the poor', what exactly constitutes a breach? The answer depends on whether the plural is indefinite (منكر) or definite (معرف), and whether the noun is a true plural or a generic noun (اسم جنس). Understanding these distinctions ensures that oaths are fulfilled correctly according to Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of plural
Determine whether the oath uses an indefinite plural (e.g., 'عبيد' without ال) or a definite plural (e.g., 'العبيد' with ال). This classification governs the minimum number required to break the oath.
2
Apply the general Hanafi principle
For indefinite plurals, the minimum is three (أقل الجمع ثلاثة). For definite plurals without a known referent (لا عهد), they act as a generic noun (اسم جنس), and speaking to one breaks the oath.
The text cites al-Dhakhira: the ruling attaches to the least of the plural for indefinite, and to the least that applies to the name for definite with no referent.
3
Consider exceptions and intentions
If the swearer intends all members, his intention is valid between him and Allah but not in court. Also, note the dispute from Abu Yusuf: non-human plurals (e.g., animals, clothes) break by one, unlike human plurals.
Also consider the case of 'brothers' when the swearer knows the number; if he knows there is only one brother, he intended the singular and breaks by one.
4
Extend to related expressions
The same principles apply to oaths involving time periods. For example, 'every day of this week' vs. 'in every day' lead to different outcomes regarding how many times the oath is broken.
Refer to the examples from al-Zahiriyya and the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب واللبس والكلام الفصل الأول المنع في فلان لا لمعنى هذه الأشياء فتتقيد اليمين باعتبار منسوبين إلى فلان وقد ذكر النسبة باسم الجمع وأقل الجمع ثلاثة أما في الفصل الثاني المنع لمعنى في هؤلاء فتعلقت اليمين بأعيانهم وصار تقدير المسألة لا أكلم هؤلاء فما لم يكلم الكل لا يحنث. وإن نوى الحالف في الفصل الأول الدواب كلها الغلمان كلها يدين فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى وفي القضاء لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه ؛ كذا في الزيادات وظاهره أنه لا يحنث بواحدة في الكل. وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف أنه لا يحنث بالواحد في بني آدم ويحنث في غيره فإذا حلف لا يكلم عبيد فلان وله ثلاثة فكلم واحداً منهم لا يحنث ويمينه على الكل بخلاف لا أركب دوابه ولا ألبس ثيابه. وفي الواقعات : قال والله لا أكلم إخوة فلان وله أخ والأخ واحد، فإن كان يعلم يحنث إذا كلم ذلك الواحد لأنه ذكر الجمع وأراد الواحد، فإن كان لا يعلم لا يحنث لأنه لم يرد الواحد فبقيت اليمين على الجمع كمن حلف لا يأكل ثلاثة أرغفة من هذا الحب وليس له فيه إلا رغيف واحد وهو لا يعلم لا يحنث ا هـ . وقيد المصنف بالأيام ونحوها لأنه لو قال والله لا أكلم الفقراء أو المساكين أو الرجال فكلم واحداً منهم يحنث لأنه اسم جنس بخلاف قوله رجالاً أو نساء ؛ كذا في الواقعات. ففي المنكر فلا فرق بين الكل، وأما في المعرف فإنه ينصرف للمعهود إن أمكن، وإلا فهو للجنس لأن الألف واللام إذا دخلت على الجمع ولا عهد فإنه يبطل معنى الجمعية كقوله لا اشتري العبيد لا أتزوج النساء كما عرف في الأصول وفي الذخيرة: الأصل أن الحكم اذا علق بجمع منكر كعبيد ورجال ونساء يتعلق وقوعه بأدنى الجمع الصحيح وهو الثلاثة دون المثنى، ومتى علق بجميع معرف بالألف واللام يتعلق بأدنى ما ينطلق عليه ذلك الاسم عند عامة المشايخ إذا لم يكن ثمة معهود كالحكم المعلق باسم الجنس، وعند بعض المشايخ ينصرف إلى كل الجنس ا هـ. وفي تهذيب القلانسي : وأما الأطعمة والنساء والثياب يقع على واحد إجماعاً، ولو نوى الكل صحت نيته . وفي الظهيرية : لو قال والله لا أكلمك كل يوم من أيام هذه الجمعة فكلمه في تلك الجمعة ليلاً أو نهاراً مرة واحدة حنث به ولو قال والله لا أكلمك في كل يوم من أيام هذه الجمعة لا يحنث حتى يكلمه في كل يوم، ولو ترك كلامه يوماً واحداً لا يحنث، وإن كلمه كل يوم لا يحنث إلا مرة واحدة لاتحاد الاسم، ولو حلف لا يكلم فلاناً أيامه هذه قال أبو يوسف: هو على ثلاثة أيام ولو قال لا أكلمه أيامه فهو على العمر ولو قال لا أكلمك يوماً بعد الأيام عن محمد إن كلمه في سبعة أيام لا يحنث وبعد السبعة يحنث والمعنى فيه على أصل محمد ظاهر ا هـ والله أعلم. اهـ.
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oath regarding Eating, Drinking, Clothing, and Speech. First section: Prohibition concerning a specific person without a meaning in these things; the oath is restricted with reference to those attributed to that person, and the attribution is mentioned with the collective noun, and the minimum of a collective noun is three. As for the second section: Prohibition for a meaning in these people, the oath attaches to their individuals, and the estimation of the issue becomes: 'I will not speak to these people'; so as long as he does not speak to all, he does not break his oath. If the swearer in the first section intends all animals, all slaves, he is believed between himself and Allah Almighty, and in court, because he intended the reality of his speech – likewise in al-Ziyadat. Its apparent meaning is that he does not break the oath by one in the whole. In Nawadir Ibn Samāʿa from Abu Yusuf: he does not break the oath by one in the case of human beings, but breaks in other things. So if he swears 'I will not speak to the slaves of so-and-so' and he has three, and he speaks to one of them, he does not break the oath; his oath is upon all, unlike 'I will not ride his animals' or 'I will not wear his clothes'. In al-Waqiʿat: He said, 'By Allah, I will not speak to the brothers of so-and-so' and he has one brother, and the brother is one; if he knows, he breaks the oath when he speaks to that one because he mentioned the plural and intended the singular; if he does not know, he does not break because he did not intend the singular, so the oath remains upon the plural, like one who swears not to eat three loaves from this pile and he has only one loaf in it and he does not know – he does not break the oath. The author restricted it with 'days' and the like, because if he says 'By Allah, I will not speak to the poor' or 'the needy' or 'the men' and he speaks to one of them, he breaks the oath because it is a generic noun, unlike his saying 'men' or 'women' – likewise in al-Waqiʿat. As for the indefinite, there is no difference among all; as for the definite, it refers to the known referent if possible, otherwise it is for the genus, because when the definite article enters a plural and there is no specific reference, it nullifies the meaning of plurality, like his saying 'I will not buy the slaves' or 'I will not marry the women' as known in Usul. In al-Dhakhira: The principle is that when a ruling is attached to an indefinite plural like 'slaves', 'men', 'women', its occurrence attaches to the least of the correct plural, which is three, not the dual. And whenever it is attached to a definite plural with the definite article, it attaches to the least that applies to that name according to the majority of scholars if there is no known referent, like a ruling attached to a generic noun; and according to some scholars, it refers to the entire genus. In Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: As for foods, women, and clothes, it applies to one by consensus, and if he intends all, his intention is valid. In al-Zahiriyya: If he says 'By Allah, I will not speak to you every day of the days of this week' and he speaks to him in that week by night or day once, he breaks the oath. And if he says 'By Allah, I will not speak to you in every day of the days of this week', he does not break until he speaks to him every day; if he leaves speaking to him one day, he does not break; and if he speaks to him every day, he only breaks once due to the unity of the name. And if he swears not to speak to so-and-so these days, Abu Yusuf said: it is for three days; and if he said 'I will not speak to him his days', it is for a lifetime; and if he said 'I will not speak to you a day after the days', from Muhammad: if he speaks to him within seven days, he does not break, and after seven days he breaks, and the meaning according to Muhammad's principle is apparent. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq is a commentary on the chapter of oaths regarding eating, drinking, clothing, and speech. The author explains the rulings when an oath is taken with a collective noun (جمع). The text divides the discussion into two sections: first, when the prohibition is not for a meaning present in the individuals but due to their relation to a person, then the oath is tied to the minimum of the plural (three). In the second section, when the prohibition is for a meaning in the individuals, the oath attaches to each individual, and the swearer does not break until he deals with all. The passage then discusses various scenarios: if the swearer intends all, his intention is accepted privately but not in court; a difference from Abu Yusuf regarding humans vs. other things; the case of 'brothers' when there is only one brother depends on knowledge; the effect of definite vs. indefinite plurals; and finally a extended discussion about oaths involving days, with positions from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The overarching principle is that indefinite plurals require at least three, while definite plurals may be broken by one if no specific referent is intended.

Key Terms
الجمع (al-jamʿ): The plural form, here referring to a collective noun like 'عبيد' (slaves) or 'رجال' (men).
المنكر (al-munkar): The indefinite plural (without definite article), e.g., 'عبيد'.
المعرف (al-muʿarraf): The definite plural (with the definite article), e.g., 'العبيد'.
اسم الجنس (ism al-jins): A generic noun that applies to the whole species, like 'الفقراء' (the poor).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, because it is a generic noun
B) No, because it is a plural
C) Only if he speaks to three
D) It depends on his intention

Key Takeaway
The key principle: indefinite plurals in oaths require three; definite plurals without a known referent act as generic nouns and break by one.

Rulings on Oaths Involving Glad Tidings
Explaining the distinction between glad tidings, news, and notification in oath rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between glad tidings (bishara), news (khabar), and notification (i'lam) in Hanafi oath rulings, and apply the conditions for truthfulness and means of communication.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oath rulings, only glad tidings and notification require truth; news applies to both truth and falsehood.

This lesson explores the Hanafi distinction between three types of communication in oath contexts: glad tidings, news, and notification. Understanding these categories is crucial for determining when an oath is broken and when truthfulness is required. The source passage uses a hadith and examples from the Companions to illustrate the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Determine which type of communication (bishara, khabar, i'lam) is involved in the oath. The ruling hinges on whether truthfulness is required or not.
Bishara and i'lam require truth; khabar does not unless specified with a preposition.
2
Locate the Dalil
The hadith of Ibn Mas'ud is cited: the Prophet's statement about recitation and the Companions' reactions illustrate the concept of bishara.
The hadith shows that bishara is associated with joy and truthfulness.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The hadith provides the basis that glad tidings (bishara) are distinct from mere news (khabar) because they cause joy and require truth.
The Companions' usage: Abu Bakr gave glad tidings, Umar informed.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
No explicit disagreement among Hanafis; the passage clarifies the distinctions within the school. The key ikhtilaf is between bishara and khabar regarding truthfulness.
Some might conflate these categories, but the Hanafi school maintains the distinction.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The passage from Bahr al-Raiq is a later commentary that confirms the established Hanafi position. Writing and messengers are treated like direct speech for bishara.
The tarjih is that bishara via writing or messenger is effective, but for hadith (conversation), only oral speech applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
روي كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الطلاق والعتاق لخبر سار صدق ليس للمبشر به علم عرفاً ويتحقق ذلك من الأول دون الباقين، وأصله ما أنه صلى الله عليه وسلم مر بابن مسعود وهو يقرأ القرآن فقال عليه السلام: من أحب أن يقرأ القرآن غضاً طرياً كما أنزل فليقرأ بقراءة ابن أم عبد فابتدر إليه أبو بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما فسبق أبو بكر عمر فكان يقول : بشرني أبو بكر وأخبرني عمر . ولو كتب إليه أحدهما كتاباً بالبشارة يعتق إلا إذا نوى المشافهة لأن البشارة قد تكون بالكتابة لأن الكتابة من الغائب بمنزلة الخطاب من الحاضر وكذا لو أرسل إليه رسولاً فإنه يعتق في البشارة والخبر بخلاف الحديث لا يحنث إلا بالمشافهة ولو حلف لا يدعو فلاناً فكتب إليه يدعوه حنث كما في الذخيرة. وقيدناها بالصدق لأنه لو بشره كذباً لا يقع لأنه وإن ظهر في بشرة الوجه الفرح والسرور باعتبار الظاهر لكنه قد زال لما تبين له خلافه بخلاف من أخبرني أن فلاناً قدم فكذا فأخبره واحد كذباً فإنه يعتق لأنه ينطلق على الكذب والصدق بخلاف ما إذا قال من أخبرني بقدومه فلا بد من الصدق كما قدمناه ففي البشارة لا فرق بين أن يأتي بالباء أو لا بخلاف الخبر، وقد علم الفرق في بحث الباء من الأصول والكتابة كالخبر، فلو قال إن كتبت أن فلاناً قدم فكذا فكتب كذباً عتق لأنها جمع الحروف وقد وجد بخلاف إن كتبت بقدومه فلا بد من قدومه حقيقة، فلو كتب بقدومه غير عالم به وقد قدم حقيقة عتق بلغ الخبر إلى الحالف أولاً لوجود الشرط كما في المحيط . وأما الإعلام فلا بد فيه من الصدق لأن الإعلام إثبات العلم والكذب لا يفيده؛ كذا في البدائع ولا فرق فيه بين أن يأتي بالباء أولاً ما في الذخيرة. وخرج الخبر الضار فليس ببشارة عرفاً وإن سماه الله بشارة في قوله تعالى فبشرهم بعذاب أليم ﴾ [آل عمران: ۲۱] لأنه بشارة لغة والكلام في العرف. وفي المحيط : لو قال أول من بشرني بقدوم فلان من عبيدي فهو حر فأرسل بعض عبيده عبداً آخر فقال قل للمولى إن فلاناً يقول لك قد قدم فلان فأبلغه ذلك العبد قال يعتق المرسل دون الرسول وهو بمنزلة الكتابة، ولو قال الرسول إن فلاناً قد قدم ولم يقل أرسلني إليك فلان عبدك بكذا عتق الرسول دون المرسل
It is narrated: Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oath in Divorce and Manumission: For glad tidings, truthfulness is required; the one giving the tidings does not know the news by custom, and that is realized from the first one, not the rest. Its basis is that he (peace be upon him) passed by Ibn Mas'ud while he was reciting the Quran and said: 'Whoever loves to recite the Quran fresh as it was revealed, let him recite according to the recitation of Ibn Umm Abd.' So Abu Bakr and Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) rushed to him, and Abu Bakr preceded Umar, and he would say: 'Abu Bakr gave me glad tidings and Umar informed me.' If one of them wrote a letter to him with the glad tidings, he is freed unless he intended oral communication, because glad tidings may be through writing, for writing from an absent person is like speech from a present one. Similarly, if he sent a messenger to him, he is freed in the case of glad tidings and news, unlike a conversation; he does not break his oath except with oral speech. If he swore not to invite so-and-so, and he wrote to him inviting him, he breaks his oath, as in al-Dhakhira. We have restricted it to truthfulness because if he gives false glad tidings, it does not occur, because even if joy and happiness appear on the face of the one being told, considering the apparent, it vanishes when the contrary becomes clear. Unlike the case where someone informs me that so-and-so has arrived, and one person informs him falsely: he is freed, because it applies to both false and true. Unlike if he said 'whoever informs me of his arrival,' then truthfulness is required, as we have mentioned. So in glad tidings, there is no difference between using the preposition 'ba' or not, unlike news. The difference has been learned in the discussion of the preposition from the principles. Writing is like news. So if he said, 'If I write that so-and-so has arrived, then such-and-such,' and he wrote falsely, he is freed, because it is the gathering of letters, and it has occurred. Unlike if he said, 'If I write of his arrival,' then his actual arrival is necessary. So if he wrote of his arrival without knowing it, and he actually arrived, he is freed, whether the news reaches the one swearing first or not, because the condition is fulfilled, as in al-Muhit. As for notification (i'lam), truthfulness is necessary, because notification is establishing knowledge, and falsehood does not achieve that; as in al-Bada'i, and there is no difference between using the preposition or not, as in al-Dhakhira. Harmful news is excluded; it is not considered glad tidings by custom, even though Allah called it glad tidings in His saying: 'So give them tidings of a painful punishment' (3:21), because it is glad tidings linguistically, but the discussion is about custom. In al-Muhit: If he said, 'The first of my slaves who gives me glad tidings of the arrival of so-and-so is free,' and he sends one of his slaves another slave, saying 'Tell the master that so-and-so says to you: so-and-so has arrived,' and that slave conveys it: he said, the sender is freed, not the messenger, and it is like writing. And if the messenger said, 'So-and-so has arrived,' and did not say 'So-and-so, your slave, sent me to you with this,' the messenger is freed, not the sender.
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing oaths involving glad tidings (bishara), news (khabar), and notification (i'lam). The author begins by stating that for glad tidings, truthfulness is required, and the one giving the tidings does not know the news by custom. The proof is the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, where Abu Bakr gave glad tidings and Umar informed. The ruling distinguishes between glad tidings and mere news: in glad tidings, truthfulness is needed because the focus is on the emotional effect; in news, the word is used for both true and false reports. Writing and messengers are treated like direct speech for glad tidings, but for conversations (hadith), only oral speech breaks the oath. For notification (i'lam), truthfulness is always required because it aims to establish knowledge. Harmful news is not considered glad tidings customarily, even if the Quran uses the term for punishment. The example from al-Muhit illustrates the case of a slave giving glad tidings via a messenger: the one who initiated the message is freed, not the messenger, analogous to writing.

Key Terms
بشارة (bishāra): Glad tidings that cause joy to the recipient, requiring truthfulness in an oath.
خبر (khabar): News that can be true or false; in oaths, it does not require truthfulness unless specified with a preposition.
إعلام (i‘lām): Notification that establishes knowledge; truthfulness is always required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears 'Whoever gives me glad tidings of Zayd's arrival is free,' and a slave sends a messenger who says 'Zayd has arrived' without mentioning the sender, who is freed?
A) The messenger
B) The sender
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath law, glad tidings require truthfulness, news does not, and notification always does; writing and messengers are equivalent to speech for bishara and khabar but not for conversation.

Multiple Glad Tidings and the Preposition 'Ba' in Oaths
Clarifying rulings on multiple glad tidings and the effect of the preposition 'ba' in oaths, with a critical refinement of earlier statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on multiple glad tidings and distinguish the effect of the preposition 'ba' in oaths, applying the correct conditions for breaking an oath based on the specific wording.

Core Idea
The preposition 'ba' in oaths requires actual existence; different oath formulations condition breaking on truthfulness or the informant's action.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies two subtle points in the chapter on oaths. First, when multiple persons give glad tidings simultaneously, each is considered to have given glad tidings, and all are freed. Second, the author critically refines earlier statements about notification, drawing on Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir to show that the ruling depends on the specific wording, especially the presence of the preposition 'ba'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings being refined
The passage addresses (a) multiple persons giving glad tidings together, and (b) the effect of different oath formulations, especially with the preposition 'ba'. Note that the author initially stated there was no difference, then corrects himself.
The refinement is crucial for correct fatwa.
2
Study the Quranic proof for multiple glad tidings
The verse 'So they gave him glad tidings of a forbearing son' (51:28) shows that glad tidings can be given by multiple subjects simultaneously. Each angel is considered to have given the glad tidings, so the same applies in oaths.
The dalala is zanni because the verse is not explicitly about oaths.
3
Resolve the apparent contradiction
The earlier statement claimed no difference between formulations, but Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir clarifies that 'akhbartani' and 'katabta ilayya' break the oath with falsehood, whereas 'bashshtani' and 'alamtani' require truthfulness and the swearer's ignorance.
The effective cause (sabab) differs: act of informing vs. conveying gladness.
4
Apply the rule: when does the oath break?
For 'bashshtani' and 'alamtani': only if the news is true and the swearer did not know it. For 'akhbartani' and 'katabta': breaking occurs with false information even if the swearer knew. For a phrase with 'ba' (e.g., 'bi qudumihi'), actual existence is required.
Gesture breaks the oath in 'alamtani'; writing and messenger break it in all formulations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن بشروه معاً عتقوا لتحققها من الجميع قال تعالى فبشروه بغلام عليم [الذاريات : ٢٨] كما . قوله : (ففي البشارة لا فرق الخ) هذا مخالف لما قدمه قبل هذا الباب في شرح قول المصنف لا يكلمه فناداه وهو نائم وكذا قوله (وأما الإعلام مخالف لما مر كما نبهنا عليه. وفي تلخيص الجامع الكبير : لو قال إن أخبرتني أن زيداً قدم فكذا حنث بالكذب كذا إن كتبت إلي وإن لم يصل. وفي بشرتني أو اعلمتني يشترط الصدق وجهل الحالف لأن الركن في الأوليين الدال على المخبر وجمع الحروف، وفي الأخريين إفادة البشر والعلم بخلاف ما إذا قال بقدومه لأن باء الإلصاق تقتضي الوجود وهو بالصدق. ويحنث بالإيماء في أعلمتني وبالكتاب والرسول في الكل قوله : (فبشروه بغلام عليم) كذا في التبيين والفتح والنهر والتلاوة وبشروه بالواو قوله : (وينبغي أنه لو وهب له قريبه أو تصدق به عليه أو أوصى له به أو جعل مهراً لها فنوى أن يكون عن كفارته عند قبوله فإنه يجوز لأن النية صادفت العلة الاختيارية بخلاف الإرث لأنه جبري ولم أره منقولاً صريحاً وكلامهم يفيده دلالة)
His saying: 'And if they give him glad tidings together, they all are freed, because it is realized from all of them. He said, Exalted: So they gave him glad tidings of a forbearing son (51:28).' As. His saying: 'So in glad tidings there is no difference, etc.' This contradicts what he presented before this chapter in the commentary on the author's saying: 'He does not speak to him, so he called him while he was sleeping.' Similarly his saying: 'And as for notification, it contradicts what has passed, as we have cautioned.' In Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir: If he said, 'If you inform me that Zayd has arrived, then such-and-such,' he breaks the oath with falsehood, similarly if he writes to me even if it does not reach. In 'you give me glad tidings' or 'you notify me,' truthfulness and the ignorance of the one swearing are required, because the pillar in the first two is the indication of the informant and the gathering of letters, and in the last two is the conveyance of glad tidings and knowledge. Unlike if he said 'of his arrival,' because the preposition of adhesion necessitates existence, and that is with truthfulness. He breaks the oath with gesture in 'you notify me' and with writing and messenger in all. His saying: 'So they gave him glad tidings of a forbearing son' – thus in al-Tabyin, al-Fath, al-Nahr, and al-Tilawa, and 'and they gave him glad tidings' with a waw. His saying: 'It is appropriate that if someone gives his relative as a gift, or gives it in charity, or bequeaths it to him, or makes it a dowry for her, and intends it to be from his expiation upon acceptance, it is permissible because the intention meets the voluntary cause, unlike inheritance because it is compulsory. I have not seen it explicitly narrated, but their words indicate it.'
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement discussing two issues: (1) When multiple persons give glad tidings together, all are freed because the glad tidings are realized from each. The proof is the Quranic verse about the angels giving glad tidings to Ibrahim of a wise son. (2) The commentator notes that the statement 'in glad tidings there is no difference [with or without the preposition]' contradicts an earlier passage in the chapter. He also notes a contradiction regarding notification. Then he quotes from Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir to clarify: If the oath uses 'akhbartani' (you informed me) or 'katabta ilayya' (you wrote to me), breaking the oath occurs with falsehood as well. But if it uses 'bashshtani' (you gave me glad tidings) or 'alamtani' (you notified me), truthfulness and the ignorance of the swearer are required because the effective cause in the first two is the act of informing or writing, while in the latter it is the effect of gladness or knowledge. If the phrase includes the preposition 'bi' (e.g., 'bi-qudumihi'), it requires actual existence and truthfulness. Breaking the oath also occurs by gesture in 'alamtani' and by writing and messenger in all cases. Finally, the commentator adds that if someone gives a relative as a gift or charity etc. with the intention of expiation, it should be valid because the intention meets the voluntary cause, unlike inheritance.

Key Terms
باء الإلصاق (bā’ al-ilṣāq): The preposition 'ba' indicating adhesion or direct connection, requiring actual existence of the thing mentioned.
تلخيص الجامع الكبير (Talkhīṣ al-Jāmi‘ al-Kabīr): A summary work by a Hanafi scholar containing detailed rulings on oaths.

A) The information must be true and the swearer must be ignorant
B) Only the information must be true
C) Only the swearer must be ignorant
D) The information can be false

Key Takeaway
The preposition 'ba' and the specific verb (bashshara vs. akhbara) fundamentally change the conditions for breaking an oath in Hanafi fiqh.

Purchasing Relatives for Expiation and Oaths of Manumission
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain when purchasing a relative counts as manumission for expiation and distinguish valid from invalid expiation based on the cause and ownership.

Core Idea
The intention must coincide with the true cause of manumission for expiation to be valid.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the nuanced rulings on using purchase of relatives for expiation. It distinguishes between purchasing a father (which is considered manumission) and purchasing someone on whom one has sworn to free (which is not). The key principle is that the intention must align with the actual cause of freedom. The passage also addresses a subtle disagreement with Zufar on oaths involving concubinage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key distinction
The text distinguishes between purchasing a relative (father) for expiation vs. purchasing someone on whom one swore to free. Recognize that the first is valid, the second is not.
The proof for the first is the Prophet's (PBUH) practice of making purchase itself manumission.
2
Understand the cause vs. condition
In the valid case, the purchase itself is the cause of manumission. In the invalid case, the oath is the cause, and the purchase is merely a condition.
The intention must meet the cause; here the intention meets the purchase (cause) for father, but not for the oath case where the cause is the oath.
3
Analyze the umm walad exception
A slave-girl who is already an umm walad has a prior claim to freedom (istilad), so freeing her for expiation is not valid because her freedom is not due to the oath.
This contrasts with a regular slave-girl where the oath is the sole cause.
4
Examine the concubinage oath
Understand the dispute between the majority and Zufar: when one says 'If I take a concubine, she is free,' does mentioning concubinage imply ownership? The majority says no; ownership is only a condition for the act, not part of the consequence.
Zufar argues that concubinage only makes sense with ownership, so ownership is implicitly mentioned.
5
Apply the talil (reasoning)
Use the analogy to divorce: if a man says to a foreign woman 'If I divorce you, you are thrice divorced,' then marries and divorces her once, she is not thrice divorced. Similarly, the condition is measured precisely; ownership is not part of the consequence.
This analogy supports the majority view.

For expiation, not purchasing one who swore to free him and the mother of his child; if he takes a slave-girl as a concubine, she is free, correct if in his ownership, otherwise not. The cause. His saying: 'And the purchase of his father for expiation is valid, not the purchase of one who swore to free him and the mother of his child.' Because purchasing a relative is manumission, as he (peace be upon him) made the act of purchase itself manumission, because it does not require anything else, so it became like his saying 'he gave him a drink and quenched his thirst.' So the intention met the cause and sufficed for expiation. As for purchasing one on whom he swore to free him, as if he said 'If I purchase so-and-so, he is free,' then he purchases him intending it as expiation for his oath or otherwise, it does not suffice because the condition is the conjunction of the intention with the cause of manumission, which is the oath; the purchase is its condition. As for the mother of the child, it has preceded in the chapter on zihar that if he frees her for expiation, it is not permissible, and this is not his intent here. And his saying 'mother of the child' is conjoined to 'one who swore,' meaning that if he said to a slave-girl whom he had made a mother of his child by marriage, 'If I purchase you, you are free for the expiation of my oath,' then he purchases her, she is freed due to the existence of the condition, but it does not suffice for expiation because her freedom is due to istilad, so it is not attributed to the oath in every respect. Unlike if he said to a slave-girl not a mother, 'If I purchase you, you are free for the expiation of my oath,' it would suffice when he purchases her because her freedom is not due to another reason, so the attribution to the oath is not impaired, and the intention accompanies it. The upshot is that if the intention accompanies the cause of manumission and the slave is fully owned, expiation is valid; otherwise not. Their saying here that the oath is the cause of manumission is from the category of using the whole to mean part, because it is the consequence, and 'you are free' is the totality of the oath from condition and consequence. It is restricted to purchase because if he inherits his relative and intends it for his expiation, it is not valid because no action came from him to be considered a manumission; as in al-Muhit. It is appropriate that if his relative is given to him as a gift, or given in charity, or bequeathed to him, or made a dowry for him, and he intends it for his expiation upon acceptance, it is permissible because the intention meets the voluntary cause, unlike inheritance because it is compulsory. I have not seen it explicitly narrated, but their words indicate it. Because if she is in his ownership, the oath is concluded regarding her because it meets ownership. This is because the slave-girl is indefinite in this condition, so it applies to every slave-girl individually. And if he purchases a slave-girl and takes her as a concubine, she is not freed, contrary to Zufar who says: Taking a concubine is only valid in ownership, so mentioning it is mentioning ownership, so it becomes like if he said to a foreign woman 'If I divorce you, my slave is free,' making the marriage mentioned. Our argument is that ownership becomes mentioned by necessity of the validity of concubinage, and it is a condition so it is measured by its extent, and it does not appear regarding the validity of the consequence, which is freedom. In the issue of divorce, it appears only regarding the condition, not the consequence, so if he said to her 'If I divorce you, you are thrice divorced,' then marries her and divorces her once, she is not thrice divorced. This is analogous to our issue. He restricted with his saying 'she is free' because if he said 'If I take a concubine, then you are divorced' or 'my slave is free,' and he takes as concubine one in his ownership or one he purchases after the suspension, then she
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the purchase of relatives for expiation and oaths related to concubinage. The commentator explains the matn: Purchasing one's father for expiation is valid because the act of purchase itself is considered manumission by the Prophet (peace be upon him), similar to giving a drink that quenches thirst. However, purchasing someone on whom one has sworn to free (e.g., saying 'if I buy him, he is free') does not count as expiation because the cause of freedom is the oath, not the purchase; the purchase is merely a condition. Similarly, freeing an umm walad (mother of a child) for expiation is invalid, as her freedom is already due to istilad (bearing the master's child). The commentator distinguishes between a slave-girl who is an umm walad and one who is not: in the latter case, purchasing her with the intention of expiation suffices because her freedom is solely due to the oath. The principle is that for expiation to be valid, the intention must coincide with the cause of manumission and the slave must be fully owned. The passage then addresses the case of concubinage: If a man says 'If I take a concubine, she is free,' and he takes a concubine he already owns, she is freed because the condition is met. But if he buys a slave-girl and then takes her as a concubine, she is not freed, contrary to Zufar who argues that the mention of concubinage implies ownership. The Hanafi position is that ownership is only implicitly required for the validity of concubinage, not for the freedom consequence. The commentator draws an analogy to divorce: if a man says to a foreign woman 'If I divorce you, you are thrice divorced,' then marries and divorces her once, she is not thrice divorced because the condition is not fully met. Finally, the restriction 'she is free' is explained: if the oath is about divorce or freeing a slave, the ruling differs.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has borne a child to her master, acquiring a right to freedom upon her master's death.
تسري (tasarrī): Taking a slave-girl as a concubine, which is only valid if she is owned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) She is freed
B) She is not freed
C) It depends on his intention
D) She is freed only if he owned her before

Key Takeaway
The validity of expiation depends on the cause of manumission coinciding with the intention; mere purchase with an oath as cause does not suffice.

Conditional Manumission on Taking a Concubine
Ruling on conditional manumission with Zufar's dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional manumission tied to taking a concubine, explain why later acquisition does not trigger the oath, and distinguish Zufar's position.

Core Idea
A condition in an oath binds only to existing items; later acquisition does not fulfill the condition.

This lesson examines a specific oath: 'If I take a concubine from a slave-girl, she is free.' The Hanafi school holds that such an oath is valid only if the slave-girl is already in his possession at the time of the oath. But what if he later buys a slave-girl and takes her as concubine? Imam Zufar argued that since taking a concubine implies ownership, the oath should cover future acquisitions. The Hanafi majority, however, distinguish between the condition for the action and the consequence of the oath. We will unpack this reasoning, including an analogy to conditional divorce.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its condition
Identify the act: a man makes an oath conditional on taking a concubine. The ruling: the oath is valid only if the slave-girl is in his possession at the time of the oath. If not, the oath is invalid from the start.
2
Identify the ikhtilaf with Zufar
Zufar argues that since taking a concubine requires ownership, mentioning it implies ownership, so the oath should be valid for any slave-girl he later acquires and takes as concubine.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Ownership is a condition (shart) for the action, but it does not appear in the consequence (jazā'). Therefore, it does not extend the scope of the oath to future acquisitions. The analogy: if one says to a foreign woman 'If I divorce you, you are thrice divorced,' then marries and divorces once, only one divorce occurs, not three.
The condition of marriage is necessary for the action of divorce but does not affect the number.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Zufar's view, the later-acquired slave-girl would be freed. The Hanafi position: she is not freed. The Hanafi position is relied upon for fatwa.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Consider: a man says 'If I ride a horse, my slave is free.' He later buys a horse and rides it. According to the Hanafi principle, the slave is not freed because the horse was not owned at the time of the oath. This reinforces the rule that conditions are tied to existing property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إن تسريت أمة فهي حرة صح لو في ملكه وإلا لا) أي وإن لم يكن في ملكه لم يصح التعليق لأنها إن كانت في ملكه فقد انعقدت اليمين في حقها لمصادفتها الملك، وهذا لأن الجارية منكرة في هذا الشرط فتتناول لكل جارية على الانفراد وأما إذا اشترى جارية وتسراها فإنها لا تعتق خلافاً لزفر فإنه يقول : التسري لا يصح إلا في الملك. فكان ذكره ذكر الملك فصار كما إذا قال لأجنبية إن طلقتك فعبدي حر يصير التزوج مذكوراً ولنا أن الملك يصير مذكوراً ضرورة صحة التسري وهو شرط فيتقدر بقدره، ولا يظهر في حق صحة الجزاء وهو الحرية، وفي مسألة الطلاق إنما يظهر في حق الشرط دون الجزاء حتى لو قال لها إن طلقتك فأنت طالق ثلاثاً فتزوجها وطلقها واحدة لا تطلق ثلاثاً فهذا وزان مسألتنا .
His statement: (If I take a concubine from a slave-girl, she is free, it is valid if she is in his possession, otherwise not) – i.e., if she is not in his possession, the conditional oath is not valid. Because if she is in his possession, the oath is established regarding her due to encountering ownership. This is because the slave-girl is indefinite in this condition, so it includes every slave-girl individually. But if he buys a slave-girl and then takes her as a concubine, she is not freed, contrary to Zufar who says: taking a concubine is only valid if in ownership, so mentioning it implies ownership, as if he said to a foreign woman 'If I divorce you, my slave is free' making marriage mentioned. Our argument: ownership is implied necessarily for the validity of taking a concubine, and that is a condition, so it is limited to its extent, and does not appear in regard to the validity of the consequence (freedom). In the divorce case, it appears in regard to the condition not the consequence, so if he said 'If I divorce you, you are thrice divorced' and then married and divorced her once, she is not thrice divorced. This is analogous to our issue.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of an oath like 'If I take a concubine (tasarrā) from a slave-girl, she is free.' The author explains that such an oath is valid only if the slave-girl is already in his possession at the time of the oath. If he buys a slave-girl later and takes her as a concubine, she is not freed. This opposes Zufar, who argues that since taking a concubine implies ownership, the oath should be valid. The Hanafi response: ownership is a necessary condition for the action, but it is not mentioned in the oath, so it does not make the consequence (freedom) valid. The analogy is made to divorce conditional on marriage: if a man says to a foreign woman 'If I divorce you, you are thrice divorced,' then marries and divorces her once, she is not thrice divorced, because the condition of marriage is not mentioned for the consequence. This is a matter of how conditional statements operate in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
تسري (tasarrā): To take a slave-girl as a concubine, i.e., make her one's female partner with the intention of intimacy.
جزاء (jazā'): The consequence or result of a conditional statement, e.g., she is free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'If I take a concubine from a slave-girl, she is free.' He buys a slave-girl and takes her as concubine. According to the Hanafi school, is she freed?
A) Yes, because the condition is met.
B) No, because the oath only applies to slave-girls in his possession at the time of the oath.
C) Yes, because ownership is implied.
D) No, because she must be a relative.

Key Takeaway
A condition in an oath is limited to the specific context; conditions necessary for the action are not automatically conditions for the consequence.

Scope of 'Every Slave of Mine is Free' in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on the scope of a general manumission statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the scope of the phrase 'Every slave of mine is free' according to the Hanafi school and identify which slave categories are automatically freed and which are not.

Core Idea
A general manumission statement frees only those slaves whom the master fully owns in both body (raqaba) and possession (yad).

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the Hanafi ruling on a master's declaration 'Every slave of mine is free.' It distinguishes between categories of slaves based on the completeness of ownership. This lesson will help you parse the key terms and apply the ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the slave categories
List the types of slaves mentioned: qinn (fully owned), umm walad (mother of master's child), mudabbar (promised freedom after death), mukatab (under contract of manumission), and part-freed slave.
2
Understand the inclusion criterion
The passage states that automatic inclusion requires the master to have full ownership in both raqaba (the slave's body) and yad (possession/use). Determine for each category whether both elements are present.
3
Identify the exceptions
Recognize that mukatab and part-freed slaves are not included automatically because the master's yad is incomplete. They require explicit intention to be freed by the general statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كل مملوك لي حر عتق عبيده القن وأمهات أولاده ومدبروه لا مكاتبه) لوجود الإضافة المطلقة فيما عدا المكاتب إذا لملك ثابت فيهم رقبة ويداً ولا يدخل المكاتب إلا بالنية لأن الملك غير ثابت يداً فيه ولهذا لا يملك إكسابه، ولا يحل له وطء المكاتبة بخلاف المدبر وأم الولد فاختلت الإضافة ومعتق البعض كالمكاتب لما ذكرنا.
His statement: (Every slave of mine is free: his slaves who are qinn, his ummahat awlad, his mudabbar slaves are freed, but not his mukatab) Because the absolute attribution (to ownership) exists except for the mukatab, since ownership is established over them (qinn, umm walad, mudabbar) in both ownership of the slave (raqaba) and possession (yad). The mukatab is not included except with intention because ownership is not established over him in possession; for this reason he does not own his earnings, and it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with a mukataba, unlike the mudabbar and umm walad; thus the attribution is incomplete. And a partially freed slave is like the mukatab for the reason we mentioned.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage explains the scope of a statement 'Every slave of mine is free.' It is a general pronouncement that includes all fully owned slaves: qinn (unconditional slaves), ummahat awlad (mothers of children), and mudabbar (slaves promised freedom after master's death). However, it does not include mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) because ownership over a mukatab is not complete; the master does not have full possession (yad) over him, as the mukatab has certain rights. Similarly, a slave who is partially freed (e.g., half-free) is like a mukatab. The mukatab can only be included if the master explicitly intends him. The basis is the nature of ownership: for the included categories, the master owns both the slave's body (raqaba) and their services (yad); for the mukatab, the master's ownership is restricted.

Key Terms
قن (qinn): A fully owned slave who has not been promised freedom or contract of manumission.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract with his master to earn and pay for his freedom in installments.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose master has declared he will be free after the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master declares, 'Every slave of mine is free.' Which of the following categories is NOT automatically freed?
A) Qinn
B) Umm walad
C) Mudabbar
D) Mukatab

Key Takeaway
A general manumission statement in Hanafi fiqh frees only slaves fully owned in both body and possession; mukatab and part-freed slaves require explicit intent.

Three Categories of Actions That Break an Oath by Ordering
Explanation of three categories of acts that break an oath when ordered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify acts that break an oath when ordered into three categories and explain why the act must actually be performed by the agent.

Core Idea
An oath not to do an act is broken by ordering another only when the act is performed and its rights return to the orderer.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq introduces a classification system for acts that break an oath whether done directly or by ordering. The author lists numerous acts and then categorizes them into three types based on the return of rights. A crucial point made is that mere command is insufficient; the commanded act must actually occur. This lesson unpacks the three categories and the underlying principle of attribution.

Guided Path
1
List the acts mentioned
Identify all acts listed in the matn that break an oath when done by direct action or ordering. These include marriage, divorce, khul', manumission, etc.
2
Identify the three categories
Group the acts into the three categories: (1) those whose rights return to the commander, (2) those for which he has no rights, (3) sensory acts. Understand the basis for each category.
3
Analyse the condition of actual performance
Note the commentator's critique: mere command does not break the oath; the agent must actually perform the act. This is a key condition that must be satisfied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وما يحنث بهما النكاح والطلاق والخلع والعتق والكتابة والصلح عن دم ا العمد والهبة والصدقة والقرض والاستقراض وضرب العبد والذبح والبناء والخياطة والإيداع والاستيداع والإعارة والاستعارة وقضاء الدين وقبضه والكسوة والحمل بيان لثلاثة أنواع : الأول ما ترجع حقوقه إلى الآمر . الثاني ما لا حقوق له أصلاً . الثالث ما كان من الافعال الحسية. والضمير في قوله «بهما» عائد إلى المباشرة والأمر وفيه تسامح لأنه لا يحنث بمجرد الأمر بل لا بد من فعل الوكيل حتى لو حلف لا يتزوج فوكل به لا يحنث حتى يزوجه الوكيل فلو قال وما يحنث بفعله وفعل ماموره لكان أولى . وفسر الشارح الزيلعي الأمر بالتوكيل وليس مقتصراً عليه بل هو أعم من التوكيل والرسالة لأنه يحنث بالرسالة والدليل على عدم اقتصاره على التوكيل أن من هذا النوع الاستعراض والتوكيل به غير صحيح، وإنما حنث في هذا النوع بفعل المأمور لما أن غرض الحالف التوقي عن حكم العقد وحقوقه وهذه العقود تنتقل إليه بحقوقها فصار كمباشرته في حق الأحكام وصار الوكيل سفيراً ومعبراً ولهذا لا يستغنى عن إضافتها إلى الآمر . وما كان من الأفعال حسياً كضرب الغلام والذبح ونحوهما منقول أيضاً إلى الآمر حتى لا يجب الضمان على الفاعل فكان منسوباً إليه فيحنث . وقد فرق المصنف بين ضرب الولد وضرب العبد ، فلو خلف لا يضرب ولده فضربه غيره بأمره لا يحنث، ولو حلف لا يضرب عبده فضربه غيره بأمره حنث بناء على أن منفعة ضرب الولد عائدة إلى الولد المضروب وهي التأدب والتثقيف أي التقويم وترك الاعوجاج في الدين والمروءة والأخلاق فلم ينسب فعل المأمور إلى الآمر وإن كان يرجع إلى الأب أيضاً لكن أصل المنافع وحقيقتها إنما ترجع إلى المتصف بها فلا موجب للنقل بخلاف ضرب العبد فإن منفعته راجعة إلى الأمر على الخصوص وهو ما يحصل من أدبه وانزجاره وإن كان نفعه يرجع إلى العبد لكنه غير مقصود. فالحاصل أن المقصود من ضرب الولد حاصل له وإن حصل للأب ضمناً، والمقصود من ضرب العبد حاصل للمولى وإن حصل للعبد ضمناً فافترقا . وفي فتح القدير : وما في عرفنا وهو عن أبي يوسف ومحمد اهـ. قوله : حلف إن اشتراها يحنث (بالاقالة عزاه في النهر إلى عقد الفرائد مخالف لما تقدم عن الظهيرية والظاهر أنه قول آخر
His statement: 'And what breaks the oath by both of them: marriage, divorce, khul', manumission, kitabah, reconciliation for premeditated murder, gift, charity, loan, borrowing, hitting a slave, slaughter, building, sewing, deposit, seeking deposit, lending, borrowing, repaying debt, receiving it, clothing, and carrying' is an explanation of three types: First, those whose rights return to the commander. Second, those for which he has no rights at all. Third, sensory acts. The pronoun in 'both of them' refers to direct action and command, and there is looseness because he does not break the oath by mere command; rather, the agent must act. So if he swears not to marry and appoints an agent, he does not break the oath until the agent marries him. So if he said 'what breaks the oath by his action and the action of his subordinate,' it would be better. The commentator al-Zayla'i interpreted 'command' as appointment (tawkil), but it is not limited to that; it is more general, including both tawkil and message (risalah), because he breaks the oath by a message. The evidence that it is not limited to tawkil is that among this type is [a case where] representation (isti'rad) and tawkil are not valid. He only breaks the oath in this type by the action of the commanded person because the swearer's purpose is to avoid the legal effect of the contract and its rights, and these contracts transfer to him with their rights, so it becomes as if he did it directly in terms of rulings, and the agent becomes a messenger and intermediary; hence it is necessary to attribute the act to the commander. As for sensory acts like hitting a slave, slaughter, and the like, these are also attributed to the commander so that the doer is not liable for damages; thus it is attributed to him and he breaks the oath. The author distinguished between hitting a child and hitting a slave: if he swears not to hit his child and another hits him by his command, he does not break the oath; if he swears not to hit his slave and another hits him by his command, he breaks the oath. This is based on the fact that the benefit of hitting the child returns to the child himself, namely discipline and education, i.e., straightening and leaving crookedness in religion, chivalry, and morals. So the action of the commanded person is not attributed to the commander, even though it also benefits the father, but the origin and essence of the benefits return to the one characterized by them, so there is no reason for transfer. In contrast, the benefit of hitting the slave returns specifically to the commander, namely the discipline and deterrence he obtains, even though the benefit also returns to the slave but it is not intended. The conclusion: the purpose of hitting the child is achieved for the child, even if it incidentally benefits the father, while the purpose of hitting the slave is achieved for the master, even if it incidentally benefits the slave. So they differ. In Fath al-Qadir: 'And what is in our custom...' and it is from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. His statement: 'If he swears to buy her, he breaks his oath by cancellation' he attributed in al-Nahr to 'Uqud al-Fara'id, contrary to what preceded from al-Zahiriyyah, and apparently it is another opinion.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the matn (likely Kanz al-Daqa'iq) that lists actions which break an oath whether done directly or by ordering someone else. The commentator explains that these actions fall into three categories. First, actions where the rights and benefits return to the commander (e.g., marriage, divorce, manumission, sale of slave). Second, actions where the commander has no personal rights but the act is attributed to him due to his authority (e.g., kitabah, reconciliation for murder, gift, etc.). Third, sensory acts (e.g., hitting a slave, slaughter, building) which are physically done by another but legally attributed to the commander because he ordered them. The commentator clarifies that mere command does not break the oath; the commanded person must actually perform the act. He then critiques the author (probably al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah) for not specifying that the act must be performed by the agent.

A key distinction is made between hitting a child and hitting a slave. If a father swears not to hit his child and orders someone else to do it, he does not break the oath because the primary benefit of discipline is for the child. The order is not attributed to the father. However, if a master swears not to hit his slave and orders someone else, he breaks the oath because the benefit of discipline is for the master. This distinction is based on the purpose of the hitting. The passage ends with a note from Fath al-Qadir mentioning that this is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. It also notes a difference of opinion regarding breaking an oath by cancellation (iqala).

Key Terms
المباشرة (al-mubasharah): Directly performing an act oneself.
الأمر (al-amr): Command or ordering someone else to perform an act; in this context, it includes both agency (tawkil) and mere message (risalah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for an oath to be broken by ordering another?
A) The order must be in writing.
B) The ordered person must be an adult.
C) The ordered person must actually perform the act.
D) The swearer must intend to break the oath.

Key Takeaway
An oath broken by ordering another requires actual performance of the act by the agent, and acts fall into three categories based on return of rights.
