The Child-Slave Distinction in Oath Cases
Distinction between hitting a child and hitting a slave in oath cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hitting a child and hitting a slave in the context of oaths, based on the intended beneficiary of the act.

Core Idea
Ordering another to hit your slave breaks your oath to not hit, but ordering to hit your child does not, because the primary benefit of hitting a child returns to the child.

This passage introduces a key distinction in Hanafi oath law: the same act of hitting leads to different outcomes depending on whether the one hit is a child or a slave. The determining factor is the purpose of the hitting. If the benefit (discipline) is primarily for the one hit, the act is not attributed to the orderer; if the benefit is for the orderer, it is attributed. This lesson unpacks the distinction and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the two scenarios
First scenario: a father swears not to hit his child, but orders another to hit the child. Second scenario: a master swears not to hit his slave, but orders another to hit the slave.
2
Identify the ruling for each
In the child case, the father does not break his oath. In the slave case, the master breaks his oath.
3
Analyse the reasoning
The benefit of hitting the child (discipline) returns to the child, so the act is not attributed to the father. The benefit of hitting the slave (deterrence) returns to the master, so it is attributed.
4
Note the source of this distinction
The passage mentions that this view is reported from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in Fath al-Qadir, indicating it is their position within the Hanafi school.

8 min
Attribution of Actions in Oaths: The Child vs. Slave Distinction
Commentary on the three types of actions that break an oath when done by an agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of benefit (manfa'ah) to determine whether an action ordered by a swearer breaks his oath, and distinguish between beating a child and a slave.

Core Idea
An oath breaks only if the action's primary benefit returns to the swearer.

Oaths are common in daily life, and sometimes a person may order another to perform an action. Does that break the oath? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer: it depends on who primarily benefits from the action. This lesson unpacks the classification of actions into three types and applies the principle of benefit attribution, especially the subtle distinction between beating a child and beating a slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Types of Actions
From the text, list the three categories: (1) legal contracts whose rights return to the commander, (2) actions with no rights, (3) physical actions like beating. Understand that the classification determines attribution.
Legal contracts include marriage, divorce, sale, etc. Physical actions include beating, slaughter, building.
2
Understand the Attribution Principle
For legal contracts, the rights and effects transfer to the commander, so the agent's action is attributed to him and breaks the oath. For physical actions, attribution depends on who receives the primary benefit (manfa'ah).
The text emphasizes that if the primary benefit goes to the commander, the action is attributed to him; if it goes to the person acted upon, it is not.
3
Apply the Principle to the Child vs. Slave Distinction
Analyze the two scenarios: beating a child vs. beating a slave. The child's benefit (discipline, moral correction) primarily accrues to the child, even though the father also benefits indirectly. The slave's benefit (deterrence, training) primarily accrues to the master.
The author states: 'the origin and reality of the benefits return only to the one who possesses them'.
4
State the Ruling
If a man swears not to beat his child and orders someone else to do it, he does NOT break his oath. If he swears not to beat his slave and orders someone else, he DOES break his oath.
This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school.
5
Identify the 'Illa (Effective Cause)
The 'illa for attribution is the return of primary benefit to the commander. This is derived from qiyas (analogical reasoning) and is consistent with the principle that 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'—matters are judged by their purposes.
The dalil is not a direct text but a rational extension (qiyas) based on maslahah (benefit).

كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في البيع والشراء والتزويج والصوم والصلاة ۵۸۳ والكتابة والصلح عن دم عمد والهبة والصدقة والقرض والاستقراض وضرب العبد والذبح والبناء والخياطة والايداع والاستيداع والإعارة وفعل ماموره لكان أولى . وفسر الشارح الزيلعي الأمر بالتوكيل وليس مقتصراً عليه بل هو أعم من التوكيل والرسالة لأنه يحنث بالرسالة والدليل على عدم اقتصاره على التوكيل أن من هذا النوع الاستعراض والتوكيل به غير صحيح، وإنما حنث في هذا النوع بفعل المأمور لما أن غرض الحالف التوقي عن حكم العقد وحقوقه وهذه العقود تنتقل إليه بحقوقها فصار كمباشرته في حق الأحكام وصار الوكيل سفيراً ومعبراً ولهذا لا يستغنى عن إضافتها إلى الآمر . وما كان من الأفعال حسياً كضرب الغلام والذبح ونحوهما منقول أيضاً إلى الآمر حتى لا يجب الضمان على الفاعل فكان منسوباً إليه فيحنث . وقد فرق المصنف بين ضرب الولد وضرب العبد ، فلو خلف لا يضرب ولده فضربه غيره بأمره لا يحنث، ولو حلف لا يضرب عبده فضربه غيره بأمره حنث بناء على أن منفعة ضرب الولد عائدة إلى الولد المضروب وهي التأدب والتثقيف أي التقويم وترك الاعوجاج في الدين والمروءة والأخلاق فلم ينسب فعل المأمور إلى الآمر وإن كان يرجع إلى الأب أيضاً لكن أصل المنافع وحقيقتها إنما ترجع إلى المتصف بها فلا موجب للنقل بخلاف ضرب العبد فإن منفعته راجعة إلى الأمر على الخصوص وهو ما يحصل من أدبه وانزجاره وإن كان نفعه يرجع إلى العبد لكنه غير مقصود. فالحاصل أن المقصود من ضرب الولد حاصل له وإن حصل للأب ضمناً، والمقصود من ضرب العبد حاصل للمولى وإن حصل للعبد ضمناً فافترقا .
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oath Regarding Sale, Purchase, Marriage, Fasting, and Prayer. And [if he takes] wages and spends it or gives it to her husband, he does not break his oath. And leaving it in the hands of the residents is not ijarah. If he said to the residents: 'Stay in these houses,' it becomes ijarah and he breaks his oath. Similarly, if he demands from them rent for a month they did not reside in, unlike if he paid them rent for a month they had already resided in, that is not ijarah. End. His statement: 'What breaks the oath through them: marriage, divorce, khul', emancipation, kitabah, compromise for intentional blood, gift, charity, loan, borrowing, beating a slave, slaughter, building, sewing, deposit, request for deposit, lending, borrowing, repayment of debt, receipt of debt, clothing, carrying' is an explanation of three types: First: what rights return to the commander. Second: what has no rights at all. Third: what is physical actions. The pronoun 'both' in his statement refers to direct action and command, but there is lenience because he does not break the oath by mere command; rather, the agent's action is required. So if he swore not to marry and appointed an agent, he does not break until the agent marries him. If he had said 'what breaks by his action and the action of his subordinate' it would have been better. Al-Zayla'i interpreted 'command' as delegation, but it is not limited to that; it is more general, including delegation and message, because he breaks by a messenger. Evidence that it is not limited to delegation is that among this type is 'command to borrow' and delegation for that is invalid. He breaks in this type by the action of the commanded one because the swearer's purpose is to avoid the legal effect and rights of the contract. These contracts transfer to him with their rights, so he becomes like the direct performer in terms of rulings, and the agent becomes a envoy and conduit; therefore, he cannot dispense with attributing them to the commander. As for physical actions like beating a boy, slaughter, and similar, they are also attributed to the commander, so that liability does not fall on the doer. They are attributed to him, so he breaks. And the author distinguished between beating a child and beating a slave. If he swore not to beat his child and someone else beat him by his order, he does not break. If he swore not to beat his slave and someone else beat him by his order, he breaks, based on the fact that the benefit of beating the child accrues to the child himself, which is discipline and training, i.e., correction and removal of crookedness in religion, manliness, and character. So the action of the commanded one is not attributed to the commander, even though it also relates to the father, but the origin and reality of the benefits return only to the one who possesses them, so there is no reason for transfer. Unlike beating a slave, because its benefit accrues to the commander specifically, which is discipline and deterrence, even though its benefit also relates to the slave, but it is not intended. The bottom line is that the purpose of beating the child is achieved for him, even if it is indirectly for the father; the purpose of beating the slave is achieved for the master, even if indirectly for the slave. So they differ.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq expanding on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding oaths and the actions of an agent. The author first lists several actions that break an oath when performed by the swearer's agent, then classifies them into three categories: (1) Legal contracts whose rights and effects return to the commander (the swearer), such as marriage, divorce, sale; (2) Actions that have no rights accruing to the commander, like borrowing or deposit; (3) Physical actions like beating or slaughter. The key principle is that for the oath to be broken, the action must be attributable to the commander. Legal contracts are attributed because their effects (e.g., marital rights, ownership) apply to him. Physical actions are also attributed because the doer is not liable; the commander bears responsibility. The author then highlights a distinction between beating a child and beating a slave: if the swearer orders someone to beat his child, he does not break his oath because the primary benefit of discipline goes to the child himself, even though the father also benefits indirectly. In contrast, beating a slave benefits the master primarily, so ordering it breaks the oath. This distinction is based on whose benefit is the primary purpose.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah or a divorce/manumission conditional on an action.
المأمور (al-ma'mur): The person commanded by the swearer to perform an action.
المنفعة (al-manfa'ah): The benefit or utility derived from an action, used to determine attribution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I will not beat my child.' He then orders his friend to beat the child, and the friend does so. According to the Hanafi position, does the swearer break his oath?
A) Yes, because he ordered the beating.
B) No, because the primary benefit of disciplining the child goes to the child.
C) Yes, because the father is liable for the child's discipline.
D) No, because the friend is the direct beater.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath is broken by an agent's action only if the primary benefit of that action returns to the swearer, as illustrated by the distinction between beating a child (no broken oath) and beating a slave (broken oath).

Custom and Rulings for Beating Wife, Small Child, and Big Child
Discussion of custom, small vs. big child, and wife for breaking oath by agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the legal rulings for beating a small child, a big child, and a wife through an agent, and apply the preferred Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Father's authority differs: small child like slave, big child like stranger; wife's benefit accrues to her, so oath not broken.

This passage explores how custom ('urf) interacts with legal rulings on oaths when beating is carried out by an agent. The key distinction is whether the person beaten is a small child, a big child, or a wife, each with different rules. The author reconciles a reported custom with the legal distinction by applying it to older children only.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The core issue is whether a person who swears not to beat someone breaks his oath when he orders another person to do the beating.
2
Categorize the person being beaten
The ruling depends on the category: small child, big child, or wife. Each has different rules based on the swearer's authority (wilayah) and the beneficiary of the beating.
3
Apply the rule for small child
The father has authority to beat his small child, so ordering another to do so breaks the oath, similar to a slave.
Based on Fatawa Qadikhan
4
Apply the rule for big child
The father has no authority to beat an adult child; only a sultan or judge may order such beating. Thus, ordering does not break the oath unless directly beaten.
Custom reconciled by applying to big child
5
Analyze the wife case and ikhtilaf
Two opinions exist: wife like slave (breaks oath) or wife like child (does not break). The preferred opinion is that she does not break because primary benefit returns to her.
Tarjih by author

وفي فتح القدير : وما في عرفنا وهو عن أبي يوسف ومحمد اهـ. قوله : حلف إن اشتراها يحنث (بالاقالة عزاه في النهر إلى عقد الفرائد مخالف لما تقدم عن الظهيرية والظاهر أنه قول آخر قوله : (وكذا إذا تقاضى منهم أجرة شهر لم يسكنوا فيه قال في النهر : وأنت خبير بأن تقاضى أجرة شهر لم يسكنوا فيه ليس إلا الإجارة بالتعاطي فينبغي أن يجري فيه الخلاف السابق قوله : (وليس مقتصراً عليه الخ) قال في النهر : لك أن تقول إنما خصه لتعلم الرسالة منه بالأولى .  ٥٨٤ كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في البيع والشراء والتزويج والصوم والصلاة وعرف عامتنا فإنه يقال ضرب فلان اليوم ولده وإن لم يباشر ، ويقول العامي لولده غداً أسقيك علقة ثم يذكر المؤدب الولدان يضربه فيعد الأب نفسه أنه قد حقق إيعاده ذلك ولم يكذب فمقتضاه أن تنعقد على معنى لا يقع به ضرب من جهتي ويحنث بفعل المأمور .اهـ. وينبغي أن يكون مرادهم بالولد الولد الكبير لأنه لا يملك ضربه فهو كما لو حلف لا يضرب حراً أجنبياً فإنه لا يحنث إلا بالمباشرة لأنه لا ولاية له عليه فلا يعتبر أمره إلا أن يكون الحالف سلطاناً أو قاضياً لأنهما يملكان ضرب الأحرار حداً وتعزيراً فملكا الأمر به. وأما الولد الصغير فكالعبد لما في فتاوى قاضيخان : ولو حلف لا يضرب ولده الصغير فأمر غيره فضربه ينبغي أن يحنث الحالف لأن الأب يملك ضرب ولده الصغير فيملك التفويض إلى غيره ويكون بمنزلة القاضي والسلطان اهـ . وإنما لم يجزم به في الفتاوى لأن الولد أعم من الصغير والكبير ولم يخصص بالكبير في الروايات . وفي الذخيرة ولو حلف على امرأته لا يضربها فأمر غيره حتى ضربها فقد قيل إنها نظير العبد فيحنث في يمينه، وقيل إنها نظير الولد فلا يحنث الحالف في يمينه اهـ. ولم يرجح وينبغي ترجيح الثاني لأن معظم المنفعة تعود لها وإن حصلت للزوج ضمناً.
And in Fath al-Qadir: 'What is in our custom' - and it is from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. End. His statement: 'If he swore to buy it, he breaks [by] the iqalah' - al-Nahr attributed it to 'Uqud al-Fara'id, contradicting what came from al-Zahiriyyah; apparently it is another opinion. His statement: 'Similarly, if he demands from them rent for a month they did not reside in' - he said in al-Nahr: 'You are aware that demanding rent for a month they did not reside in is only ijarah by mutual giving, so the previous disagreement should apply to it.' His statement: 'And he is not limited to it, etc.' He said in al-Nahr: 'You could say that he specified it to make the messenger known from it by priority.' [PAGE 584] And the custom of our common people: it is said 'so-and-so beat his child today' even if he did not directly do it. The common man says to his child 'tomorrow I will give you a beating' then he mentions the teacher [who] beats the children, so the father considers himself that he has fulfilled his promise and did not lie. So the implication is that the oath is made on the meaning that no beating occurs from my side, and he breaks by the action of the commanded one. End. It should be that by 'child' they mean the older child, because he does not have authority to beat him; it is like if he swore not to beat a free stranger, he only breaks by direct action because he has no guardianship over him, so his command is not considered unless the swearer is a sultan or judge, because they have authority to beat free men for hadd and tazir, so they have authority to command it. As for the small child, he is like a slave, as in Fatawa Qadikhan: 'If he swore not to beat his small child and ordered someone else to beat him, it is appropriate that the swearer breaks, because the father has authority to beat his small child, so he has authority to delegate to others, and he is like the judge and sultan.' End. He did not definitively state it in the Fatawa because 'child' includes both small and big, and it was not specified as big in the narrations. And in al-Dhakhirah: 'If he swore concerning his wife not to beat her and ordered someone else until he beat her, it is said she is like a slave, so he breaks his oath; it is said she is like a child, so he does not break.' End. He did not give preponderance, and it is appropriate to prefer the second because most of the benefit accrues to her, even if it is obtained for the husband indirectly.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on breaking oaths through an agent, focusing on the role of custom ('urf) and the specific cases of children and wife. The author first quotes Fath al-Qadir about the custom of the common people where a father is said to have beaten his child even if he only ordered the beating. This custom suggests that the father's oath is broken when the agent beats the child, contrary to the earlier ruling that distinguished the child from the slave. However, the author resolves this by clarifying that the custom applies to the older child (al-walad al-kabir), because the father does not have the legal authority (wilayah) to beat an adult child; such beating is only permissible for a sultan or judge. Therefore, for an older child, the oath is not broken by ordering, just as with a free stranger. For a small child, the father has authority to beat, so ordering a beating breaks the oath, similar to a slave. Finally, the author discusses the case of a wife: two opinions are reported – one considers her like a slave (breaking the oath), another like a child (not breaking). The author prefers the second because the primary benefit of beating the wife (discipline) accrues to her, even though the husband also benefits indirectly. The authority weight includes nawadir (rare transmissions) and mutakhkhirin opinions.

Key Terms
الولد الصغير (al-walad al-saghir): A minor child whom the father has the right to discipline physically.
الولد الكبير (al-walad al-kabir): An adult child whom the father may not beat; only sultan/judge may order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He breaks his oath because the wife is like a slave.
B) He does not break his oath because the primary benefit of discipline returns to the wife.
C) He breaks his oath because ordering is equivalent to direct action.
D) He does not break his oath because the brother acted independently.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the ruling for breaking an oath by ordering a beating depends on whether the person beaten is a small child (breaking), a big child (not breaking), or a wife (preferred: not breaking).

Intention in Oaths: Legal vs. Physical Actions
Legal vs. physical actions in oath fulfillment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between legal and physical actions in Hanafi oath jurisprudence and apply the rule regarding acceptance of intention in court vs. conscience.

Core Idea
In oaths, intention limits general language but is accepted only for physical actions, not legal ones.

When a person swears an oath and then the action is performed by an agent, the Hanafi school examines the nature of the action. If the action is legal (like divorce or marriage), the swearer's intention to do it directly is not accepted in court; but if it is physical (like beating), it is accepted both religiously and legally. This lesson explains the reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that the passage divides actions into legal (hukmiyat) and physical (hissiyat). Legal actions are accomplished by speech (e.g., divorce, marriage); physical actions by bodily movement (e.g., beating, slaughter).
2
State the ruling for each category
For legal actions, intention is accepted religiously (diyanatan) but not judicially (qada'an). For physical actions, it is accepted both religiously and judicially.
3
Trace the reasoning (talil)
Legal actions like divorce are achieved by speech; commanding someone to speak is like speaking oneself. The wording covers both, so specifying direct action is against the apparent meaning, hence not accepted in court. Physical actions require direct impact; commanding is metaphorical, so intending direct action is the true meaning.
This is a qiyas-based reasoning distinguishing speech from tangible effect.
4
Apply to additional cases
The passage extends the rule to cases of marrying off (tazwij) vs. marrying (tazawwuj), ratification (ijazah), and force. For example, swearing not to marry off and ordering an agent does not break the oath, but swearing not to marry and ordering an agent does.
These applications illustrate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو نوى المباشرة بنفسه فقط في هذه النوع قالوا فما كان من الحكميات كالتزوج والطلاق فإنه يصدق ديانة لا قضاء، وما كان من الحسيات كالضرب والذبح فإنه يصدق ديانة وقضاء. والفرق أن الطلاق ليس إلا تكلماً بكلام يفضي إلى الوقوع والأمر بذلك مثل التكلم به واللفظ ينتظمهما، فإذا نوى أن لا يليه فقد نوى الخصوص في العام فلا يصدق قضاء لأنه خلاف الظاهر. وما كان حسياً فإنه يعرف بأثره المحسوس في المحل وإنما يحصل بالفعل فكان فيه حقيقة والنسبة إلى الأمر بالسبب مجاز ، فإذا نوى الفعل بنفسه فقد نوى حقيقة كلامه . وقيد بالنكاح لأنه لو قال والله لا أزوج فلانة فأمر رجلاً فزوجها لا يحنث بخلاف التزوج. قال محمد بن الوليد : سألت نجم الدين عن الفرق فقال : التزويج بأمره لا يلحقه حكمه والتزوج بأمره يثبت حكمه لو وهو الحل؛ كذا في الفيض معزياً إلى مجموع النوازل وفي البدائع : حلف لا يزوج بنته الصغيرة فتزوجها رجل بغير أمره فأجاز حنث لأن حقوقه تتعلق بالمجيز، ولو حلف لا يزوج ابناً له كبيراً فأمر رجلاً فزوجه ثم بلغ الابن فأجاز أو زوجه رجل وأجاز الأب ورضي الابن لم يحنث، وسيأتي تمامه في قوله لو حلف لا يتزوج فأجاز بالقول حنث وبالفعل لا». وفي الظهيرية : رجل قال لامرأة لا يحل له نكاحها إن تزوجتك فعبدي حر فتزوجها حنث لأن يمينه تنصرف إلى ما يتصور عبد حلف أن لا يتزوج فزوجه مولاه وهو كاره لذلك لم يحنث لأن لفظ النكاح وجد من المولى. ولو حلف رجل أن لا يتزوج امرأة فاكره على النكاح فتزوج .
And if he intended direct action by himself only in this type, they said: as for legal actions like marriage and divorce, he is believed religiously but not judicially. As for physical actions like beating and slaughter, he is believed both religiously and judicially. The difference is that divorce is nothing but speech that leads to occurrence, and commanding it is like speaking it, and the wording encompasses both. So if he intended not to do it himself, he intended a specific within a general, so he is not believed judicially because it contradicts the apparent. As for physical actions, they are known by their tangible effect on the object and are only achieved by action, so there is a reality, and attribution to command is metaphorical. So if he intended the action himself, he intended the reality of his words. And it is restricted by marriage because if he said 'By Allah, I will not marry off so-and-so' and ordered a man and he married her, he does not break, unlike 'I will not marry'. Muhammad ibn al-Walid said: I asked Najm al-Din about the difference, and he said: 'Marrying off by his order does not attach his ruling; marrying by his order establishes his ruling, which is permissibility.' As in al-Fayd cited from Majmu' al-Nawazil. And in al-Bada'i': He swore not to marry off his minor daughter, and a man married her without his order, then he ratified, he breaks because his rights are attached to the ratifier. If he swore not to marry off his adult son, and ordered a man to marry him, then the son became adult and ratified, or a man married him and the father ratified and the son consented, he does not break. Its completion will come in his statement: 'If he swore not to marry, and he ratified by speech he breaks, by action he does not.' And in al-Zahiriyyah: A man said to a woman not permissible for him to marry: 'If I marry you, my slave is free,' then he married her, he breaks because his oath is directed to what is conceivable. A slave swore not to marry, and his master married him while he disliked it, he does not break because the wording of marriage came from the master. And if a man swore not to marry a woman, and he was forced into the marriage and married...
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of intention (niyyah) in oaths when the action is performed by an agent. The author distinguishes between legal actions (hukmiyat) like marriage and divorce, and physical actions (hissiyat) like beating. If the swearer intends that he will only perform the action himself (directly), and then the action is done by an agent, his intention is accepted religiously (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an) for legal actions. For physical actions, his intention is accepted both religiously and judicially. The reasoning is that legal actions like divorce are accomplished through speech, and commanding someone to speak the divorce is similar to speaking it oneself; the expression covers both. So if he intends not to do it directly, he is limiting the general, which is not accepted in court because it contradicts the apparent meaning. Physical actions, however, require direct physical impact; attributing them to command is metaphorical, so intending direct action is a true interpretation of his words, and he is believed. The passage also clarifies the difference between 'tazwij' (marrying off) and 'tazawwuj' (marrying oneself). If he swears not to marry off a woman, and he orders someone to do so, he does not break the oath because the effects of the marriage (like permissibility) attach to the woman, not to him. But if he swears not to marry (himself), ordering an agent to marry him does break the oath because the marriage's effects attach to him. The text also discusses cases involving ratification (ijazah) and force, and mentions that if a slave is forced by his master into marriage, the oath is not broken because the master performed the marriage contract.

Key Terms
الحكميات (al-hukmiyat): Legal actions accomplished by speech, like marriage, divorce, where ruling transfers via words.
الحسيات (al-hissiyat): Physical actions accomplished by direct bodily movement, like beating or slaughter.
ديانة (diyanatan): In terms of religious conscience and private obligation, not enforceable in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I will not divorce my wife.' He intends only to divorce her himself, but then he orders his agent to divorce her. According to the Hanafi ruling in the passage, which of the following is true?
A) He is believed both religiously and judicially.
B) He is believed religiously but not judicially.
C) He is believed judicially but not religiously.
D) He is not believed at all.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath jurisprudence, the nature of the action determines whether the swearer's subjective intention is accepted in court: for legal actions, the apparent wording prevails; for physical actions, intention can limit the scope.

intermediate
8 min
Oath on Hitting: Young Child, Older Child, and Wife
Clarifies when ordering another to hit counts as violating an oath not to hit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state whether ordering another to hit violates an oath not to hit, depending on the swearer's authority over the person.

Core Idea
Ordering another to strike violates the oath only if the swearer has authority to strike that person directly.

This lesson examines a common scenario: someone swears not to hit their child or wife, but then orders someone else to do the hitting. Does this count as breaking the oath? The answer depends on who has legal authority to discipline the person. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the nuances for older children, young children, and wives, and introduces the special case of sultans and judges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
The swearer swore not to strike, but ordered another to strike. We need to determine if this order counts as a violation.
2
Determine the nature of the person struck
Is the person an older child, young child, wife, or free stranger? The ruling depends on the swearer's relationship to them.
3
Assess the swearer's authority
Does the swearer have the right to strike that person directly? For example, a father has authority over a young child but not over an independent older child.
A sultan or judge has authority over free persons for punishment.
4
Apply the principle
If the swearer can legally delegate the striking (because he has authority), then ordering another counts as a violation. If not, he only violates by striking directly.
5
Note the ikhtilaf for the wife
There are two views: analogizing wife to a slave (order counts) or to a child (order does not count). The commentator prefers the second.
The tarjih is based on the benefit returning to the wife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في البيع والشراء والتزويج والصوم والصلاة وعرف عامتنا فإنه يقال ضرب فلان اليوم ولده وإن لم يباشر ، ويقول العامي لولده غداً أسقيك علقة ثم يذكر المؤدب الولدان يضربه فيعد الأب نفسه أنه قد حقق إيعاده ذلك ولم يكذب فمقتضاه أن تنعقد على معنى لا يقع به ضرب من جهتي ويحنث بفعل المأمور .اهـ. وينبغي أن يكون مرادهم بالولد الولد الكبير لأنه لا يملك ضربه فهو كما لو حلف لا يضرب حراً أجنبياً فإنه لا يحنث إلا بالمباشرة لأنه لا ولاية له عليه فلا يعتبر أمره إلا أن يكون الحالف سلطاناً أو قاضياً لأنهما يملكان ضرب الأحرار حداً وتعزيراً فملكا الأمر به. وأما الولد الصغير فكالعبد لما في فتاوى قاضيخان : ولو حلف لا يضرب ولده الصغير فأمر غيره فضربه ينبغي أن يحنث الحالف لأن الأب يملك ضرب ولده الصغير فيملك التفويض إلى غيره ويكون بمنزلة القاضي والسلطان اهـ . وإنما لم يجزم به في الفتاوى لأن الولد أعم من الصغير والكبير ولم يخصص بالكبير في الروايات . وفي الذخيرة ولو حلف على امرأته لا يضربها فأمر غيره حتى ضربها فقد قيل إنها نظير العبد فيحنث في يمينه، وقيل إنها نظير الولد فلا يحنث الحالف في يمينه اهـ. ولم يرجح وينبغي ترجيح الثاني لأن معظم المنفعة تعود لها وإن حصلت للزوج ضمناً. ولو نوى المباشرة بنفسه فقط في هذه النوع قالوا فما كان من الحكميات كالتزوج والطلاق فإنه يصدق ديانة لا قضاء، وما كان من الحسيات كالضرب والذبح فإنه يصدق ديانة وقضاء. والفرق أن الطلاق ليس إلا تكلماً بكلام يفضي إلى الوقوع والأمر بذلك مثل التكلم به واللفظ ينتظمهما، فإذا نوى أن لا يليه فقد نوى الخصوص في العام فلا يصدق قضاء لأنه خلاف الظاهر. وما كان حسياً فإنه يعرف بأثره المحسوس في المحل وإنما يحصل بالفعل فكان فيه حقيقة والنسبة إلى الأمر بالسبب مجاز ، فإذا نوى الفعل بنفسه فقد نوى حقيقة كلامه .
Book of Oaths, Chapter of Oath concerning Sale, Purchase, Marriage, Fasting and Prayer. And the understanding of our common people: it is said 'So-and-so struck his son today' even if he did not do it directly, and the common person says to his son 'Tomorrow I will give you a drink of milk' then the teacher mentions that the boys strike him, so the father counts himself as having fulfilled his promise and not lied; thus the implication is that the oath is construed on a meaning that no actual striking occurs from my side, and he violates by the act of the one commanded. End. It should be that by 'son' they mean the older son because [the father] does not have the right to strike him; so it is like if he swore not to strike a free stranger, then he only violates by direct action because he has no authority over him, so his command is not considered unless the swearer is a sultan or judge because they have the right to strike free persons as a prescribed punishment or discretionary punishment, so they have authority over the command. As for the young child, he is like a slave according to what is in Fatawa Qadikhan: If he swore not to strike his young child and commanded another and he struck him, it is proper that the swearer violates his oath because the father has the right to strike his young child, so he has the right to delegate to another and he is like the judge and sultan. End. The reason it was not stated definitively in the Fatawa is that 'child' is general including young and old, and it was not specified as old in the narrations. In al-Dhakhira: If he swore about his wife not to strike her and commanded another until he struck her, it was said that she is like a slave so he violates his oath, and it was said that she is like a child so the swearer does not violate his oath. End. There is no preference, and the second should be preferred because most benefit returns to her even if it accrues to the husband indirectly. And if he intended direct action by himself only in this type, they said: whatever is of the hukmi [cognitive] type like marriage and divorce, he is believed religiously but not judicially; and whatever is of the hissi [physical] type like striking and slaughtering, he is believed both religiously and judicially. The difference is that divorce is nothing but a speech by words that lead to occurrence, and commanding it is like saying it, and the wording comprehends both; so if he intended not to perform it himself, he intended a specific within a general, so he is not believed judicially because it contradicts the apparent. As for the physical, it is known by its tangible effect on the subject and is only achieved by an act, so in it there is reality, and attribution to command is metaphorical; so if he intended the act himself, he intended the reality of his speech.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of rulings on oaths concerning hitting. It opens by explaining the common usage: people say 'he struck his son' even if he ordered someone else to do it, so when someone swears not to strike his son, the oath is interpreted according to the common understanding. The author (al-Marghinani) then clarifies that the term 'son' here likely refers to an older son who is independent, because the father has no right to hit him, so if he orders another, it is not a violation, just as with a free stranger. However, if the swearer is a sultan or judge who has authority to strike free persons as punishment, then ordering does count. For a young child, the father has authority, so ordering another is like a violation, as in Fatawa Qadikhan. The madhhab distinction: for a wife, there are two views: some analogize her to a slave (so ordering counts) and some to a child (so not). The text prefers the second because the benefit of discipline returns to her. Then comes an important principle about intention: for cognitive acts (hukmi) like talaq and tazwīj, if one intends only direct action, he is believed religiously (diyānah) but not in court (qaḍāʾ) because the wording 'I divorce' can also be fulfilled by commanding. For physical acts (hissī) like striking, the words are metaphorical for commanding, so if he intends direct action, he is believed in both contexts because he intended the literal meaning. This distinction is crucial for judges when evaluating oath cases.

Key Terms
ولد (walad): Child, distinguished between young (صغير) and old (كبير) with different rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A father swears not to hit his 10-year-old son, then asks his wife to spank the son. According to the preferred Hanafi view, does he violate his oath?
A) Yes, because the father has authority over a young child.
B) No, because the father did not directly strike.
C) Yes, because the wife has authority.
D) No, because the son is older.

Key Takeaway
Whether ordering another to hit violates an oath depends on the swearer's legal authority to strike that person directly.
